Phase I Variables (polity-based)[edit]

General variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Edward A L Turner; Jenny Reddish; Dan Hoyer ♥ These codes were developed at Seshat General Workshop in Oxford 2017

♠ Expert ♣ ♥


♠ UTM zone ♣ 39 S ♥

♠ Original name ♣ Achaemenid Empire ♥

♠ Alternative names ♣ Persia; Persian Empire ♥ "Iran (from Airyanam, genitive plural adjective of Airya-), 'the land of the Aryans' ... western Iran became Media and souther Iran Parsa/Persia."[1] "The Archaemenids did not - could not - provide a name for their multinational state. Nevertheless, they referred to it as Khshassa, 'the Empire.'"[2]


♠ Peak Date ♣ 500 BCE ♥ [3]


Temporal bounds[edit]

♠ Duration ♣ 550-331 BCE ♥ [4]

Cyrus II was "heir to both the Median and Persian thrones. In 550 BCE, Cyrus rose up against his despotic grandfather and overthrew him. From a contemporary document known as the Nabunaid Chronicle and the testimony of Herodotus we learn that, dissatisfied with Astyages, the Media aristocracy, led by a military commander named Harpagus, joined Cyrus and accepted him as the legitimate heir to the throne. This established the Achaemenid - of the first Persian - Empire, in which the Medes shared the status of ruling people with the Persians, so much so indeed that the Greeks frequently called the Persians 'Medes' and coined the term 'Medizing' to denote 'pro-Persian policy' or 'Persian partisan'."[5]


♠ Degree of centralization ♣ unitary state; confederate state ♥

Loose federation with irregular taxation under Cyrus and Cambyses with automomous satrapies. Relied on non-Persian officials and existing institutions.[6]

After Darius put down rebellions he created the 'world empire'[7] but under this system regions were still autonomous, the improvement was fixed system of tax collection based on surveys[8] and an inspection regime.

Centralization occurred at the satrap level such as with codification of laws, and coinage. Before Darius trade was in barter or Lydian gold coins. Satraps could coin money but only King of Kings could coin in gold.[9] Darius I developed a national script (Old Persian cuneiform)[10] but seems to have been ceremonial and used by Persians, in satraps local languages used.

"Prior to the Parthians, political systems in Southwest Asia were for the most part relatively loose confederations in which central government ruled their 'empires' through unstable alliances with vassals and satraps. Even Hammurabi, Darius, and Alexander were only temporarily successful in linking their centralized governments to local administrative institutions, particularly outside of the core areas of Greater Mesopotamia."[11]

♠ Supra-polity relations ♣ none ♥

Supra-cultural relations[edit]

♠ preceding (quasi)polity ♣ Median Empire ♥
♠ relationship to preceding (quasi)polity ♣ continuity ♥ Between 553 and 550 BCE, Cyrus the Great rebelled against Median control and re-established the independence of Persia. He took control of the area of Elam and Susa and eventually the whole of the Median empire. [12]
♠ succeeding (quasi)polity ♣ Macedonian Empire ♥
♠ Supracultural entity ♣ Persian ♥
♠ scale of supra-cultural interaction ♣ [2,500,000-3,000,000] ♥ km squared. Area that includes parts of modern day Turkey, Levant, Iraq, Iran, Caucasus, Transoxania and Afghanistan.

♠ Capital ♣ Pasargadae; Susa ♥ [Pasargadae: 550-521 BCE; Susa: 521-330 BCE]

Susa was the administrative capital. Persepolis was the ceremonial and religious center. Ecbatanna were commercial, strategic and provincial centers.[13]

Susa was the administrative capital, probably from Darius I. [14]

Pasargade was the capital under Cyrus the Great. Thereafter it was used as a ceremonial centre for the coronation of the Achaemenid king. [15] 135 km to northeast of Shiraz.[16]

Persepolis never played an important role in the governments of the empire. It was a ritual centre.[17][18]

Ecbatana and Babylon were strategic and commercial cities. [19]

The actual residence of the king varied between the seasons. Ecbatana was a summer resort, Persepolis was typical for the autumn season, and either at Susa or Babylon over the winter.[20]

Cyrus II moved the capital to Susa after he unified Elam in 559 BCE. [21]

Susa was the main capital. [22]

♠ Language ♣ Old Persian; Aramaic; Elamite; Greek; Egyptian ♥ 'The Persians of the Achaemenid period spoke an early form of Persian - "Old Persian" - a member of the Indo-European language family'.[23] Aramaic used for imperial documents and diplomatic correspondence.[24] Elamite, Babylonian, Aramaic, hieroglyphic Egyotian, Greek all used in royal and provincial chancelleries. Old Persian cuneiform script, from Darius, also used as official language and this was used for ceremonial inscriptions.[25] ♠ Linguistic family ♣ Indo-European; Afro-Asiatic; language isolate ♥ Old Persian is an Indo-European language.[26] Aramaic is in the Afro-Asiatic language family. Elamite is a language 'of unknown linguistic affiliation'[27] but may be related to the Dravidian languages of South Asia.[28]

Map[edit]

General Description[edit]

The Achaemenid Empire was established by Cyrus II 'the Great', who inherited the small kingdom of Persia (named after the capital city, Persis) in southwest Iran, a vassal territory of the larger Median Empire to the Northwest. From 553 to 550 BCE, Cyrus led his fellow Persians against Median hegemony (even though the Medes were ruled by his own relatives), establishing the Persians as the dominant group in Iran. His kingdom became known as the Achaemenid Empire after the legendary first King of Persia, Achaemenes, claimed to be an ancestor of the Great Cyrus himself (Achaemenid essentially translates to 'children of Achaemenes').[29]
Capitalizing on these early victories, Cyrus II the Great continued his military domination, conquering the wealthy Lydian Kingdom in modern-day Turkey along with most of Asia Minor and the Neo-Babylonian Kingdom in Mesopotamia, as well as consolidating Persia's hold over much of central Asia as far as modern Pakistan. His son and heir, Cambyses II, continued this tradition, expanding Achaemenid rule into the large and wealthy kingdom of Egypt. After Cambyses II's death in 522 BCE, a noble Persian named Darius came to power after overthrowing an alleged usurper to the throne (Gautama, supposedly posing as Cyrus II's son Bardiya, more commonly known by his Greek name Smerdis).[30] Darius I, who also took the title of 'the Great', was a powerful ruler who inaugurated several military, administrative, and economic reforms,[31] though is most well known for leading the Persian army to defeat at the hands of a coalition of small Greek city-states during the famous Persian Wars of the early 5th century BCE. Despite the fact that Darius' son and heir Xerxes I (the Great) also failed to conquer the Greek Aegean and lost a decisive battle to the same outnumbered coalition of Greeks, the Achaemenid Empire remained intact.[32]
In 330 BCE, Darius III became the twelfth and final emperor in the Achaemenid line when he succumbed to the conquests of Alexander the Great and his invading Macedonian army (twelfth not including the alleged usurper Bardiya/Smerdis nor the short-lived Artaxerxes V, who declared himself emperor for a brief moment after Darius III was killed as Alexander was completing his conquest).[33] Alexander became the ruler of all the territory formerly held by the Persians, incorporating it into the massive, though short-lived, Macedonian Empire and bringing an end to the great Persian Achaemenid Empire.

Population and political organization

The Achaemenid Empire was one of the largest empires in the pre-modern world, stretching nearly 6 million square kilometres across the Near East, Central Asia, the Indus Valley, Middle East, and into Egypt at its greatest extent.[34] It was a massive, multi-ethnic society made up of Medes, Persians, Lydians, Greeks, Egyptians, Babylonians, Bactrians, Sogdians, and numerous other cultural-ethnic groups; indeed, Old Persian, Elamite, Babylonian, Aramaic, hieroglyphic Egyptian, and Greek were all used in royal and provincial communication.[35] Between the Great rulers Cyrus II, Cambyses II, and Darius I, the Persians had stitched together an empire out of the centres of the oldest civilizations from Anatolia to Egypt, Mesopotamia and the Indus valley. Persepolis and the grand Pasargadae were large ceremonial and ritual centres in the heartland of Persia, while Susa in western Iran was the major administrative capital. At its peak under Darius I, the empire covered a huge swathe of diverse territory from the eastern Mediterranean all the way to the Indus Valley, incorporating navigable seas and rivers, protected ports and fertile agricultural land as well as rough mountainous passes. This territory held a population of between 17 and 35 million people.[36]

Hierarchical Complexity[edit]

♠ Settlement hierarchy ♣ [5-6] ♥

Susa was the administrative capital. Persepolis was the ceremonial and religious center. Ecbatanna were commercial, strategic and provincial centers.[48]

1. Capital City.

2. Satrap capital.
3. Provincial capital.
4. Town.
5. Village
6. Hamlet

♠ Administrative levels ♣ [6-8] ♥


1. King of Kings

Hereditary kings who ruled from a royal court within a feudal Iranian milieu. Darius I was a slight exception as he was an Achaemenid who was elected by members of the Persian elite.[49]
Dual kingship (father and son) possibly existed from Darius I to Artaxerxes II.
King of kings. "However, unlike previous Near Eastern dynasties, they did not claim divine descent or nature."[50]
King ruled law by decree but 'bound by tradition to respect the views of elders and consult great nobles on important occasions; he had to marry from the great noble's families; he could not pass arbitrary judgement; and there was alway sthe fear of assassination if he went too far in autocracy."[51]


_Central government line_

Designed according to a Babylonian template.[52] Presumably via Elamties? Persians 'learned a good deal of Elamite culture, especially in administration and arts."[53]

2. Head of administration[54] Steward of the House
"during Darius I's reign, Parnaka, the king's uncle, headed the administration, supported by a core of assistants ... and a battalion of lower administrators and scribes[55]
Steward of the House was viceroy of the empire.[56]
The very centre of the bureaucracy was the royal palace and an efficient chancellery.[57]
3. Core of Assistants[58]
4. Scribes
4. Lower administrators[59]
5. Scribes


_Provincial government_

2. Satrap (or king)
"As long as the subject nations obeyed the central authority and paid their taxes, they were free to follow their own laws and religious traditions, continue their artistic norms, retain their own languages, write in their own script, and maintain their own social system. In some cases, even local dynasties were left undisturbed and native kings retained their hereditary rights to kingship. Hence, the Persian king was called 'the Great King' or 'the King of Kings'."[60]
Twenty taxation districts called satrapies that had a civilian governor, assisted by military commander and treasurer. These individuals were "inspected by the most trusted envoys of the sovereign (called the 'King's ears and eyes'), who had full authority to reward meritorius deeds and punish unlawful ones."[61]
Governor of a satrapy. Had an indefinite period of term. All the satrapies - except the Persians who were governed by the king directly - were responsible to the king's command and had to pay him tribute.
Before Darius the empire had been split into satrapies by Cyrus II but government was mostly at a local level according to local traditions. Darius, who expanded the empire to its peak territorial extent, implemented a more centralized organization.
525-404 BCE "Egypt became a Persian satrapy, along with Libya, Cyrene and Barca (Herodotus 3.91), and was governed by a satrap in Memphis, who had to ensure the payment of tribute to the royal treasury. [62]
Satraps appointed by king. The 'Medizing' Greek Themistocles left Greece after Greek-Persian war c479 BCE to become a Persian satrap.[63]
3. Satrap's court
3. Provincial sub-satraps
Satrapies were themselves divided into provinces which paid a tribute tax to the satraps.[64] [65]
4. Local districts.
5. Village headmen.

"Prior to the Parthians, political systems in Southwest Asia were for the most part relatively loose confederations in which central government ruled their 'empires' through unstable alliances with vassals and satraps. Even Hammurabi, Darius, and Alexander were only temporarily successful in linking their centralized governments to local administrative institutions, particularly outside of the core areas of Greater Mesopotamia."[66]


♠ Religious levels ♣ [3-5] ♥

1. Achaemenid king

"However, unlike previous Near Eastern dynasties, they did not claim divine descent or nature."[67]
Achaemenid kings claimed a divine right to rule as the representative of the supreme god Ahura Mazdā on earth - a cultural link to the Mesopotamian peoples.[68]
When the empire expanded the Achaemenid kings assumed the pre-eminent position at the top of the religious hierarchy in conquered lands. In Egypt he became "son of the god Atum."[69]
2. Mobats (upper magi) does this term encompass multiple levels?
3. Herbats (lower magi) does this term encompass multiple levels?

"Alexander is cited by Zoroastrian tradition as having "killed the magi ... many teachers, lawyers, Herbats [the lower magi], Mobats [the upper magi]. Much of the literature of Persia, notably works of learning and Zoroastrian texts, simply perished during the Alexandrian conquests."[70]

"Darius supproted alien faiths and temples 'as long as those who held them are submissive and peaceable."[71]

Persepolis was a palatial city and the ritual centre of the empire.[72] However, priests of the temples performed the coronation ritual in Parsargadae, in which the new king had to wear the old clothes of Cyrus the Great.[73]

Numerous religions. State contributed to building of temples. [74] Cyrus II left native religious and political institutions intact. [75]

♠ Military levels ♣ 7 ♥

Decimal system. [76]

1. King

2. General
3. myriad (10 hazaraba)
4. hazaraba (1000 men)
5. sataba (100 men)
6. dathaba (10 men)
7. Individual soldier


Supreme Commander of the spāda. Baivarapati of the Corps. Hazārapati of the division. θatapati of the battalion. Daθapati of the company. [77]

Top position in the army was the commander of the royal guards, the hazārapati or chiliarch. Reputed to be second in power only to the king, this position may have functioned as a prime minister but little evidence supports this idea.[78]

In Egypt "Garrison commanders were usually Persian, but the garrison commander of the Hermopolite nome during the fifteenth year of Darius' reign was Egyptian."[79]

Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥

♠ Professional military officers ♣ present ♥ [80] [81]

According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) "The Assyrian army as well as the Persians always retained a large corps of loyal professionals as the centerpiece of their military establishments and ensured that loyal professionals remained in control of key logistics and supply functions of the various national units under imperial command." [82]

♠ Professional soldiers ♣ present ♥ [83] Persian army used mercenaries. For example, used Egyptian soldiers during Persian Wars, as mentioned by Herodotus. [84]

♠ Professional priesthood ♣ inferred present ♥ "Alexander is cited by Zoroastrian tradition as having "killed the magi ... many teachers, lawyers, Herbats [the lower magi], Mobats [the upper magi]. Much of the literature of Persia, notably works of learning and Zoroastrian texts, simply perished during the Alexandrian conquests."[85]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ present ♥ At its peak the permanent bureaucracy employed 3 million, and many millions on contract.[86] Including contractual functionaries, mercenaries and services that were obliged of conquered people estimate for workers in the bureaucracy would be 6-8 million.[87]

♠ Source of support ♣ state salary ♥

♠ Examination system ♣ present ♥ Examination system.[88]

♠ Merit promotion ♣ present ♥ Present. [89] However, ethnic Persians held the most important civil and military positions[90] so this likely was merit promotion among Persians, or perhaps merit promotion among Persians and among other ethnicity but with a bias toward Persians for the most important positions..

♠ Specialized government buildings ♣ present ♥ E.g. government mints.[91]

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ present ♥

"The Persian word for 'law' was data." "By royal decrees, local jurists codified the laws of their own nations, and imperial authorities enforced those laws. Thus, the Jews worked according to Biblical laws with the royal sanctions, the Egyptians according to older Egyptian laws, the Iranians according to Avestan injunctions and their local traditions."[92]

Civil law based on Persian law. Cyrus II and Darius I known to have made reforms. "Unfortunately no Achaemenid law code, comparable to the Babylonian one or to the Hittite laws, has survived, if any ever existed."[93]

"Universal justice system" and judges. [94]

"Punishment was as cruel as in the ancient Near East generally. Execution, crucifixion, impalement, mutilation, banishment were common."[95]

♠ Judges ♣ present ♥ Corrupt judges could be sentenced to death. [96]

♠ Courts ♣ present ♥ Law courts mentioned here for Egypt: In Late Period Egypt "Egyptian women (unlike Greeks) could act in transactions on their own behalf and without any guardian whatsoever; equally, women could come forward in law-courts totally unaided as plaintiffs or defendants. And it is quite evident that women were capable of independent economic activities regardless of marital status."[97]

♠ Professional Lawyers ♣ present ♥ "Alexander is cited by Zoroastrian tradition as having "killed the magi ... many teachers, lawyers, Herbats [the lower magi], Mobats [the upper magi]. Much of the literature of Persia, notably works of learning and Zoroastrian texts, simply perished during the Alexandrian conquests."[98] This quote seems to imply that Greek women might need the assistance of a lawyer, although a male relative could have been used: In Late Period Egypt "Egyptian women (unlike Greeks) could act in transactions on their own behalf and without any guardian whatsoever; equally, women could come forward in law-courts totally unaided as plaintiffs or defendants. And it is quite evident that women were capable of independent economic activities regardless of marital status."[99]

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Communal buildings ♣ present ♥

♠ Special purpose house ♣ present ♥

♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ present ♥ "The infrastructural and agricultural measures (the extension of the road system, the maintenance of river traffic and irrigation, and the provision of drinking water and new crops), modeled on Near Eastern examples, facilitated the rapid transfer of troops and improved the diet of royal subjects."[100] Darius the Great "initiated the construction of dams to prevent drought in India" and excavated a canal which connected the Red Sea with the Mediterranean.[101] "Darius ordered the reconstruction of the city of Sarod destroyed by the Greeks. Mendrokles presented to Darius a plan of the city, which was to be built over an area of 50 x 50 Ostad [1 Ostad = 200m]. Piped water and sewers were considered in the plan."[102]

♠ irrigation systems ♣ present ♥ "From the earliest times, the flow of water was controlled for agricultural purposes by an elaborate system of canals, sluices, dams, embankments, and dikes."[103] 1000 miles of irrigation canals. [104] Qanat technology. Subterranean irrigation canals.[105] Royal control over most of the irrigation systems and canals.[106] Egyptians called Darius the Great a Pharaoh "since by digging qanats and other initiatives he had supplied the south of Egypt with irrigation water."[107] "Iranians were the inventors of qanats ... during the Archaemenid era there appeared an extensive system of underground networks known as qanats" [108]
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ present ♥ "The infrastructural and agricultural measures (the extension of the road system, the maintenance of river traffic and irrigation, and the provision of drinking water and new crops), modeled on Near Eastern examples, facilitated the rapid transfer of troops and improved the diet of royal subjects."[109] "One of the greatest deeds of Darius the Great was the creation of "Water Organisation". The head of the organisation was called "Ao-Tar" or "Water Master" and he controlled the qanats, dams, rivers, etc." [110] "Darius ordered the reconstruction of the city of Sarod destroyed by the Greeks. Mendrokles presented to Darius a plan of the city, which was to be built over an area of 50 x 50 Ostad [1 Ostad = 200m]. Piped water and sewers were considered in the plan."[111]
♠ markets ♣ inferred present ♥ "The state was not only an important final recipient of merchandise and raw materials that had been acquired by Phoenician traders in the open market and that often finally flowed into the coffers of the Great King. It also played a pivotal role in the organization of the exchange of raw materials and rations for precious metals or even coins, therefore promoting the development of local markets."[112]
♠ food storage sites ♣ present ♥ Warehouses and granaries provisioned garrisons and workers. [113]

♠ Symbolic building ♣ present ♥

Darius built a monumental "rock-cut tomb in Naqsh-e Rostam near persepolis for himself and his immediate family."[114]

Tomb of Cyrus the Great at Parsargadae (built 546-530 BCE). [115] "Created some of the most impressive monuments of imperial and imperialistic art ever seen."[116]

Palatial residences in Parsargadae, Ecbatana, Susa, Persepolis. Babylon and Dahan-i-Ghulaman in Seistan. Sanctuaries and royal tombs such as Naqsh-i Rustem. [117]

Large building projects required many skilled stone workers and a well-organized administration. Persepolis was a palatial city. [118]

Sardis was the western capital of the Empire. 'many Lydian and Ionian artisans were employed by Cyrus to work on buildings at Pasargadae, his capital in the heart of Persia.'[119]

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ inferred present ♥ sports such as wrestling and boxing may have required infrastructure.

Polo was probably not present. "As Wolfgang Knauth shows in his study of the athletic performance of Iranian kings, the Achaemenid founders of the first Persian empire did not know the game. It seems probable that polo was introduced by the Parthian dynasty (247 BCE - 224 CE), which originated in north- ..."[120]


♠ Knowledge/information buildings ♣ inferred present ♥ Archives of bureaucracy. Zoroastrian libraries.

Transport infrastructure[edit]

♠ Roads ♣ present ♥ Royal road from Susa in Elam (near the Persian Gulf) to Sardis in Lydia (near the Aegean sea). [121] Paved road building ordered by Darius I. High quality and suitable for wheeled vehicles. Royal road is the best known of a number of roads built to facilitate the speedy movement of troops, royal inspectors and trade caravans. Other roads included Babylon to Persepolis via Susa, Babylon to Bactria via Ecbatana, and Issus to Sinope. The Royal road was 2,600 km long and had 111 royal post stations. [122]
♠ Bridges ♣ present ♥ "Mandorcles, another engineer of the Darius period, constructed a bridge over Begas Bosporus to allow the army to pass over. Bolts and nuts were used to fix the boards in its construction."[123] "Darius crossed into Europe (in about 513) over a pontoon bridge built by his Samian engineer, Mandrocles (a feat not rivaled until 1973), which continued the royal road into Europe."[124]
♠ Canals ♣ present ♥ Suez Canal and Atosa Canal. [125] Suez Canal which linked the west and east of the empire by sea was already planned by the Egyptians and was finished by Darius I. [126] Darius ordered a canal dug between Red Sea and the Nile; the commemorative stela suggests his primary transit interest was 'from Egypt through this canal to Persia'.[127]
♠ Ports ♣ inferred present ♥


The most impressive/costly building(s)[edit]

♠ height ♣ 43 ♥ Meters. Mausoleum at Halicarnassus stands at 140 ft or ~43 m. [128]
♠ extent ♣ ♥

Information[edit]

Measurement System[edit]

♠ Length ♣ present ♥ Different in each locality. The Archaemenids "did not attempt to standardize weights and measures."[132] Bivar (2010) says: "The principal units of length in the Near Eastern measurement systems were the foot and the cubit, standing in the ratio of 1:1.5. Subdivisions of the foot were its quarter, the palm, and its 16th, the finger. A higher unit, the fathom, consisted of four cubits."[133]
♠ Area ♣ present ♥ Different in each locality. The Archaemenids "did not attempt to standardize weights and measures."[134]
♠ Volume ♣ present ♥ Different in each locality. The Archaemenids "did not attempt to standardize weights and measures."[135] However, Bivar (2010) says there was an attempt at harmonization. "The dominant units of capacity in the Achaemenid period are defined by a vessel recovered in the excavations at Persepolis. As in the case of weight-units, the reforms of Darius seem to have made an attempt to harmonize the Babylonian and Old Iranian systems."[136]
♠ Weight ♣ present ♥ Different in each locality. The Archaemenids "did not attempt to standardize weights and measures."[137] However Bivar (2010) says there was a universal standard: the standard unit of weight of the shekel "was enforced throughout the Archaemenid Empire by Darius the Great ... around 515 BCE. In this system, the shekel stood at 8.40 grams."[138]
♠ Time ♣ present ♥ Different in each locality. The Archaemenids "did not attempt to standardize weights and measures."[139]
♠ Geometrical ♣ inferred present ♥
♠ Other ♣ ♥

Writing System[edit]

♠ Mnemonic devices ♣ inferred present ♥
♠ Nonwritten records ♣ inferred present ♥
♠ Written records ♣ present ♥ Sources include official and private documents in Old Persian, Elamite, Babylonian, Aramaic, Greek, Egyptian, Hebrew [140] and Akkadian. [141] Behistun Inscription of Darius I contains 3,000 words. Combined, the other royal inscriptions amount to 2,600 words. Other inscriptions include two texts from Cyrus the Great (8 words), 44 other texts from Darius I (1500 words), 13 texts from Xerxes (850 words), 7 texts from Artaxerxes I and II (180 words), 1 unassigned fragment (8 words). Persepolis fortification tablets from Darius I is an additional, a magnitude larger,source of text. [142]
♠ Script ♣ present ♥ Cuneiform. [143] Aramaic widespread in bureaucracy from Darius I. Totally replaced Elamite language and cuneiform writing by c460 BCE.[144]
♠ Non-phonetic writing ♣ absent ♥ Old Persian cuneiform is a semi-alphabetic (semisyllabic) cuneiform script that was the primary script for Old Persian. Texts written in this cuneiform have been found in Persepolis, Susa, Hamadan, Armenia, Romania (Gherla) and emerged around 515 BCE during the reign of Darius I and continuing on through the reign of his son, Xerxes I. Later kings down to Artaxerxes III used more recent forms of the language classified as "pre-Middle Persian". [145]
♠ Phonetic alphabetic writing ♣ present ♥ Old Persian cuneiform is a semi-alphabetic (semisyllabic) cuneiform script that was the primary script for Old Persian. Texts written in this cuneiform have been found in Persepolis, Susa, Hamadan, Armenia, Romania (Gherla) and emerged around 515 BCE during the reign of Darius I and continuing on through the reign of his son, Xerxes I. Later kings down to Artaxerxes III used more recent forms of the language classified as "pre-Middle Persian". [146]

Kinds of Written Documents[edit]

♠ Lists, tables, and classifications ♣ present ♥ land registers [147]
♠ Calendar ♣ present ♥ In approximately 539 BC, Persia's rulers conquered Babylon, and soon afterwards - at least by the 4th century BC - adopted the Babylonian luni-solar calendar for civil purposes. [148]
♠ Sacred Texts ♣ inferred present ♥ The Achaemenid period "witnessed major developments in art, philosophy, literature, historiography, religion, exploration, economics, and science, and those developments provided the direct background for the further changes, along similar lines, that made the Hellenistic period so important in history." [149]
♠ Religious literature ♣ inferred present ♥ The Achaemenid period "witnessed major developments in art, philosophy, literature, historiography, religion, exploration, economics, and science, and those developments provided the direct background for the further changes, along similar lines, that made the Hellenistic period so important in history." [150]
♠ Practical literature ♣ inferred present ♥ The Achaemenid period "witnessed major developments in art, philosophy, literature, historiography, religion, exploration, economics, and science, and those developments provided the direct background for the further changes, along similar lines, that made the Hellenistic period so important in history." [151]
♠ History ♣ present ♥ "Ancient Iranians favored oral narration of history, which allowed successive transmitters to rework narratives of events and reattribute them to different heroes at different times (Boyce, 1954, 1955, 1957; Shahbazi, 1990). Their oldest historical traditions are the heroic material found in the Avestan Yašts (Christensen, 1917, 1928, 1931; Boyce, Zoroastrianism I, pp. 92-108; Yarshater, pp. 411-53), in which "historical facts and accurate genealogies" are interwoven with "poetic fiction and fable." In these traditions "are seemingly preserved both secular and priestly traditions, transmitted by minstrel poets as well as by religious schools; and there are elements also of popular superstition and dread, in the tales of demons and witches and fearsome beasts. These intermingle with the stories of valour which show also the power of the gods to grant to men's prayers and succor them in distress" (Boyce, Zoroastrianism I, p. 108). With the conquest of the ancient Near East, the Iranians became familiar with cultures that had long established traditions of written history (Klima, pp. 214-17; Grayson, 1975a, pp. 1-7; G. Cameron, pp. 79-81). This led to a number of developments. Firstly, the Iranians began keeping records of historical events, of which Cyrus's Chronicle from Babylonia (see CYRUS CYLINDER) and Darius's Bisotun inscriptions (q.v.) and their Aramaic versions, which were dispatched to the empire's provinces, are the best examples. They meant to convincethe reader that "Persians were divinely appointed saviors whose mission was to bring justice, order, and tranquillity to the people of the world" (G. Cameron, pp. 81-94, esp. p. 93). The Achaemenids also kept Babylonian-style "diaries" (on the genre see Grayson, 1975a, p. 1)."[152] The Achaemenid period "witnessed major developments in art, philosophy, literature, historiography, religion, exploration, economics, and science, and those developments provided the direct background for the further changes, along similar lines, that made the Hellenistic period so important in history." [153]
♠ Philosophy ♣ inferred present ♥ "Iranians were familiar with Greek philosophy from the Achaemenid period."[154] The Achaemenid period "witnessed major developments in art, philosophy, literature, historiography, religion, exploration, economics, and science, and those developments provided the direct background for the further changes, along similar lines, that made the Hellenistic period so important in history." [155]
♠ Scientific literature ♣ inferred present ♥ Greeks practised medicine at the royal courts of Darius I and Artaxerxes II.[156] Darius the Great "dispatched Estilakis and a delegation to India to report on the regional facilities for construction of a dam. The latter presented a report, equal in quality to those prepared by modern day geography and hydrology experts."[157]
♠ Fiction ♣ present ♥ Present due to the presence of fictional literature in the regions they conquered. For the Persian tradition only the code would appear to be absent,. The possibly that some oral works were occasionally written down or imported and read by the scribal class cannot be excluded but ancient Mesopotamian royalty was often not literate. The Achaemenid period "witnessed major developments in art, philosophy, literature, historiography, religion, exploration, economics, and science, and those developments provided the direct background for the further changes, along similar lines, that made the Hellenistic period so important in history." [158] "The interest in oral literature in pre-Islamic Iran meant that, apart from state or commerical records and documents and, on rare occasions, religious works, nothing was written down until the Sasanian period. Secular oral literature was preserved orally by gosan (poet-ministrels) or khunyagar (story-tellers)."[159] "Epic stories, frequently in verse, remained an oral form until the Sasanian period".[160] inferred present due to presence of fictional literature in regions they conquered. for Persian tradition only code would appear to be inferred absent at this time.

Money[edit]

♠ Articles ♣ present ♥ taxes could take form of contributions in kind [161]
♠ Tokens ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Precious metals ♣ present ♥ part of taxes in form of precious metals. [162] "Achaemenid "bar-ingots" may have been ancestral to some of the Indian "bent-bar" currency."[163]
♠ Foreign coins ♣ inferred present ♥ Royal coinage encouraged trade. Before Darius trade in barter or Lydian gold coins.[164]
♠ Indigenous coins ♣ absent: 550-516 BCE; present: 515-330 BCE ♥ Daric. [165] Darius I was probably the first Achaemenid king to mint coins. Created a single currency monetary system. Standard coin was the gold Daric which was maintained at 97% purity. 3,000 darics made one talent. Silver coins were called shekels and were at least 90% pure. Twenty shekels to one daric, for a 40:3 silver-gold ratio. The currency system was maintained from 515 BCE until 330 BCE. The reluctance of the Persian kings to release their treasure to be minted hampered the empire's economy.[166] Royal coinage encouraged trade. Before Darius trade was in barter or Lydian gold coins. Satraps could coin money but only King of Kings could coin in gold. Coin potraits first appeared in Persia.[167]
♠ Paper currency ♣ absent ♥
♠ Debt and credit structures ♣ present ♥ Private banks (known in Mesopotamia from 2nd millennium BCE). Murashu and Sons at Nippur were involved in tax collecting, deposits and money-lending around 455-403 BCE.[168] Loans were taken by individuals from merchants during Late Period Egypt.[169] "Banking in Iran dates back to 600 BCE ... Private banks, such as those of the Egibi family and the Murashu family, were founded at that time and engaged in mortgages, deposits, and loans. Customers possessed checkbooks and invested in property, farms, slaves, cattle, irrigation projects, fishing, and commercial ships. The Egibi family had the oldest banking institution in Persia, with headquarters in Ecbatana, Pasargadae, Babylonia, Jerusalem, and Sardis at the time of Cyrus the Great. The bank was responsible for collecting taxes and fees for the central government."[170]
♠ Store of wealth ♣ present ♥

Postal System[edit]

♠ Couriers ♣ present ♥ Royal road basis of a courier system.[171]
♠ Postal stations ♣ present ♥ [172] Lettered communication between central bureaucracy and the satrapies.[173] "The Archaemenids introduced the world's first postal service, and it was said the network of relay horses could deliver mail to the furthest corner of the empire within 15 days."[174] "In Persia the postal service appears to have originated in the Achaemenid period. ... There were way stations where the couriers could rest and where fresh horses could be obtained. The itinerary was measured in parasangs, or stages, along roads that seem not to have been paved or well maintained (cf. Olm­stead, p. 299)."[175]
♠ General postal service ♣ suspected unknown ♥ [176] Lettered communication between central bureaucracy and the satrapies.[177] however unsure if this was available to private individuals "The Archaemenids introduced the world's first postal service, and it was said the network of relay horses could deliver mail to the furthest corner of the empire within 15 days."[178]
♠ Fastest individual communication ♣ 7 ♥ Sardis to Susa along the royal road could be covered in 7 days by royal couriers, using the postal-stations and multiple horses and couriers. According to Herodotus 90 days was the typical journey time. [179]

Military Technologies[edit]

Military use of Metals[edit]

♠ Copper ♣ present ♥ Copper used to make bronze. Greek mercenaries under Cyrus had "helmets, greaves and shields of bronze" [180] "Of the Medes and Persians as a whole, only a few wore armour. Some had body armour of iron scales ... and only some of the cavalry wore helmets of bronze or iron."[181]
♠ Bronze ♣ present ♥ Greek mercenaries under Cyrus had "helmets, greaves and shields of bronze" [182] "Of the Medes and Persians as a whole, only a few wore armour. Some had body armour of iron scales ... and only some of the cavalry wore helmets of bronze or iron."[183]
♠ Iron ♣ present ♥ Breastplates of iron.[184] "Of the Medes and Persians as a whole, only a few wore armour. Some had body armour of iron scales ... and only some of the cavalry wore helmets of bronze or iron."[185]
♠ Steel ♣ [absent; present] ♥ May have imported high quality steel. "It is believed that Indian steel was exported in the early centuries A.D. and was known even in the time of Alexander. By the sixth century there is more definite evidence of the manufacture of Damascene swords and the steel used for this purpose came from India."[186] Artaxerxes II of Persia (Achaemenids, ruled around 400 BCE) had a Greek physician called Ctesias of Cnidus who was impressed by his sword of Indian steel.[187][188]

Projectiles[edit]

♠ Javelins ♣ present ♥ Egyptians had javelins and Libyans had "fire-hardened" javelins. [189] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) heavy cavalry carried two short bronze or iron tipped javelin (for stabbing and throwing). The Achaemenids invented this particular form of javelin (zhubin).[190]
♠ Other thrown weapons ♣ present ♥ Spear throwers. [191]
♠ Atlatl ♣ absent ♥ New World weapon.
♠ Slings ♣ present ♥ According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) Persian light infantry carried the bow and sling, and Cyrus also made them carry spear and sword.[192]
♠ Self bow ♣ present ♥ Ethiopians had "rudimentary archery equipment."[193] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity): "The greatest number of Persian cavalry were light cavalry armed with the simple bow (noncomposite) and comprised mostly of irregular nationality troops, sometimes officered by Persians."[194]
♠ Composite bow ♣ inferred present ♥ According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) heavy cavalry and light infantry carried the bow, the type not specified by the author.[195] "Composite bows are known from both Mesopotamia and the Great Steppe from the III millennium BCE. The Scythian bow was different from the Mesopotamian one primarily in its overall dimensions - it was smaller so that it could be used from the horseback. At the same time, self bows were also in use, but because of their large size they were not suitable for use by horse riders."[196] However, the composite bow is present in previous and subsequent polities.
♠ Crossbow ♣ absent ♥
♠ Tension siege engines ♣ present ♥ The Macedonians of the time had "battering rams, catapults, and other siege engines" so Achaemenids would certainly have had them. [197]
♠ Sling siege engines ♣ absent ♥ Counter-weight trebuchet first used by the Byzantines in 1165 CE.
♠ Incendiaries ♣ present ♥ According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) first use of naphtha throwers in this period. "Cotton soaked in naphtha was attached to arrows and fired against wooden fortifications."[198]
♠ Gunpowder siege artillery ♣ absent ♥

♠ War clubs ♣ suspected unknown ♥ According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) the mace was the dominant weapon of war from 4000 BCE but had disappeared from Sumerian illustrations before 2500 BCE, a time when the helmet appears.[199] Almost certainly the technology was still present but the weapon may have been used less frequently. Coded present for Ur III, Akkad and Middle Elam and could possibly be 'inferred present' at this time.
♠ Battle axes ♣ present ♥ Sargarthians in the Persian army may have had battle axe.[200] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) Persian heavy cavalry were armed with the battleaxe.[201] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) heavy infantry carried long spear, short sword and battle axe.[202]
♠ Daggers ♣ present ♥ Knives.[203] Sargarthians in the Persian army carried a dagger.[204]
♠ Swords ♣ present ♥ Archaemenid cavalry used kopis swords.[205] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) "All armies after the seventeenth century B.C.E. carried the sword, but in none was it a major weapon of close combat; rather, it was used when the soldier's primary weapons, the spear and axe, were lost or broken."[206] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) Persian light infantry carried the bow and sling, and Cyrus also made them carry spear and sword.[207] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) heavy infantry carried long spear, short sword and battle axe.[208]

The phalanx was in use until the 1st century BCE.[209] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) Persian heavy cavalry carried two short javelin (for stabbing and throwing) and long wooden or metal lance or spear.[210] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) Persian light infantry carried the bow and sling, and Cyrus also made them carry spear and sword.[211] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) heavy infantry carried long spear, short sword and battle axe.[212]
♠ Polearms ♣ inferred absent ♥ According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) infantry that held its ground could deter chariot charges with their shields and spears.[213]

Animals used in warfare[edit]

♠ Dogs ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Cambyses in the 6th century BCE placed ibexes, sheep, dogs and cats on the front line to have a psychological impact on Egyptians who worshiped these animals and did not want to fire arrows near them.[214]
♠ Donkeys ♣ present ♥ Achaemenids (Darius the Great) used donkeys in their baggage train.[215]
♠ Horses ♣ present ♥ Chariots.[216] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) small chariot corps.[217] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) light cavalry armed with a simple bow and heavy cavalry. Initially the heavy carried were armed with bow, battleaxe and oval shield, later carried two short javelin (for stabbing and throwing), long wooden or metal lance or spear and oval shield.[218]
♠ Camels ♣ present ♥ According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) "The Persians experimented with the use of camel cavalry."[219] "Arab troops were equipped with swords slung over their backs, and many fought as archers on camels."[220] "Bactrian camels began to be used for cavalry between 500 and 100 BC."[221] Cyrus I pushed baggage camels on to the front lines to throw Lydian cavalry horses into a confused retreat. This event was a touchstone for future commanders who sought to keep their horses acquainted with camel scent.[222]
♠ Elephants ♣ present ♥ According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) "After conquests in India, the Persians introduced elephants into their armies."[223] With this reference we can date the code of present more precisely. Darius III of Persia had a few war elephants.[224] Indian war elephants at the Battle of Gaugamela in 331 BCE.
♠ Other ♣ ibex; sheep; cats ♥ Cambyses in the 6th century BCE placed ibexes, sheep, dogs and cats on the front line to have a psychological impact on Egyptians who worshiped these animals and did not want to fire arrows near them.[225]

Armor[edit]

♠ Wood, bark, etc ♣ present ♥ Rhomboid wicker shield.[226] Wicker targes.[227] "From ancient times the peoples of Persia favoured a light, tough shield made of withies or cane. As remarked on at the beginning of this chapter, Herodotus describes the soldiers of Xerxes who carry targes of wicker. Large and deeply convex shields built up of concentric rings of cane or withies are carried by the Sacae (Scythian) guards in the reliefs from the great staircase of the Achaemenid, from the Palace of Persepolis, now in the Berlin Museum. All but the caps of these guards are in the Persian fashion. The large shields are not those of nomadic horsemen, but are a foot soldier’s defence."[228]
♠ Leather, cloth ♣ present ♥ According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) cavalry carried a small oval shield made of leather with a metal rim.[229] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) cavalry wore leather greaves to protect the legs.[230] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) body armour of cavalryman could include a "leather coat covered with overlapping disks of bronze, iron, and sometimes gold."[231] Archaemenid cavalry wore scale armour but may also have worn linen armour of the Greek style.[232] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) heavy infantry wore black hoods in close combat though this does not seem to be armour.[233]
♠ Shields ♣ present ♥ "From ancient times the peoples of Persia favoured a light, tough shield made of withies or cane. All but the caps of these guards are in the Persian fashion. The large shields are not those of nomadic horsemen, but are a foot soldier’s defence."[234] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) Persian heavy cavalry carried an oval shield.[235] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) front ranks of the phalanx carried a tall wicker shield.[236]
♠ Helmets ♣ present ♥ Greek mercenaries under Cyrus had "helmets, greaves and shields of bronze" [237] "Of the Medes and Persians as a whole, only a few wore armour. Some had body armour of iron scales and wicker targes and only some of the cavalry wore helmets of bronze or iron."[238]
♠ Breastplates ♣ present ♥ Breastplates of iron.[239]
♠ Limb protection ♣ present ♥ Greek mercenaries under Cyrus had "helmets, greaves and shields of bronze" [240] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) Archaemenid cavalry wore leather greaves to protect the legs.[241]
♠ Chainmail ♣ absent ♥ "Of the Medes and Persians as a whole, only a few wore armour. Naval technology[edit]

♠ Small vessels (canoes, etc) ♣ inferred present ♥
♠ Merchant ships pressed into service ♣ inferred present ♥ In 345 BCE vs Sidonians: "The fleet consisted of 300 warships and 500 cargo vessels."[248]
♠ Specialized military vessels ♣ present ♥ Infantry, cavalry and navy. [249] In 345 BCE vs Sidonians: "The fleet consisted of 300 warships and 500 cargo vessels."[250] Navy had 600 fighting ships - tiremes, that had 170 oarsmen and 30 fighters.[251] According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) "the Persians were the first to introduce a large-scale navy used primarily in support of ground operations."[252]

Both town and citadel were surrounded by a defensive wall with a circumference of approximately thirteen kilometres. ... It would appear that this large city originated in the Achaemenid period (Masson 1959: 127)."[254] Was the wall of Marakanda made of rammed mud or stone? Assuming former.

Other technologies[edit]

Military Organization[edit]

♠ Standing armies ♣ present ♥ Developed some time after founding of the empire. In peacetime it consisted only of Persians. [260] From Darius a 'truly professional army' earlier they were recruited as needed.[261]

♠ Uniformity of equipment ♣ absent ♥ Great diversity of equipment among different ethnicities of the army.[262]

♠ Total army ♣ [500,000-1,000,000] ♥

According to Herodotus, total size of the land army alone was 1.7 million, with an additional 517,610 in the navy, 80,000 cavalry and 20,000 camel riders. This was the count of the actual fighting force before logistical personnel. Herodotus further says 5,283,220 men were levied in Europe. Subsequent historians believe the actual army size was much lower. [263]

"Even at the end of the empire, the Persians could deploy very large forces. In 331 B.C.E. ... at Arbela, Darius III fielded a force of 300,000 men, 40,000 cavalry, 250 chariots, and 50 elephants."[264]

♠ Professional army ♣ 80,000 ♥ From Darius a 'truly professional army' earlier they were recruited as needed.[265]

Army consisted of six corps (10,000) each infantrymen and 10,000 'Immortals' (an elite force) and 10,000 horsemen. Navy had 600 fighting ships.[266] 10,000 Persian royal guards whom Herodotus called "the Immortals" because the size of the unit was always maintained. [267]

♠ Largest fielded army ♣ 340,000 ♥

According to one military historian (data needs to be checked by an expert for this polity) "Even at the end of the empire, the Persians could deploy very large forces. In 331 B.C.E. ... at Arbela, Darius III fielded a force of 300,000 men, 40,000 cavalry, 250 chariots, and 50 elephants."[268]

In 345 BCE vs Sidonians: "According to Diodorus (XVI 43-51), the Persian Army counted 300,000 men, including 30,000 horsemen. The fleet consisted of 300 warships and 500 cargo vessels."[269]

Xerxes' invasion of Greece 120,000 (Young) or 180,000 (Olmstead). [270]

♠ Maximum logistical lag ♣ ♥

An ox cart could travel 2 mph for five hours. Five horses could carry the same load at 4 mph for 8 hours.[271] That would be 16 km per day for an ox cart. 51 km per day for a five-team horse-cart. Assyrians (early or late?) introduced the five-team horse-cart.[272]

♠ Sources of recruits ♣ domestic; foreign; mercenary ♥ Under Cyrus II all male Persians required to serve. [273] Non-Persians also used in the permanent garrisons, such as at the Nubian border, Elephantine in Egypt: " Persian rule was supported and defended by Persians, Medes, Greeks, Carians, Chorasmians, etc., but especially by Jews."[274]

Greek mercenaries used against Egypt mid 4th Century BCE. [275]

"The non-Medo-Persian Iranian contingents comprised mainly the Saka/Scythians, Parthava (Parthians), Chorasmians, Arians, Bactrians, and Soghdians."[276]

Lydians and Assyrians were heavy infantry.[277]

Assyrian spearmen.[278]

Babylonian archers.[279]

Carians had daggers and drepana sickle-swords.[280]

Egyptians with long double-curved bow.[281] Egyptians had javelins, swords, axes, and spears for close combat.[282]

Phoenicians were marines, had Greek-style helmets, linen cuirasses, javelins and "rimless shields."[283]

Indians "armed with iron-tipped arrows and very large broadswords" [284]


♠ Procurement of equipment and animals ♣ by soldiers; by state ♥

Soldiers settled on military land allotments probably required to source own equipment. [285]

Crimson tunics: Greek mercenaries thought to have supplied their own weapons "but their tunics may have been distributed."[286]

General Characteristics of Warfare[edit]

Types of Warfare[edit]

♠ Inter-polity wars ♣ present ♥ In 330 BCE, Darius III became the twelfth and final emperor in the Achaemenid line when he succumbed to the conquests of Alexander the Great and his invading Macedonian army.[287]

♠ Internal wars ♣ present ♥ Bisotun inscription tells that Darius triumphed over nine rebel 'kings'.[288]

Almost all of the Achaemenid power transitions were contested. The only example of orderly, consensual succession seems to be that which followed the death of Cyrus the Great, after which his chosen heir and eldest son Cambyses took the throne.[289] Below is a list of contested power transitions and a brief historical account for each. 1. Cyrus II, 'the Great' (r. 560/59-530 bce).[290] Astyages was the last Median king, overthrown by Cyrus II around 550 bce.[291] There is 'conflicting information' about Astyages, his family and the rise of Cyrus. Frye believes that common motifs from Indo-European myth have been integrated into the 'Cyrus saga' related in the Greek and Roman sources, making it hard to sift fact from fantasy,[292] and Cizek notes that tales of Cyrus's birth fall into the '"divine" bastard' category of myths.[293]. He was probably Astyages's grandson, son of Cambyses I and Astyages's daughter Mandane,[294] but stories of his concealment and secret childhood with a 'herdsman and his wife in the north of Media' are 'not to be believed'.[295] From Akkadian sources we can be reasonably sure that 'Cyrus defeated Astyages in battle, and the Median army revolted against Astyages delivering him to Cyrus who plundered Ecbatana and took Astyages as a prisoner to Persis'.[296] The Achaemenid Empire was thus founded through a violent revolt. (NB: Cyrus became king of the Persian tribes around 560 bce, ten years before rising up to dethrone Astyages).[297] 2. Bardiya or 'false Bardiya' (Gaumata) (r. 522 bce).[298] Cambyses II had become king in an orderly succession after the death of his father Cyrus the Great. However, the jostling for the throne that followed Cambyses' death and eventually resulted in the accession of Darius I is 'one of the most debated episodes of early Achaemenid history ... During this brief period, the Achaemenid throne was held/seized by one or several individual(s), about whose identity our sources provide conflicting information.'[299] Shayegan outlines two competing hypotheses: 'false-Bardiya' and 'true-Bardiya'. Adherents of the former, who accept the truth of Darius' statements on the Bisitun (or 'Behistun') inscription as well as the (slightly different) account of Herodotus, believe that before Cambyses II left to fight in Egypt, he secretly had his full brother Bardiya (also known as Smerdis) murdered. The man who later seized power, calling himself Bardiya, was an impostor: a Magian called Gaumata, in fact the brother of Patizeithes, the Magian whom Cambyses had left in charge of the throne.[300][301] However, other scholars consider the Bisitun inscription to be a 'propagandistic narrative destined to cover Darius' own coup d'etat against the legitimate Bardiya, for the concealment of whose rule the presumed fiction of a usurper-magus was invented'.[302][303] Briant states that the true-Bardiya hypothesis, entailing propagandistic deception by Darius, is 'these days generally accepted'[304], and this is certainly the line taken by Bruce Lincoln in a 2007 book.[305] Equally unclear is the mechanism by which power was transferred from Cambyses to Bardiya/Gaumata. One possibility is that this was not a 'real usurpation' but an example of orderly succession after Cambyses' death in Egypt - Cambyses left no sons, and Bardiya would have been the 'sole male descendant of the royal family'.[306] Alternatively, (true) Bardiya may have taken advantage of Cambyses' absence to usurp his brother's throne, or (false) Bardiya/Gaumata, the impostor Magian, may have usurped the throne, as Darius claims. 3. Darius I, 'the Great' (r. 522-486 bce).[307] Before he came to the throne, the man who became Darius the Great served Cambyses II as 'spear-bearer' in Egypt.[308] According to the Histories of Herodotus and Darius' own account in the Bisotun inscription, Darius killed the 'impostor' Magian Gaumata, who had seized the throne in Cambyses' absence, claiming to be Cambyses' brother Bardiya.[309] (See no. 3, above). Darius was aided by six Persian nobles[310] and may even have played a relatively minor role in the planning of the coup.[311] After Bardiya/Gaumata was dead, 'the Seven' entered discussions about the rulership of the empire (according to Herodotus, who also says that one man, Otanes, favoured 'the rule of the multitude') and Darius was eventually accepted as the rightful king on the condition that he preserve special rights for his co-conspirators.[312][313] He then began to shore up his legitimacy: he used the Bisotun inscription to assert his hereditary right to the throne, presenting himself as the descendant of an ancient kingly line: 'For this reason, we are called Achaemenians. From long ago we have been noble. From long ago our family (tauma) had been kings'.[314] Despite this, his 'seizure of the throne was immediately followed by risings in Mesopotamia, Armenia, and the Iranian lands ... There does not seem to have been popular support for Darius'.[315] In no sense could we call this transition of power 'orderly'. 4. Xerxes I (r. 486-465 bce).[316] Xerxes was the son of Darius the Great, but even his accession was not simple.[317] Herodotus gives a detailed account of a 'great quarrel' between Darius' sons, which is partly corroborated by Xerxes' own XPf ('Harem') inscription.[318] According to Herodotus, 'while Darius was making preparations against Egypt and Athens, a great quarrel arose among his sons concerning the chief power in the land. They held that before his army marched he must declare an heir to the kingship according to Persian law. Three sons had been born to Darius before he became king by his first wife, the daughter of Gobryas, and four more after he became king by Atossa daughter of Cyrus. Artobazanes was the oldest of the earlier sons, Xerxes of the later; and as sons of different mothers they were rivals. Artobazanes pleaded that he was the oldest of all Darius' offspring and that it was everywhere customary that the eldest should rule; Xerxes argued that he was the son of Cyrus' daughter Atossa and that it was Cyrus who had won the Persians their freedom.'[319] Darius eventually decided in favour of Xerxes, publicly proclaiming him as the heir to the crown.[320] 5. Artaxerxes I (r. 465-424 bce).[321] Xerxes I was assassinated by court officials in a plot masterminded by Artabanus, his chief bodyguard who 'aspired to the kingship' according to Justin and Diodorus.[322][323] The Greek and Roman sources generally agree that Artabanus then convinced Artaxerxes, the dead king's youngest son, that his elder brother Darius was the murderer. Artaxerxes kills Darius and becomes king, but then discovers Artabanus' deception and has him killed.[324] The degree to which this transition of power was contested is unclear: 'According to Diodorus and Justin, Artaxerxes then reigns without further ado. According to Ctesias, a quarrel arises between the accomplices - Artabanus's sons on the one hand, the other Persians on the other ... Bactria revolts, led by a different Artabanus; Artaxerxes wins and subdues Bactria'.[325] There was another son called Hystaspes, the second eldest, but he was satrap in Bactra at the time of his father's assassination.[326] Briant thinks that Hystaspes may have been the 'other' Artabanus mentioned by Ctesias, so the Bactrian rebellion could be seen as the response of an older brother who considered himself the rightful heir to the throne.[327] 6. Xerxes II (r. 424/3 bce).[328] Artaxerxes died in late 424 or early 423 bce, leaving his only legitimate son to take 'the throne under the name Xerxes (II)'.[329] 'It is likely that Xerxes took the throne because of his parentage and perhaps also because his father had recognized him as crown prince.'[330] However, I have counted this transition as contested because Xerxes was assassinated after a reign of less than two months and was not subsequently recognized as a legitimate king in the Babylonian records (see numbers 7 and 8). 7. Sogdianus (r. 424/3 bce).[331] Although Xerxes II was the only legitimate son of Artaxerxes I, he also had a number of illegitimate sons, of which 'three were of consequence'.[332] One of these, Sogdianus, assassinated Xerxes while he was drunk, after a reign of just 45 days, and seized the throne.[333][334] 8. Darius II (r. 423-404 bce).[335] Another illegitimate son of Artaxerxes I, Ochus, challenged Sogdianus' accession and soon managed to wrest the throne from him in 423 bce.[336] He took the throne name Darius II.[337] It should be noted that the Babylonian scribes recognized the reigns of neither Xerxes II or Sogdianus, and that Sogdianus and Ochus/Darius II probably 'announced their pretensions simultaneously', winning over different nobles to their respective sides.[338] In light of this, it seems that Sogdianus would have had a very brief and contested reign. 9. Artaxerxes II (r. 404-359 bce).[339] Darius II 'had several sons from his marriage to Parysatis', and at least four of them survived past childhood.[340] He seems to have chosen Arses as his heir, who acceded to the throne after his father's death in 404 bce and took the throne name Artaxerxes (II).[341] However, he was challenged by his younger brother, Cyrus (the Younger), who marched against him using Greek mercenaries but was defeated in battle.[342] Briant dubs this the 'war of the two brothers'.[343] 10. Artaxerxes III (r. 359-338 bce).[344] According to an assortment of classical authors (Justin, Plutarch, Aelian), Artaxerxes II had three legitimate sons 'from his marriage to Stateira: Darius (the oldest), Ariaspes ... and Ochus (the youngest ...)'.[345] He chose Darius as his heir, but Darius plotted to kill him and was sentenced to death; Ochus then drove his brother Ariaspes to suicide.[346] When Artaxerxes II died of old age, Ochus took the throne under the name Artaxerxes III.[347] 11. Artaxerxes IV (r. 338-336 bce).[348] According to several classical authors, at this point the kingship came under the influence of the powerful eunuch Bagoas, who became chiliarch in 343.[349] Bagoas 'assassinated Artaxerxes III and his brothers' and installed Arses, Artaxerxes' son, on the throne.[350][351] Arses took the throne name Artaxerxes (IV). Briant is sceptical of classical accounts of the scheming eunuch Bagoas, noting that this was a common trope in Greek depictions of the Persians, but accepts that Artaxerxes III was assassinated.[352] Although the kingship passed from father to son, this clearly cannot be seen as an orderly transition of power. 12. Darius III (r. 336-330 bce).[353] To follow the classical narrative, after installing Artaxerxes IV (Arses) on the throne, Bagoas the eunuch then also poisoned him and his children 'and chose Codoman/Artasata to be king (Darius III)'.[354] Darius III was a second cousin and 'not a direct descendant' of Artaxerxes IV.[355] We do have some corroboration for this account: 'The murder of Arses after two years of rule is also men­tioned (without citing his name) in a well-known Babylonian text, the Dynastic Proph­ecy, which states that the murder was committed by a sa resi (obviously Bagoas), after which a prince seized power and ruled for five years (Darius III)'.[356] Darius then had to contend with the invading Alexander of Macedon and disloyal Persian aristocrats. In 330 bce, Darius III was 'murdered by two Persian nobles (Bessos and Nabarzanes), one of whom proclaim[ed] himself king of Persia in eastern Iran and Bactria'.[357] However, through a combination of diplomacy - for instance, fashioning himself as Darius III's 'avenger and heir' and adopting Achaemenid royal customs - and military 'brutality', Alexander of Macedon was able to achieve recognition as the new ruler of the Persian Empire.[358] Some scholars consider him to be the 'last of the Achaemenids'.[359]

♠ Intra-elite conflicts ♣ present ♥ Artaxerxes III "poisoned by his own physician at the instigation of the court eunuch Bagoas". [360] Plutarch says Artaxerxes III (358-338 BCE) seized throne by assassinating eight half-brothers.

♠ Popular uprisings ♣ inferred present ♥ Bisotun inscription tells that Darius triumphed over nine rebel 'kings'.[361]

♠ Military revolts ♣ present ♥

♠ Separatist rebellions ♣ present ♥ Pedubast III's Rebellion. Psamtik IV's? Rebellion. Rebellion of Inaros. Greek cities of Asia 479 BCE (after battle of Mycale). In Egypt, "There were several attempts to expel the foreigners, especially in the Western Delta, and these are interpreted as 'popular' rather than 'nationalistic' by Devauchelle." [362] "After conquering Egypt in 525, the Persians faced repeated Egyptian revolts over the next century and a quarter, the first almost immediately after the initial conquest. Persian recovery efforts succeeded until the end of the fifth century, when Egypt finally broke away entirely."[363] Bisotun inscription tells that Darius triumphed over nine rebel 'kings'.[364]

♠ rural succession ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Violent civil conflict ♣ present ♥ Bisotun inscription tells that Darius triumphed over nine rebel 'kings'.[365]

♠ Violent riots urban ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Oppression / extermination of subaltern group ♣ suspected unknown ♥

Cultural distance[edit]

♠ Wars within same ethnic group ♣ present ♥ Plutarch says Artaxerxes III (358-338 BCE) seized throne by assassinating eight half-brothers.

♠ Wars involving groups with different dialects ♣ inferred present ♥ Darius fought a widespread rebellion.[366]

♠ Wars involving groups with different languages ♣ present ♥ Nubians. Karga Oasis. Pedubast III. Expedition to Cyrenaica. Psamtik IV's? Rebellion. Rebellion of Inaros. Saite Egypt. 29th Dynasty Egypt. 30th Dynasty Egypt. Meroe Kingdom. Macedonian Adventurer.[367]

♠ Wars involving groups with different language families ♣ present ♥ Nubians. Karga Oasis. Pedubast III. Expedition to Cyrenaica. Psamtik IV's? Rebellion. Rebellion of Inaros. Saite Egypt. 29th Dynasty Egypt. 30th Dynasty Egypt. Meroe Kingdom. Macedonian Adventurer.[368] The Achaemenids spoke an Indo-European language,[369] while the people of Egypt and Nubia spoke Afro-Asiatic languages.

♠ Wars between groups from different archaeological cultures ♣ present ♥ Nubians. Karga Oasis. Pedubast III. Expedition to Cyrenaica. Psamtik IV's? Rebellion. Rebellion of Inaros. Saite Egypt. 29th Dynasty Egypt. 30th Dynasty Egypt. Meroe Kingdom. Macedonian Adventurer.[370]

♠ Wars between different religious sects ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Wars between different world religions ♣ absent ♥

♠ Wars of world religion against tribal religion ♣ present ♥ E.g. wars with Greeks.[371] The Achaemenids were Zoroastrians, whereas we would classify the Greek religion as "tribal".

♠ Wars on Urbanism-Literacy frontier ♣ inferred present ♥ "Darius also campaigned against a group of Scythians in southeastern Europe, in the Danube and Black Sea regions. Herodotus is our main source. [...] Most historians date the campaign sometime in the later 510s BCE. Details of the military campaign are sparse, and interpretations of its significance vary widely. [...] Herodotus judged the campaign a failure, because Darius ultimately withdrew, but a more sober assessment of his possible objectives, and a consideration of his subsequent moves, tempers that negative judgment."[372] "The terms “Scythians” or “Scyths” (Greek Σκúθης, Σκúθoι ; Latin Scythae) do not describe peoples or tribes within an ethnic definition, but a cultural phenomenon, which manifests itself in the dispersal of horse‐riding nomads and their culture in the Eurasian steppes from the eighth century BCE, where they are mentioned for the first time during the reign of Sargon II (721–705 BCE). [...] “Scythians” did not leave any written sources."[373]

♠ Wars on steppe frontier ♣ present ♥ "Darius also campaigned against a group of Scythians in southeastern Europe, in the Danube and Black Sea regions. Herodotus is our main source. [...] Most historians date the campaign sometime in the later 510s BCE. Details of the military campaign are sparse, and interpretations of its significance vary widely. [...] Herodotus judged the campaign a failure, because Darius ultimately withdrew, but a more sober assessment of his possible objectives, and a consideration of his subsequent moves, tempers that negative judgment."[374] "The terms “Scythians” or “Scyths” (Greek Σκúθης, Σκúθoι ; Latin Scythae) do not describe peoples or tribes within an ethnic definition, but a cultural phenomenon, which manifests itself in the dispersal of horse‐riding nomads and their culture in the Eurasian steppes from the eighth century BCE, where they are mentioned for the first time during the reign of Sargon II (721–705 BCE)."[375]

♠ Wars on agricultural frontier ♣ absent ♥

Intensity of warfare[edit]

♠ Annexation ♣ present ♥ "Cyrus II the Great continued his military domination, conquering the wealthy Lydian Kingdom in modern-day Turkey along with most of Asia Minor and the Neo-Babylonian Kingdom in Mesopotamia, as well as consolidating Persia's hold over much of central Asia as far as modern Pakistan. His son and heir, Cambyses II, continued this tradition, expanding Achaemenid rule into the large and wealthy kingdom of Egypt. After Cambyses II's death in 522 BCE, a noble Persian named Darius came to power after overthrowing an alleged usurper to the throne (Gautama, supposedly posing as Cyrus II's son Bardiya, more commonly known by his Greek name Smerdis)."[376]

♠ Looting ♣ present ♥ Mid 4th Century BCE Egypt plundered.[377]

♠ Sack ♣ present ♥ "After the victory, Miletus was besieged and ultimately sacked; its inhabitants were sold into slavery or deported, and its main sanctuary at nearby Didyma was burned."[378]

♠ Rape ♣ present ♥ "A failed campaign against the island of Naxos (in the middle of the Aegean, west in a line from Halicarnassus in Ionia) served as a catalyst for a wider revolt, or series of revolts, between 499 and 494 BCE. [...] Mop-up operations continued through the following year (493 BCE) as the Persians reasserted their authority throughout Ionia. They carried through on threats made earlier, before Lade, for those who did not submit. Select girls were sent to the royal court, boys were made into eunuchs."[379]

♠ Deportation ♣ present ♥ "The surviving part of the Sidonian population was sold into slavery and transported to Babylon and Susa. The deportation is attested by a document which was composed in Babylon in October 345 B.C."[380] In 344 BCE, some of the Jews "were deported to Hyrcania along the Caspian Sea". [381]

♠ Expulsion ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Enslavement ♣ present ♥ "The surviving part of the Sidonian population was sold into slavery and transported to Babylon and Susa. The deportation is attested by a document which was composed in Babylon in October 345 B.C."[382]

♠ Imprisonment ♣ inferred present ♥ From Akkadian sources we can be reasonably sure that 'Cyrus defeated Astyages in battle, and the Median army revolted against Astyages delivering him to Cyrus who plundered Ecbatana and took Astyages as a prisoner to Persis'.[383]. women also taken as prisoners to serve as concubines, maids, or entertainers [384]

♠ Destruction ♣ present ♥ Sidon 345 BCE "Artaxerxes decided to chastise Sidon in a severe manner and to break the morale of the other Phoenician cities. Many Sidonians burned their own houses, their wives, children, and themselves. Those who remained were thrown into the fire from the town walls. According to Diodorus, more than 40,000 people were killed." [385] Mid 4th Century BCE Egyptian revolt punished with destruction of towns and temples. [386]

♠ Torture ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Mutilation ♣ present ♥ "A failed campaign against the island of Naxos (in the middle of the Aegean, west in a line from Halicarnassus in Ionia) served as a catalyst for a wider revolt, or series of revolts, between 499 and 494 BCE. [...] Mop-up operations continued through the following year (493 BCE) as the Persians reasserted their authority throughout Ionia. They carried through on threats made earlier, before Lade, for those who did not submit. Select girls were sent to the royal court, boys were made into eunuchs."[387]

♠ Targeted massacre ♣ present ♥ Sidon 345 BCE King Tennes, who was later executed by Artaxerxes, "treacherously handed his companions over to the Persians, who on the orders of Artaxerxes were all executed." [388]

♠ General massacre ♣ inferred present ♥ Targeted massacre and extermination already attested for this polity. E.g. Sidon 345 BCE King Tennes, who was later executed by Artaxerxes, "treacherously handed his companions over to the Persians, who on the orders of Artaxerxes were all executed." [389]

♠ Extermination ♣ present ♥ Sidon 345 BCE "Artaxerxes decided to chastise Sidon in a severe manner and to break the morale of the other Phoenician cities. Many Sidonians burned their own houses, their wives, children, and themselves. Those who remained were thrown into the fire from the town walls. According to Diodorus, more than 40,000 people were killed." ... "The surviving part of the Sidonian population was sold into slavery and transported to Babylon and Susa. The deportation is attested by a document which was composed in Babylon in October 345 B.C."[390]

♠ Ethnocide ♣ absent ♥ "It was characteristic of Achaemenid polity to grant their provinces and satrapies local autonomy, allowing the greatest possible freedom for all cultural, religious and economic structures and 'to interfere as little as possible in the traditional social structures of their province.' When Persian sovereignty was endangered by upheaval or conflict, however, this local autonomy could immediately be curtailed. In the sphere of religious observance, moreover, radical interventions could be made into the practices of a non-Zoroastrian religion if the sensibilities of the Persian governor were offended." Example: Achaemenids banned "burnt offerings in the newly built Jewish temple of Elephantine ... a rational decision in view of the religion of the ruling dynasty, and illustrates the boundaries of Achaemenid religious tolerance."[391]


Archaeological proxies:

♠ Skeletal evidence of violence ♣ present ♥ Referring to excavation carried out in Lydian Sardis: "In addition to pottery and other material offering an absolute date in the mid sixth century B.C., the destruction contained evocative remains of a battle: weapons, armor, and the skeletons of casualties. Two human skeletons were found in the recess (figs. 5 [3, 6] , 8). Both were males in their early to mid 20s, and both displayed physiognomic traits of soldiers, such as differential development of the arms and shoulders consistent with carrying a shield on the left and wielding a weapon with the right arm. The neck vertebrae of the skeleton in the corner of the recess were compressed, probably by wearing a heavy object, such as a helmet, on his head. Both had survived injuries during their lives, including wounds to the head, arms, and hands; the casualty in the erosion gully had suffered a broken rib three to four weeks before death, perhaps at Pteria, as described by Herodotus (1.76). And both died violently, with broken forearms, probably from warding off blows ("parry fractures"), and wounds in the back inflicted at the time of death. The casualty in the gully had a broken neck and bore three head wounds from at least two different weapons, two from a sharp-edged weapon such as a sword and one from a narrower, blunter one, perhaps a battle axe."[392]

♠ Mass burials ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Settlement destruction ♣ present ♥ "Although often indirect, scattered across the empire is tantalizing evidence for the deployment of the instruments of violence. At Gordion, for example, a major stronghold in Phrygia strategically located on the Royal Road from Sardis to Susa, archaeologists have associated the presence of arrowheads embedded in fortification walls, the destruction of a nearby pre - Achaemenid fortress abutted by what appears to be a monumental siege mound, and the abandonment of a pre-Achaemenid Lower Town, with the historically attested Persian capture of Gordion in 546 BC (Voigt and Young 1999)."[393]

♠ Pictorial evidence of warfare ♣ present ♥ "Placing the enemy under the victor's foot must at all times have been a very degrading and insulting act, as proven by its continuous use in royal iconography--from Narmer's palette to Achaemenid Persia and Imperial Rome (e.g., Porada 1965)."[394]
