Ritual variables[edit]

A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥ These codes were developed at Seshat General Workshop in Oxford 2017


♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ sip ♥ "Allocations for sip include flour, barley, wine, sheep/goats, poultry, and cattle. The cumulative value of these allocations is among the highest for religious purposes recorded in the Fortification archive (S5). In fact, the sip texts explicitly state that large amounts were consumed, presumably by substantial groups of people; in one case, the human beneficiaries are identified as royal muleteers (S6.3.3). The occurrence of a kurdabattis, 'chief of workers', in several texts confirms that groups of laborers, possibly consisting of up to 520 individuals, may have attended the feast. Apart from its social and ideological functions, sip also had an economic side to it (cf. S4 on lan), namely, the distribution of meat rations to laborers. "Dated texts on sip indicate that the feast was celebrated in November/December, that there was no fixed date, and that sip could be celebrated more than once during the same season. Practical reasons for such an autumnal feast involving a high amount of animal sacrifices may have been the reduction of (internal) herds owing to the scarcity of fodder during the winter season, the occurrence of the autumnal rutting season in October, and the occurrence of the first lambing season in November (S6.3.4). The official reason, on the other hand, may have been the presence of the king in Fars during the Fall season, as is suggested (however elusively) by Greek and Elamite sources. His presence at the time of at least some sip celebrations may be assumed (S6.4.1). "The royal connection is expressed not only by the timing of sip but also through the pronounced involvement of Parnakka and Zissawis in its performance and organization (paralleled only in the sumar and basur offerings, which are also directly connected to the crown [S6.2]). Since Xerxes himself claims to have performed sip (XPh), it seems reasonable to suggest that Parnakka and Zissawis acted as the king’s representatives when they presided over the feast (S6.4.2). In addition, sip is mostly celebrated at places with a marked royal character: Appistapdan, Tikranus (large plantations; royal banquets), and Pasargadae (plantation; palaces of Cyrus; coronation ceremony). Finally, an analysis of Fortification texts on poultry, cattle, and sheep/ goats suggests that animals issued for sacrificial purposes always had a specific royal "earmark" that would be understood by the people consuming these gifts from the king. Such royal food tagging is confirmed by the Greek sources (S6.4.2). "As Herodotus reports, the Persian king organized a feast called tukta, which seems to have served as a forum for the acquittal of "debts," i.e., the rewarding of services rendered to the king (S6.4.3). Though perhaps not the same feast, sip may have had similar characteristics; the participation of royal muleteers, possibly responsible for the movements of the court, in a sip ceremony would suggest so. The feast could therefore be seen as an element in a refined system of royal gift-giving in Fars as attested in Elamite and Greek sources. The common factor in all these royal gifts is that they underlined the king's position as the greatest giver." [395]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ "Dated texts on sip indicate that the feast was celebrated in November/December, that there was no fixed date, and that sip could be celebrated more than once during the same season. Practical reasons for such an autumnal feast involving a high amount of animal sacrifices may have been the reduction of (internal) herds owing to the scarcity of fodder during the winter season, the occurrence of the autumnal rutting season in October, and the occurrence of the first lambing season in November (S6.3.4). The official reason, on the other hand, may have been the presence of the king in Fars during the Fall season, as is suggested (however elusively) by Greek and Elamite sources. His presence at the time of at least some sip celebrations may be assumed (S6.4.1)." [396]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ "Dated texts on sip indicate that the feast was celebrated in November/December, that there was no fixed date, and that sip could be celebrated more than once during the same season. Practical reasons for such an autumnal feast involving a high amount of animal sacrifices may have been the reduction of (internal) herds owing to the scarcity of fodder during the winter season, the occurrence of the autumnal rutting season in October, and the occurrence of the first lambing season in November (S6.3.4). The official reason, on the other hand, may have been the presence of the king in Fars during the Fall season, as is suggested (however elusively) by Greek and Elamite sources. His presence at the time of at least some sip celebrations may be assumed (S6.4.1)." [397]

Euphoric elements
♠ feasting ♣ present ♥ "Allocations for sip include flour, barley, wine, sheep/goats, poultry, and cattle. The cumulative value of these allocations is among the highest for religious purposes recorded in the Fortification archive (S5). In fact, the sip texts explicitly state that large amounts were consumed, presumably by substantial groups of people; in one case, the human beneficiaries are identified as royal muleteers (S6.3.3). The occurrence of a kurdabattis, 'chief of workers', in several texts confirms that groups of laborers, possibly consisting of up to 520 individuals, may have attended the feast. Apart from its social and ideological functions, sip also had an economic side to it (cf. S4 on lan), namely, the distribution of meat rations to laborers." [412]
♠ alcohol ♣ present ♥ "Sacrificial commodities" consumed at sacrificial feasts in Elam normally included wine and beer [413].

Cohesion
♠ honour code ♣ present ♥ "That which is celebrated is the community that convenes and reconstitutes itself. Group identity, social hierarchy, and bonds of loyalty are reconfirmed in the sacrifice and the ensuing banquet. This is of special importance in a situation whereby ruler and subjects are separated from each other during large parts of the year. [...] Likewise, sip was a reunion: it seems to have been an occasion at which the king’s presence in Fars was made tangible by the gift of extraordinary rations, by the special location of the feast at royal plantations and at the site of ancient Pasargadae, and by his personal attendance (XPh) or that of his highest representative." [416]

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ lan ♥ A monthly offering of wine/beer, grain/flour, fruit, and livestock (sheep/goats) [426]. "In the unlikely case that there were strict cultural barriers between Elamites and Iranians, lan would be a border-crossing phenomenon. Instead of being exclusively Iranian, linked to Auramazda or even a Zoroastrian 'state-offering,' lan seems to fit nicely in the panorama of Elamite-Iranian acculturation. Rooted in the Elamite past and cultic tradition, Achaemenid lan came to denote a type of offering that could be performed by all kinds of cultic personnel, notably šatin and makuš. It is found in the immediate vicinity of Persepolis, but also in the old Elamite towns of Gisat, Hidali and Hunar (Huhnur; cf. App.6.2. below). Although inventories of offerings do not reflect a cultic reality (combined cults), they do enumerate offerings carried out under the aegis of a single official and performed by a single officiant in a single region. In these lists lan is found with gods of both Iranian (e.g. Išpandaramattiš) and Elamite (e.g., Napiriša, Humban) descent." [427]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ monthly ♥ "If lan is special, it is not because its provisions are regulated and fixed ('regelmäßige Durchfürung'), but because they refer, implicitly or explicitly, to a monthly and sometimes to a daily ceremony. A series of twelve monthly, or 360 daily offerings cannot be established for any other offering with the exception of the šumar and bašur offerings plus a few isolated cases (all discussed below). Apart from these exceptions and lan itself, we never find offerings repeated during several or twelve successive months." [428]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ monthly ♥ "If lan is special, it is not because its provisions are regulated and fixed ('regelmaßige Durchfürung'), but because they refer, implicitly or explicitly, to a monthly and sometimes to a daily ceremony. A series of twelve monthly, or 360 daily offerings cannot be established for any other offering with the exception of the šumar and bašur offerings plus a few isolated cases (all discussed below). Apart from these exceptions and lan itself, we never find offerings repeated during several or twelve successive months." [429]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ monthly ♥ "If lan is special, it is not because its provisions are regulated and fixed ('regelmäßige Durchfürung'), but because they refer, implicitly or explicitly, to a monthly and sometimes to a daily ceremony. A series of twelve monthly, or 360 daily offerings cannot be established for any other offering with the exception of the šumar and bašur offerings plus a few isolated cases (all discussed below). Apart from these exceptions and lan itself, we never find offerings repeated during several or twelve successive months." [430]
♠ Duration ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ in absolute numbers
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ clergy; administrative personnel. Unclear. "The personnel receiving provisions for lan are introduced just by their names in 38 cases. Otherwise, they are designated as šatin, lan-lirira, haturmakša, makuš or pirramadda [...]. The last three terms are Iranian, but their etymology and/or the exact function they may have implied are debated. The precise definition and associations of šatin (cultic expert) cannot be readily given either. Occasional statements to the contrary notwithstanding, haturmakša, makuš and pirramadda appear in other religious contexts, without lan, as well; the same holds true for šatin. In addition, haturmakša is the designation of certain officials in the administrative hierarchy mentioned in the summaries of journals and account. Such profane professional contexts do not seem to exist for the other designations." [431]

♠ Orthopraxy checks ♣ present ♥ The precise quantities of each type of offering (wine, grain, livestock) to be allocated for this ritual were recorded in the so-called "Fortification archive". "The scope of the archive made up by the Fortification tablets, dating to Dar. 13-28 (509-493 BC), is modest and can be described as the handling (not the production per se) of locally grown and produced food-stuffs, and locally bred and raised livestock (including game fowl fattened in captivity). Its purpose was to arrange, survey, record, and account for the streams of these commodities within what I call the 'Persepolis economy'. The prime tasks involved were the receipt, taxation, storage, and transport of goods and their redistribution to gods, members of the nobility, officials, travellers on the royal roads, workers and livestock." [432]

♠ animal sacrifice ♣ present ♥ "The case against the purported bloodlessness of lan offerings is even clearer: NN 2295:5-8 provides an explicit case of sheep/goats being allocated for lan offerings (so already Razmjou 2004a: 106). The total number of livestock for lan (148) mentioned in this journal are the equivalent of at least 14,800 qts. of grain [...] Seen from this perspective, sheep/goat allocations take up more than half of the total volume of commodities reserved for lan (!). The idea of a bloodless lan offering should therefore be given up." [435]

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ sip ♥ "Allocations for sip include flour, barley, wine, sheep/goats, poultry, and cattle. The cumulative value of these allocations is among the highest for religious purposes recorded in the Fortification archive (S5). In fact, the sip texts explicitly state that large amounts were consumed, presumably by substantial groups of people; in one case, the human beneficiaries are identified as royal muleteers (S6.3.3). The occurrence of a kurdabattis, 'chief of workers', in several texts confirms that groups of laborers, possibly consisting of up to 520 individuals, may have attended the feast. Apart from its social and ideological functions, sip also had an economic side to it (cf. S4 on lan), namely, the distribution of meat rations to laborers. "Dated texts on sip indicate that the feast was celebrated in November/December, that there was no fixed date, and that sip could be celebrated more than once during the same season. Practical reasons for such an autumnal feast involving a high amount of animal sacrifices may have been the reduction of (internal) herds owing to the scarcity of fodder during the winter season, the occurrence of the autumnal rutting season in October, and the occurrence of the first lambing season in November (S6.3.4). The official reason, on the other hand, may have been the presence of the king in Fars during the Fall season, as is suggested (however elusively) by Greek and Elamite sources. His presence at the time of at least some sip celebrations may be assumed (S6.4.1). "The royal connection is expressed not only by the timing of sip but also through the pronounced involvement of Parnakka and Zissawis in its performance and organization (paralleled only in the sumar and basur offerings, which are also directly connected to the crown [S6.2]). Since Xerxes himself claims to have performed sip (XPh), it seems reasonable to suggest that Parnakka and Zissawis acted as the king’s representatives when they presided over the feast (S6.4.2). In addition, sip is mostly celebrated at places with a marked royal character: Appistapdan, Tikranus (large plantations; royal banquets), and Pasargadae (plantation; palaces of Cyrus; coronation ceremony). Finally, an analysis of Fortification texts on poultry, cattle, and sheep/ goats suggests that animals issued for sacrificial purposes always had a specific royal "earmark" that would be understood by the people consuming these gifts from the king. Such royal food tagging is confirmed by the Greek sources (S6.4.2). "As Herodotus reports, the Persian king organized a feast called tukta, which seems to have served as a forum for the acquittal of "debts," i.e., the rewarding of services rendered to the king (S6.4.3). Though perhaps not the same feast, sip may have had similar characteristics; the participation of royal muleteers, possibly responsible for the movements of the court, in a sip ceremony would suggest so. The feast could therefore be seen as an element in a refined system of royal gift-giving in Fars as attested in Elamite and Greek sources. The common factor in all these royal gifts is that they underlined the king's position as the greatest giver." [452]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ "Dated texts on sip indicate that the feast was celebrated in November/December, that there was no fixed date, and that sip could be celebrated more than once during the same season. Practical reasons for such an autumnal feast involving a high amount of animal sacrifices may have been the reduction of (internal) herds owing to the scarcity of fodder during the winter season, the occurrence of the autumnal rutting season in October, and the occurrence of the first lambing season in November (S6.3.4). The official reason, on the other hand, may have been the presence of the king in Fars during the Fall season, as is suggested (however elusively) by Greek and Elamite sources. His presence at the time of at least some sip celebrations may be assumed (S6.4.1)." [453]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ "Dated texts on sip indicate that the feast was celebrated in November/December, that there was no fixed date, and that sip could be celebrated more than once during the same season. The official reason, on the other hand, may have been the presence of the king in Fars during the Fall season, as is suggested (however elusively) by Greek and Elamite sources. His presence at the time of at least some sip celebrations may be assumed (S6.4.1)." [454]

Euphoric elements
♠ feasting ♣ present ♥ "Allocations for sip include flour, barley, wine, sheep/goats, poultry, and cattle. The cumulative value of these allocations is among the highest for religious purposes recorded in the Fortification archive (S5). In fact, the sip texts explicitly state that large amounts were consumed, presumably by substantial groups of people; in one case, the human beneficiaries are identified as royal muleteers (S6.3.3). The occurrence of a kurdabattis, 'chief of workers', in several texts confirms that groups of laborers, possibly consisting of up to 520 individuals, may have attended the feast. Apart from its social and ideological functions, sip also had an economic side to it (cf. S4 on lan), namely, the distribution of meat rations to laborers." [469]
♠ alcohol ♣ present ♥ "Sacrificial commodities" consumed at sacrificial feasts in Elam normally included wine and beer [470].
♠ euphoria-inducing drugs (other than alcohol) ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ sex ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ dancing ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ synchronous movement ♣ suspected unknown ♥ (e.g. dancing or marching in unison)
♠ singing ♣ present ♥ "Traditional hymns played an important role in Persian religious experience (references in Briant 2002: 245, 330), as did music, and, probably, song in Kul-e Farah." [471]
♠ entertainment ♣ present ♥ "Traditional hymns played an important role in Persian religious experience (references in Briant 2002: 245, 330), as did music, and, probably, song in Kul-e Farah." [472]

Cohesion
♠ honour code ♣ present ♥ "That which is celebrated is the community that convenes and reconstitutes itself. Group identity, social hierarchy, and bonds of loyalty are reconfirmed in the sacrifice and the ensuing banquet. This is of special importance in a situation whereby ruler and subjects are separated from each other during large parts of the year. [...] Likewise, sip was a reunion: it seems to have been an occasion at which the king’s presence in Fars was made tangible by the gift of extraordinary rations, by the special location of the feast at royal plantations and at the site of ancient Pasargadae, and by his personal attendance (XPh) or that of his highest representative." [473]

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ mourning of the king ♥ Though mourning is prohibited by Zoroastrian sacred texts, sources contemporary to the Achaemenids suggest that it was allowed at the death of the king. People would shave their heads and wear special, possibly ragged, clothing; the royal fires (a particular type of sacred fires closely associated with the king) were extinguished; and, perhaps most notably, "when a king among them has died, they have a (period of) lawlessness for the following five days, not for the sake of feeling grief, but in order to learn through this matter how bad lawlessness is, because it brings about killings and robberies and whatever is worse, so that they will become more faithful guardians of the kings" [483].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ Every time a king died [484], which, based on lists on Achaemenid kings (e.g. [485]), happened thirteen times in more than two-hundred years.
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ Every time a king died [486], which, based on lists on Achaemenid kings (e.g. [487]), happened thirteen times in more than two-hundred years.
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ Every time a king died [488], which, based on lists on Achaemenid kings (e.g. [489]), happened thirteen times in more than two-hundred years.
♠ Duration ♣ 120 ♥ hours five days The death of the king was followed by five days of lawlessness [490].
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ [491]
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ whole polity ♥ Including the Greek writers that witnessed these events [492].

Phase II Variables (polity-based)[edit]

Institutional Variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Jenny Reddish; Jill Levine; Edward A L Turner ♥ These codes were developed at Seshat General Workshop in Oxford 2017


♠ Expert ♣ ♥

♠ Editor ♣ Daniel Mullins ♥

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent ♥ This is a complicated variable. Shahbazi notes that 'Each province was administered by a civilian governor, the satrap ... who was aided by a military commander and treasurer. There was thus a division of powers in each satrapy, aimed at preventing concentration of power in one office with the potential for rebellion'.[526] Frye claims that 'We cannot clearly discern whether there was a bipartite or even a tripartite division of authority in the Achaemenid Empire', but his discussion following this statement suggests a blurring between military and executive authority both at the level of the king's court and administration of the satrapies. 'The three parties wielding power were the court, the bureaucracy (perhaps including priests) and the army. Inasmuch as the chiliarch combined military and administrative functions, as did the satraps, we may assume such a mixture in other high offices'.[527] The duties of the office of 'chiliarch' (a Greek term) or 'hazarapatish' (Old Persian) are themselves suggestive of a lack of separation of executive and military power. The chiliarch was a court official who served as the 'master of royal audiences', controlling access to the king and bringing him messages, as well as being commander of the King's bodyguard of a thousand men.[528] (However, see Dandamaev and Lukonin 1989 for disagreements about the role of the chiliarch and the nature of the military force he commanded).[529] Dandamaev and Lukonin also explain how during the fifth and fourth centuries bce, 'the suppression of uprisings by conquered peoples was the duty of the satrap. Thus, the satraps in the fourth century had troops at their disposal'.[530] On balance, it seems that though there was some movement towards separating the functions of military and executive authority under the Achaemenids, we cannot speak of any real 'independence', at least not at the level of the chief executive.
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ absent ♥ While the Achaemenids 'preserved the past legal heritage in the countries they conquered, especially in Babylonia' (see Legal System section, below), they also introduced their own laws, which (theoretically) came directly from the king rather than from any independent legislative body. 'There was a dual system of laws in the empire, the "king's law" applicable everywhere, and local laws which were codified by order of the king'.[531] Shahbazi says that the Persian king's 'words had the force of law'.[532] Magdalene claims that 'All legal authority ultimately derived from the gods, who entrusted it to the king. The king, therefore, established, maintained, and defended justice (Otto, p. 268). He was not above the law but was, rather, an integral part of it. No evidence for a legislative body exists.'[533] King ruled law by decree but 'bound by tradition to respect the views of elders and consult great nobles on important occasions; he had to marry from the great noble's families; he could not pass arbitrary judgement; and there was always the fear of assassination if he went too far in autocracy."[534]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ absent ♥ Schmitt tells us that the Persian king was a 'sovereign governor, who united in his person all power as supreme lord and judge in peace and warfare'. More specifically, he says that 'Judicial authority was in the hand of the king and special "royal judges" (Herodotus 3.31.2-3), who were chosen from the Persians and appointed by the king for life; the principle of the king's absolute and unlimited autocracy was thus maintained.'[535] Similarly, we read in an article by Magdalene that 'Judicial administration was ultimately under the authority of the king, and texts document his supervisory role ... although the Achaemenid kings rarely adjudicated individual cases.'[536]

Legal (formal) freedoms[edit]

♠ Right to petition executive ♣ absent ♥ A widespread motif in Achaemenid iconography was the 'audience scene', showing the king on his throne, attended by servants or officials and greeted by a visitor making a 'formal gesture of respect'.[537] A prominent figure in the scene probably represents the chiliarch (head of the king's personal guard), who served as a powerful gatekeeper. 'He was the one who received the petitions of all those who sought an audience with the king: "Without him, none could gain audience" ... He was the one "who took messages in to the king and presented petitioners" ... or passing on their messages to the king if they were not admitted to his presence'.[538] However, I do not think we can characterize this as a 'right' (in a legal sense) to petition the king, and it seems that direct requests would not have been well received. Kuhrt, commenting on how royal favour could be gained through loyal service, bravery in battle, and so on, notes: 'However great a service an individual performed, asking for a favour in return or finding fault with a reward met with swift punishment'.[539] Cook says that for the kings who came after Cyrus the Great (who was reputedly 'approachable' and 'father-like'), 'The pinnacle of power was a lonely perch. The King lived largely in seclusion; he is said by Xenophon to have prided himself on being inaccessible'.[540]
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ absent ♥ It seems unlikely that there was a legal right for government officials to criticize the king, given that there was no independent legislative body and 'Judicial administration was ultimately under the authority of the king'.[541] Cook comments that since the king 'was the fountainhead of justice it was axiomatic that he could do no wrong'.[542] Llewellyn-Jones also stresses the strict protocols that had to be observed in audiences with the king: 'courtiers and visitors ... had to be conversant with palace protocol to ensure that they behaved with dignified decorum and observed preordained rules in the presence of the monarch'.[543]
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ absent ♥ Cook tells us that since the king 'was the fountainhead of justice it was axiomatic that he could do no wrong':[544] in this context, it seems unlikely that there existed legal rights for agents outside the government to criticise his management of the empire. The absence of these formal rights may also be suggested by a narrative related by Ctesias and preserved by Plutarch, which (if true) implies that criticism of the king could have very serious consequences. The accounts of the Persian soldier Mithridiates and a lowborn Carian soldier of the death in battle of Cyrus the Younger, rebellious brother of Artaxerxes II, contradicted that of Artaxerxes. Ctesias claimed that both Mithdridiates and the Carian were tortured to death for this reason.[545] Lincoln comments: 'Mithridiates was, apparently, charged with having exaggerated his role in Cyrus's death and with slandering the king, whose veracity he thereby implicitly impugned.' The latter offence was 'particularly grievous as it struck directly at the sources of royal legitimacy and social order ... if the king was a liar, the empire ... stood on shifting sand'.[546]

Power distributed[edit]

♠ Sanction of executive by government ♣ absent ♥ Cook comments that since the king 'was the fountainhead of justice it was axiomatic that he could do no wrong':[547] surely for government officials to (for example) impose a fine on the king would have been in direct contradiction of this infallible status. He 'was not supposed to revoke an order, still less go back on a promise that he had given'.[548] and his 'need to protect his own rights made him inflexible against any attempt to question his authority'.[549] Another indication of the likely absence of governmental sanctions of the king is the lack of an independent legislature or judiciary.
♠ Sanction of executive by non-government ♣ absent ♥ The king was an 'incarnation of sovereign majesty',[550] the 'fountainhead of justice', and 'it was axiomatic that he could do no wrong'.[551] This widely attested royal status seems incompatible with a situation in which elite groups, economic groups or other agents outside the government would have the legal right to issue sanctions.
♠ Constraint on executive by government ♣ absent ♥ 'Darius and several of his successors say that Ahura Mazda "made them kings, the one the king of many, the one the lord of many" (cf. Aeschylus, Persae 762f.). This means, at the same time, that the Persian king was no primus inter pares but rather a sovereign governor, who united in his person all power as supreme lord and judge in peace and warfare and therefore stood far above his subjects ... The king was above the law and allowed to do whatever he liked (Herodotus 3.31.4).'[552] A passage from Llewellyn-Jones' recent book also strongly suggests a lack of constraints on the Achaemenid king: 'European Christian monarchy was counter-balanced by political groups from among the social classes and castes of the realm, whereas the absolute tribute-gathering rulers of the east tended to govern with greater independent autocracy as kings fused their political rule with their integral religious identity, so that they were not answerable to a clergy or an independent parliament.'[553]
♠ Constraint on executive by non-government ♣ absent ♥ 'Darius and several of his successors say that Ahura Mazda "made them kings, the one the king of many, the one the lord of many" (cf. Aeschylus, Persae 762f.). This means, at the same time, that the Persian king was no primus inter pares but rather a sovereign governor, who united in his person all power as supreme lord and judge in peace and warfare and therefore stood far above his subjects ... The king was above the law and allowed to do whatever he liked (Herodotus 3.31.4).'[554]
♠ Impeachment ♣ absent ♥ I have found no evidence of a legal mechanism for removing Achaemenid kings from the throne. Instead, assassination was the fate of kings who failed to hold onto power, as in the cases of Bardiya, Xerxes I, Xerxes II, Sogdianus, Artaxerxes III, Artaxerxes IV, and Darius III.[555]

Customary (informal) checks on executive[edit]

♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ inferred present ♥ We have 'no word of any [institutionalised] council of state', but 'Herodotus tells us of three occasions when a King summoned the most eminent Persians in his train or at his court to sit in council with him'.[556] Perhaps on these extraordinary occasions, Persian nobles could critique the king's policies. Herodotus also states that Xerxes I's ally Artemisia, queen of Halicarnassus who acted as naval commander in the expedition against Greece,[557] gave frank (even borderline insulting) advice to the king at a war council.[558] He reports her urging Xerxes to 'Spare your ships and do not fight at sea, for the Greeks are as far superior to your men in naval matters as men are to women'.[559] However, we are on very shaky ground here: I am not sure that we can extrapolate from these classical reports to broader speculation on the practice of criticising the Achaemenid kings. King was ruled law by decree but "bound by tradition to respect the views of elders and consult great nobles on important occasions; he had to marry from the great noble's families; he could not pass arbitrary judgement; and there was always the fear of assassination if he went too far in autocracy."[560]
♠ Informal sanction of executive ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no evidence of informal sanctions which could be applied to the king, even in Briant's extensive discussion of the 'relationships between the king and those who served him and held the highest positions in the empire'.[561]
♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ present ♥ Although the Achaemenid king was in principle 'above the law',[562] in practice 'several factors moderated this absolutism. He was bound by tradition to respect the views of elders and consult great nobles on important occasions; he had to marry from the great nobles' families; he could not pass arbitrary judgment; and there was always the fear of assassination if he went too far in autocracy'. [563] Frye also mentions 'The eunuchs, harem ladies, princesses' who he thinks 'made of the court a large collection of people using their influence on the ruler'.[564]
♠ Informal impeachment ♣ absent ♥ Not one of the Achaemenid kings was persuaded to step down from the throne by non-violent means (see paragraph for 'Number of power transitions'). Instead, assassination was the fate of kings who failed to hold onto power, as in the cases of Bardiya, Xerxes I, Xerxes II, Sogdianus, Artaxerxes III, Artaxerxes IV, and Darius III.[565]

Limits on executive power imposed by religious agents[edit]

♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ absent ♥ A limited role for the Achaemenid-period Magi in critiquing royal decisions is suggested by Cook: 'It would seem that as clergy the Magi were subject to their temporal masters in the period that concerns us and not to high priests ... Probably, then, some Magi were put in positions of trust; but there is no reason to suppose that they were able to assert themselves as a corporate body, and we do not hear of them inflaming the passions of the public like Hebrew prophets or Islamic mollahs.'[566] ET: what about the role of non-Persian religions? Seems significant.. A Babylonian document called the 'Cyrus Cylinder' records that believers sought Cyrus as their righteous ruler. The Bible, through the words of prophet Isaish, also suggests that the Cult of Marduk in Babylonia 'praised the coming of Cyrus not as an ordinary conqueror but as a messiah a deliverer anointed by God.'[567]
♠ Sanction of executive by religious agents ♣ absent ♥ A passage from Llewellyn-Jones' recent book on the Achaemenid court suggests that religious agents had no power over the king's actions: 'European Christian monarchy was counter-balanced by political groups from among the social classes and castes of the realm, whereas the absolute tribute-gathering rulers of the east tended to govern with greater independent autocracy as kings fused their political rule with their integral religious identity, so that they were not answerable to a clergy or an independent parliament.'[568] Finer states that government was a matter for priests as well as kings and bureaucrats in the older Assyrian and Egyptian polities, but agrees with Llewellyn-Jones that 'the political role of the Persian clerisy was negligible'.[569]
♠ Constraint on executive by religious agents ♣ absent ♥ A passage from Llewellyn-Jones' recent book on the Achaemenid court suggests that religious agents had no power over the king's actions: 'European Christian monarchy was counter-balanced by political groups from among the social classes and castes of the realm, whereas the absolute tribute-gathering rulers of the east tended to govern with greater independent autocracy as kings fused their political rule with their integral religious identity, so that they were not answerable to a clergy or an independent parliament.'[570]
♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ inferred present ♥ There may have been some degree of constraint on the king, at least in principle, by religious institutions. Achaemenid kingship shared with the more ancient Near Eastern traditions the idea that 'earthly kingship was vested in the gods so that the men who ruled on earth did so as mediators and intercessors of a divine agency.'[571] This subordination was reinforced through rituals of humiliation in the investiture ceremony,[572] and afterwards 'the king was expected, under the auspices of the Magi, to pray and carry out rituals in Ahuramazda's honour, or to tend to the god's sacred fire'.[573] These aspects of sacral kingship show that the Achaemenid king had certain religious duties which he could not neglect. Indeed, if we believe pro-Persian cuneiform texts like the Cyrus Cylinder and the Nabonidus Chronicle, the dissatisfaction of the people of Babylon with King Nabonidus stemmed from his lack of devotion to Marduk, the patron god of the city (he was instead a 'devotee of the god Sin of Harran').[574][575] According to this narrative, the anger of the priests of Marduk at Nabonidus' 'outright heretical' behaviour helped pave the way for Cyrus the Great's conquest of Babylon.[576] Nevertheless, it is difficult to find concrete examples of religious institutions acting as a real constraint on the Achaemenid king's political action.
♠ Impeachment by religious agents ♣ absent ♥ I have found no evidence of a legal mechanism for removing Achaemenid kings from the throne. Instead, assassination was the fate of kings who failed to hold onto power, as in the cases of Bardiya, Xerxes I, Xerxes II, Sogdianus, Artaxerxes III, Artaxerxes IV, and Darius III.[577] In addition, the Zoroastrian priests (Magi) were 'subject to their temporal masters' in the Achaemenid period,[578] suggesting a lack of power to remove the king from the throne.
♠ Subordination of the chief executive to a religious agent ♣ absent ♥ The Magi played a vital role in the Achaemenid investiture ceremony: 'at the investiture, in the presence of the Magi, and through their agency, a prince was transformed from heir designate to Great King and symbolically took on a new "body"'.[579] However, his status - as the elect of Ahura Mazda, or 'guardian of Ahuramazda's creation'[580] - was certainly greater than that of even the most distinguished of priests. Cook confirms this, saying: 'It would seem that as clergy the Magi were subject to their temporal masters in the period that concerns us and not to high priests'.[581]
♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ present ♥ Xenophon provides evidence for the dedication of war booty by Cyrus the Great to 'the gods': 'Cyrus called his friends and the commanders of the army together. He appointed some of them to take the treasures in charge, and ordered others first to select from the goods handed over by Croesus an amount for the gods, as the magi might stipulate'.[582] The Zoroastrianism practised by the Achaemenid kings was a local form which Skjaervo describes as essentially 'polytheistic ... but with one god, Ahura Mazda, outranking the others.'[583] This may go some way towards explaining why they frequently made gifts of money and offers of support to the priests and temples of other faiths. For instance, after Cyrus the Great conquered Babylon, he restored Esagila, the temple of Marduk, and the Cyrus Cylinder records him acknowledging the support of this Mesopotamian god.[584] Shortly after Darius the Great came to power in 522 bce, he 'commanded that funds for the rebuilding [of the Jewish temple in Jerusalem] should be provided out of the tribute of the satrapy, and that sacrificial animals, corn, wine and whatever else was necessary should be given to the priests in Jerusalem, so that they might offer sacrifices there to their God, "and pray for the life of the king, and of his sons".'[585] However, there were good political reasons for this (Palestine occupying a strategic position on the road from Persia to Egypt),[586] so how far Darius was motivated by belief in any supernatural agent is a matter of debate.

Mechanisms of Power Transfer (from one head of state to the next)[edit]

♠ Customary mechanism ♣ present ♥ Dynastic succession from father to first-born son (male primogeniture) was the usual mechanism, although Frye asserts that this was not a 'iron-clad rule' for the Achaemenids: instead, 'the ruler designated whomsoever of his sons he wanted to succeed him.'[587] In any case, there were several exceptions to the rule over the course of the empire's history. 'Kingship in the Persian empire seemingly was hereditary within the Achaemenid dynasty. If descent by Achaemenid blood was an essential foundation of the Achaemenids' kingship, it is no wonder that the kings reiterated the last element of their title, Haxamanisiya, "an Achaemenid." The succession seems to have been settled by the king's designation (Herodotus 7.2.1). Usually the heir was the first-born son, and only by way of exception (cf. Plutarch, Artaxerxes 2.4) the son born first after accession' ... 'The king's election by relatives of the most distinguished Persian families (as in the case of Darius, when succession was not settled automatically) was apparently not the rule'.[588]
♠ Legal mechanism ♣ inferred absent ♥ It is unclear whether the Achaemenid court had formal 'rules for the transmission of power', and Briant points to the chaos surrounding Bardiya/Gaumata and the accession of Darius as illustrative of 'the difficulty of transferring royal power, even when the successor of the king (Cyrus) had been chosen during his lifetime'.[589]
♠ Number of power transitions ♣ 13 ♥ The Persian Empire: Archaemenid Dynasty[590]: 1. Cyrus the Great (4th in descent from king Achaemenes, 559-530 CE); 2. Cambyses (son 529-533 CE); 3. Smerdis (or Bardiya, brother 522 CE); 4. Darius I (5th in descent from king Achaemenes, 521-486 CE); 5. Xerxes I (son 485-465 CE); 6. Artaxerzes I (Longimanus, son 464-424 CE); 7. ***Xerxes II (son 424 CE); 8. ***Sogdianus (brother, 424 CE); 9. Darius II (Nothus, brother 423-405 CE); 10. Artaxerxes II (Mnemon, son 404-359 CE); 11. Artaxerxes III (Ochus, son 358-338 CE); 12. Arses (son, 337-336 CE); 13. Darius III (Codomannus, great-grandson of Darius II, 335-330 CE). Note: Shayegan suggests that 'Bardiya/Gaumata' refers to two different rulers.[591] Bardiya or 'false Bardiya' (Gaumata) (r. 522 bce).[592] Shayegan suggests that 'Bardiya' and 'Gaumata' may have in fact been two historical individuals, 'later transmuted into the presence of a single ruler, unfit to rule'.[593] *** These kings were not recognized as legitimate by the Babylonian scribes.[594]
♠ Number of contested transitions ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Is enough known from this time period that we can be certain we know of all the unsuccessful contested transitions? Nb: detail has been moved to Phase I variables.
♠ Number of successful overturns ♣ 3 ♥ *424* Xerxes I: Sogdianus, assassinated Xerxes while he was drunk, after a reign of just 45 days, and seized the throne.[595][596] *423* Sogdianus: Another illegitimate son of Artaxerxes I, Ochus, challenged Sogdianus' accession and soon managed to wrest the throne from him in 423 bce.[597] He took the throne name Darius II.[598] *336* Arses: To follow the classical narrative, after installing Artaxerxes IV (Arses) on the throne, Bagoas the eunuch then also poisoned him and his children 'and chose Codoman/Artasata to be king (Darius III)'.[599]

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥ Llewellyn-Jones sketches out a two-layered image of the Achaemenid royal court, with an 'inner court' consisting of the king, his close family and harem, very high-ranking nobles and personal slaves, and an 'outer court' populated by 'Bureaucrats and administrators, ambassadors, eunuchs, and physicians'.[600] He also says that 'Courtiers were also bureaucrats. As [Norbert] Elias ... noted, the royal court was "both the first household of the extended royal family, and the central organ of the entire state administration", and there can be no doubt that the Achaemenids revelled in administrative red tape (a love affair they shared with their Assyrian and Elamite forbears).'[601]

"Prior to the Parthians, political systems in Southwest Asia were for the most part relatively loose confederations in which central government ruled their 'empires' through unstable alliances with vassals and satraps. Even Hammurabi, Darius, and Alexander were only temporarily successful in linking their centralized governments to local administrative institutions, particularly outside of the core areas of Greater Mesopotamia."[602]

♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ lower ♥ Llewellyn-Jones' image of the inner and outer royal courts of the Achaemenid Empire places 'nobles from the highest-ranking families of the realm' in the inner court, close to the king, but bureaucrats were apparently relegated to the outer court.[603] We also find in Frye that the 'nobility held the real power in the empire, but the bureaucracy was also important, especially in regard to financial matters'.[604] This may have started to shift over time: 'As the structure of the empire became more bureaucratic and less feudal, the Persian nobility was no doubt beginning to lose its dominant position'.[605]

Authority over state-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ present ♥ The king could clearly choose to appoint whomever he pleased to important offices in the administration: 'royal gifts could include appointment to high office'.[606] Public acceptance of the king's gifts 'served to stress the loyalty of the recipient and his dependence on the king for all social advancement'.[607] The Persepolis Treasury Tablets also show that Baradkama, a high treasurer at Persepolis, 'On several occasions ... received orders directly from Darius [I]'.[608] Satraps appointed by king. The 'Medizing' Greek Themistocles left Greece after Greek-Persian war c479 BCE to become a Persian satrap.[609]
♠ Indirect ♣ absent ♥ I think the control exerted by the executive over important state-level administrators could best be described as 'direct'.
♠ Diffused ♣ absent ♥ I think the control exerted by the executive over important state-level administrators could best be described as 'direct'.
♠ Mixed ♣ absent ♥ I think the control exerted by the executive over important state-level administrators could best be described as 'direct'.
♠ Governmental control ♣ inferred absent ♥ The king could clearly choose to appoint whomever he pleased to important offices in the administration: 'royal gifts could include appointment to high office'.[610] We may also note that there was no judiciary, legislative or regulatory body within the government that operated independently of royal power (see institutional variables, above) and could have removed state-level bureaucrats from their positions.
♠ Non-governmental oversight ♣ inferred absent ♥ The king could clearly choose to appoint whomever he pleased to important offices in the administration: 'royal gifts could include appointment to high office'.[611] It seems unlikely that religious groups would have the power to remove bureaucrats from their positions or veto their appointment: the political role of the Persian clergy was negligible.[612]

Source of Support of state-level officials

♠ State salary ♣ present ♥ 'Workmen in the employ of the royal sector, and even the highest officials, were paid their salaries in unminted silver and products in kind [rather than in coins]. Such a practice is also attested to in documents from Babylonia and Egypt of the Achaemenid period.'[613]
♠ Governed population ♣ ♥
♠ Land ♣ ♥
♠ None ♣ absent ♥ Officials received a state salary and food rations.[614]
♠ Other ♣ ♥

Pathologies

♠ Corruption ♣ inferred present ♥ The Persepolis Fortification Tablets provide evidence of state-level administrators engaging in what we might call embezzlement: 'There are accusations(?) of "appropriating for themselves" this or that merchandise'.[615] Royal judges, who were appointed and dismissed 'on the authority of the king' and had no real independence, could be put to death for corruption. 'On several occasions, judges were condemned to death for handing down iniquitous judgments, particularly for having sold out for money'.[616] However, I have not found any estimates of the proportion of officials who engaged in corrupt practices.
♠ Bribery ♣ present ♥ Royal judges could be put to death for corruption, including taking bribes in exchange for favourable judgements. 'On several occasions, judges were condemned to death for handing down iniquitous judgments, particularly for having sold out for money'.[617] However, I have not found any estimates of the proportion of officials who took bribes.
♠ Abuse of power ♣ ♥

♠ Professionalization of state-level bureaucracy ♣ present ♥ The Achaemenids had a 'centralized, professionalized, and powerful bureaucracy'.[618]

♠ modal number of years state-level bureaucrats spend in service ♣ ♥

♠ Promotion of state-level officials to other positions ♣ present ♥ The Persepolis Fortification Tablets allow us to follow the career of Maraza, a bureaucrat at Persepolis. 'After carrying out major responsibilities in the wine department between 504 and part of 498, and therefore figuring among Parnaka's staff ... Maraza was then reassigned to the grain department'.[619] There are also examples of officials being transferred from the central bureaucracy to the provinces. This was the case for Ezra, who had 'served as a scribe in the central administrative apparatus in Susa' but in 458 bce was appointed deputy of Judah. 'In 445 B.C. he was replaced by the Jew Nehemiah, who likewise had been in Susa before this'.[620]

♠ Frequency of promotion of state-level officials to other positions ♣ ♥

♠ Promotion from lower ranks ♣ present ♥ Briant discusses the case of Baradkama, a bureaucrat known from the Persepolis Fortification Tablets. From 495 to 494 bce, he was working as treasurer of Urandus and Shiraz, but in 490 he took the job of head treasurer, at Persepolis. 'Obviously, between 494 and 490, he profited from a major promotion that had brought him to the Persepolis treasury'.[621]

♠ Frequency of promotion of state-level officials from lower ranks ♣ ♥ Promotion seems likely to have been infrequent: Briant uses the Persepolis Fortification Tablets to make inferences about the 'complexity and rigidity of the Persepolis administration's chain of command' and its hierarchical nature.[622]

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ [20-23]: 519 bce ♥ Darius I (the Great) was 'the first systematically to divide up the empire into provinces and put a satrap in charge of them, henceforward called the satrapies. Herodotus lists twenty by name and description'. There has been some disagreement about whether these were in fact 'taxation-districts', but Finer tells us that recent scholarship generally agrees with this number, 'though some of these were subdivided during the later empire'.[623] Darius' Behistun inscription lists 23 instead of Herodotus' 20 provinces.[624] Frye says: 'Generally speaking, one may say that the tendency was to reduce the size of the satrapies, so that there were more at the end of the reign of Darius than at the beginning and still more at the end of the empire than under Darius'.[625]

♠ Local-level administrators ♣ present ♥ Dandamayev and Lukonin discuss 'local officials' in the Persian satrapies.[626] Persian notables across the empire 'gathered at the satrap's court, which was served by a chancellery of Aramaic-speaking scribes'.[627]

"Prior to the Parthians, political systems in Southwest Asia were for the most part relatively loose confederations in which central government ruled their 'empires' through unstable alliances with vassals and satraps. Even Hammurabi, Darius, and Alexander were only temporarily successful in linking their centralized governments to local administrative institutions, particularly outside of the core areas of Greater Mesopotamia."[628]

Authority over local-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ present: 519-330 bce ♥ See explanation for 'Mixed'. "Prior to the Parthians, political systems in Southwest Asia were for the most part relatively loose confederations in which central government ruled their 'empires' through unstable alliances with vassals and satraps. Even Hammurabi, Darius, and Alexander were only temporarily successful in linking their centralized governments to local administrative institutions, particularly outside of the core areas of Greater Mesopotamia."[629]
♠ Indirect ♣ present: 519-330 bce ♥ See explanation for 'Mixed'. "Prior to the Parthians, political systems in Southwest Asia were for the most part relatively loose confederations in which central government ruled their 'empires' through unstable alliances with vassals and satraps. Even Hammurabi, Darius, and Alexander were only temporarily successful in linking their centralized governments to local administrative institutions, particularly outside of the core areas of Greater Mesopotamia."[630]
♠ Diffused ♣ present: 550-519 bce ♥ "Prior to the Parthians, political systems in Southwest Asia were for the most part relatively loose confederations in which central government ruled their 'empires' through unstable alliances with vassals and satraps. Even Hammurabi, Darius, and Alexander were only temporarily successful in linking their centralized governments to local administrative institutions, particularly outside of the core areas of Greater Mesopotamia."[631] 'After conquering Media, Babylonia and Egypt, the Achaemenid kings attributed to their conquests the nature of a personal union with the peoples of these countries ... and used the traditional systems of dating and methods of administration that had developed historically'.[632] Cyrus the Great (reigned 560/59-530 bce) and Cambyses (reigned 530-522 bce),[633] 'retained with practically no alterations the local political structure within the vanquished countries, and offered the conquered peoples local self-government'.[634] Shahbazi also notes that under Cyrus and Cambyses, there was 'heavy reliance on non-Persian officials and institutions of subject states'.[635] However, the uprisings of 522-521 bce motivated Darius I (reigned 522-486 bce) to make wide-ranging administrative and financial reforms, beginning in 519 bce.[636][637] Darius's reforms 'facilitated the creation of a stable system of state administration and control over the conquered countries', though each province retained a high degree of socioeconomic independence until the end of Achaemenid rule.[638]
♠ Mixed ♣ present: 519-330 bce ♥ "Prior to the Parthians, political systems in Southwest Asia were for the most part relatively loose confederations in which central government ruled their 'empires' through unstable alliances with vassals and satraps. Even Hammurabi, Darius, and Alexander were only temporarily successful in linking their centralized governments to local administrative institutions, particularly outside of the core areas of Greater Mesopotamia."[639]After Darius’s administrative reforms, the control that the Persian king and officials responsible directly to him exerted over local officials can probably best be described as 'mixed', and varied in degree between different satrapies. On the one hand, 'The satraps and military chiefs were closely linked with the central administration and were under the constant control of the king and his civil servants. Continuous supervision over everyone in the center and in the provinces was carried out by the "ears" and "eyes" of the king, who were independent of the satraps and other local authorities. These agents were directly subordinate to the king and reported to him about any seditious speech or act' ... 'The secretary assigned to the satrap was also independent of the latter and was considered a royal scribe. He constituted the link between the central government and the chancellory of the region in question (Herod. III, 128)'.[640] Shahbazi also mentions the 'King's ears and eyes': 'the most trusted envoys of the sovereign' who had 'full authority to reward meritorious deeds and punish unlawful ones'.[641] On the other hand, Dandamaev and Lukonin also tell us about satrapies, such as Judah, 'which enjoyed autonomy in internal affairs', suggesting that royal control over local officials would not have been direct in all places at all times. 'This particularly pertains to the remote provinces, in whose internal affairs the Persian administration seldom intervened, governing them with the assistance of local princes and tribal chiefs and limiting themselves to receiving taxes. For instance, the satrap of Judah ... had his own governor in Samaria with the right of supervision over the deputy in Judah; gradually Judah began to enjoy great independence in domestic affairs. From 516 B.C. there was no Persian deputy in Judah.'[642] We should also consider the series of local uprisings and rebellions from the first half of the fifth century onwards (for instance in Egypt, Babylonia, Media and the provinces of Asia Minor), which weakened central control.[643]
♠ Governmental control ♣ inferred absent ♥ Control over satraps rested in the hands of the Persian kings, who according to Finer had 'undisputed title to appoint or remove' these local governors.[644] We may also note that there was no judiciary, legislative or regulatory body that operated independently of royal power (see institutional variables, above) and could have removed lesser local bureaucrats from their positions.
♠ Non-governmental oversight ♣ inferred absent ♥ Control over satraps rested in the hands of the Persian kings, who according to Finer had 'undisputed title to appoint or remove' these local governors.[645] It seems unlikely that religious groups would have the power to remove bureaucrats from their positions or veto their appointment: the political role of the Persian clergy was negligible.[646]

Source of Support of local-level officials

♠ State salary ♣ present ♥ Lewis argues for a well-developed bureaucratic system extending across the territory of the Achaemenid Empire. He strongly disagrees with the impression that the evidence from Babylonia of a 'fairly detailed system of ration allowances for those who worked for the state' represents 'a pure survival [from the Assyrian and Babylonian periods] in a heavily bureaucratised part of the empire'. On the contrary, 'The publication of the Persepolis Fortification Tablets by Professor Hallock has shown that the whole system went much further und [sic] that, even in Persia itself, virtually everyone, even the highest, was on a regular ration-scale'.[647]
♠ Governed population ♣ present ♥ If we count satraps as local officials, we can say that the governed population (as taxpayers) served as an additional source of support for local administration. 'The tribute for each satrapy represented only what each province had to pay the imperial treasury; the local satrap raised as much as he wanted and kept the surplus for his own local requirements'.[648]
♠ Land ♣ ♥
♠ None ♣ absent ♥ Officials received a state salary and food rations.[649]
♠ Other ♣ ♥

Pathologies

♠ Corruption ♣ ♥ In Ezra 4-6, the Jews claim that their adversaries in Judah have for many years taken advantage of corrupt officials to block the rebuilding of their temple: 'Then the people of the land discouraged the people of Judah and made them afraid to build and bribed counselors against them to frustrate their purpose, all the days of Cyrus king of Persia, even until the reign of Darius king of Persia.'[650]
♠ Bribery ♣ ♥ In Ezra 4-6, the Jews claim that their adversaries in Judah have for many years taken advantage of corrupt officials to block the rebuilding of their temple: 'Then the people of the land discouraged the people of Judah and made them afraid to build and bribed counselors against them to frustrate their purpose, all the days of Cyrus king of Persia, even until the reign of Darius king of Persia.'[651]
♠ Abuse of power ♣ ♥

♠ Professionalization of local bureaucracy ♣ present ♥ Satraps and local bureaucrats in the various provinces of the empire seem to have been full-time professionals in formal positions. Persian notables across the empire 'gathered at the satrap's court, which was served by a chancellery of Aramaic-speaking scribes'.[652] Gubaru, the Iranian governor of King Cyrus' merged province of Mesopotamia and 'Across-the-River' (Syria and Palestine), stayed in his post for 'at least ten years' and carried out a wide variety of administrative functions.[653]

♠ modal number of years local bureaucrats spend in service ♣ 7 ♥ This figure is by no means a robust estimate based on a large sample: I have so far only been able to find a discussion of the terms of office of governors in Neo-Babylonian and Achaemenid Mesopotamia. Dandamayev notes that Gubaru, the Iranian governor of King Cyrus' merged province of Mesopotamia and 'Across-the-River' (Syria and Palestine), stayed in his post for 'at least ten years'.[654] Two other known governors (who had a variety of administrative roles in Mesopotamia, Syria and Palestine) served for around 5 years and possibly over 28 years,[655] and 'some governors of Uruk held their posts about five years or more'.[656] 'In all probability, there was no limit on the governor's term of office.[657]

Legal System[edit]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ present ♥ This is a complex problem. Shahbazi is confident of the existence of multiple legal systems: 'By royal decree, local jurists codified the laws of their own nations, and imperial authorities then enforced those laws. Thus, the Jews worked according to Biblical laws with the royal sanctions, the Egyptians according to older Egyptian laws, the Iranians according to the Avestan injunctions and their local traditions' ... 'Darius [I] had Egyptian legal scholars codify their national law and enforced it in the name of the royal authority'.[658] We also read in Dandamaev and Lukonin that 'Extremely diverse legal systems and institutions, which ranged from the very primitive to the highly developed, existed in the Achaemenid state. The conquests of the Persians did not lead to dramatic changes in the legal traditions and norms of the vanquished peoples.'[659] However, Fried has examined legal documents from Achaemenid-era Egypt and Yehud and come to a different conclusion: 'The use of Persian loanwords in the Egyptian texts implies a completely Persian judicial system in the Egyptian satrapy, with judges, police, and intelligence officers all Iranian. The same would have been true throughout the empire, including the satrapy of Beyond the River and the province of Yehud.'[660] She also believes that 'The Persians may have authorized the Jews to collect and transcribe the customs and traditions of Yehud, as they did in Egypt. However, it was not intended that this collection be binding on the decisions of the Iranian judges that Ezra [the Jewish scribe and priest] appointed'.[661] Briant may provide a way through these contradictions: 'as soon as they are recognized by the Great King, local customs are included in the general category of "royal law"'.[662] When the sources tell us that 'royal law' was applied across the empire, then, this 'reflects politico-religious ideology, not judicial organization'; 'there was in the empire no law code imposed on every population without distinction'.[663]

♠ Courts are supported by the state ♣ present ♥ In the Persepolis Fortification Tablets, many of which record the payment of rations in barley, wine etc. from the central Achaemenid 'food administration' to workers of various kinds,[664], we have evidence of rations destined for 'bodies of administrators entrusted with judicial tasks'.[665] 'One tablet (PF 1272) reports the deliv­ery of wine rations to a certain Bakabada, called "judge (databara) of Parnaka"', who Briant thinks was an itinerant judge.[666] Arrangements may have been different for courts which stayed in one place, but this shows that the state administration saw itself as responsible for supporting at least some judicial institutions.

♠ Criminal Prosecution ♣ present ♥ Mehr says 'There is ample evidence that the administration of justice had reached juridical sophistication with vertically stratified courts - courts of first instance and appeal, services of defense lawyers, and well-defined procedural rules that guaranteed fair trials.'[667] However, Mehr's view may be slightly rose-tinted here, as the king could overturn judgements whenever he pleased: 'Quite often ... people were condemned by the king without previously being arraigned before a tribunal. This was certainly true for royal judges, who were con­demned by the king directly. He could also grant clemency to whomever he wished. On one occasion, we even see the king pronouncing a death sentence on an accused person who had previously been acquitted by the judges'.[668]

Accessibility of the Legal System

♠ ruling elite ♣ present ♥ 'Royal officials had standing to sue for the king.'[669] Frye mentions a term from Aramaic papyri from Egypt that seems to denote 'an official who represented the central government in legal cases, perhaps a kind of state's attorney'[670]
♠ non-ruling elite ♣ present ♥ 'Temples had standing to sue in the secular courts regarding their interests and were typically represented by senior temple officials.'[671]
♠ general population ♣ present ♥ 'Free men always had standing to sue. Heads of households would typically represent the legal interests of their household members. Women had standing in cases involving significant inner-family disputes, specifically their dowries, where their legal status or rights were at issue ... and, when independent, to protect their business interests just like men. Slaves and former-slaves had standing in regard to their status as slave or freeman. In criminal matters, all persons of whatever status could notify the court of alleged criminal activity, lodge an accusation, and testify in the matter.'[672] Dandamaev and Lukonin concur: 'there were apparently no differences in the ways in which the interests of slaves and freemen were defended. Slaves, just like freemen, gave testimonial evidence concerning crimes committed by other slaves and freemen, including their own masters'.[673]
♠ foreign groups ♣ present ♥ 'In criminal matters, all persons of whatever status could notify the court of alleged criminal activity, lodge an accusation, and testify in the matter.'[674] In the Mesopotamian city states at least, there was a long-established tradition of bestowing legal rights and privileges on anyone within the city boundaries. Kuhrt reproduces a letter from the Babylonians to the Neo-Assyrian king Esarhaddon, which asserts that 'Whoever enters inside it [Babylon], his privileged status ... is secured ... Its very name is set up for protection ... Not even a dog that enters inside it is killed'.[675] As Kuhrt puts it, 'even a dog in Babylon is free'. Despite conquering Babylon, Cyrus the Great was forced to respect its institutions and norms, participating in a ritual designed to assert 'the rights of the city':[676] it therefore seems likely that this tradition of equal treatment before the law within the walls of Babylon would have been retained. However, in a more general sense, foreigners probably had fewer legal rights than locals: 'The free people who were deprived of civil rights consisted of ... all foreigners who lived in different countries for whatever reason'.[677]
♠ ethnic groups ♣ present ♥ 'In criminal matters, all persons of whatever status could notify the court of alleged criminal activity, lodge an accusation, and testify in the matter.'[678] There is also the matter of the multiple legal traditions (e.g. Jewish and Egyptian) that co-existed with the laws imposed by the Achaemenid king and central administration:[679] this juridical pluralism would seem to reduce the chance that a subject of the empire would be deprived of legal rights on account of their ethnic background.

Accessibility of Trials

♠ ruling elite ♣ present ♥ 'Royal officials had standing to sue for the king.'[680]
♠ non-ruling elite ♣ present ♥ 'Temples had standing to sue in the secular courts regarding their interests and were typically represented by senior temple officials.'[681]
♠ general population ♣ present ♥ 'Free men always had standing to sue. Heads of households would typically represent the legal interests of their household members. Women had standing in cases involving significant inner-family disputes, specifically their dowries, where their legal status or rights were at issue ... and, when independent, to protect their business interests just like men. Slaves and former-slaves had standing in regard to their status as slave or freeman. In criminal matters, all persons of whatever status could notify the court of alleged criminal activity, lodge an accusation, and testify in the matter.'[682] Dandamaev and Lukonin concur: 'there were apparently no differences in the ways in which the interests of slaves and freemen were defended. Slaves, just like freemen, gave testimonial evidence concerning crimes committed by other slaves and freemen, including their own masters'.[683]
♠ foreign groups ♣ present ♥ 'In criminal matters, all persons of whatever status could notify the court of alleged criminal activity, lodge an accusation, and testify in the matter.'[684] In the Mesopotamian city states at least, there was a long-established tradition of bestowing legal rights and privileges on anyone within the city boundaries. Kuhrt reproduces a letter from the Babylonians to the Neo-Assyrian king Esarhaddon, which asserts that 'Whoever enters inside it [Babylon], his privileged status ... is secured ... Its very name is set up for protection ... Not even a dog that enters inside it is killed'.[685] As Kuhrt puts it, 'even a dog in Babylon is free'. Despite conquering Babylon, Cyrus the Great was forced to respect its institutions and norms, participating in a ritual designed to assert 'the rights of the city':[686] it therefore seems likely that this tradition of equal treatment before the law would have been retained.
♠ ethnic groups ♣ present ♥ 'In criminal matters, all persons of whatever status could notify the court of alleged criminal activity, lodge an accusation, and testify in the matter.'[687] There is also the matter of the multiple legal traditions (e.g. Jewish and Egyptian) that co-existed with the laws imposed by the Achaemenid king and central administration:[688] this juridical pluralism would seem to reduce the chance that a subject of the empire would be deprived of legal rights on account of their ethnic background.

Accessibility of Courts

♠ Courts in polity capital ♣ present ♥ 'During the Achaemenid occupation of Babylon ... local judges were replaced by Persians'.[689] Babylon served as the 'western capital' of the empire, 'on a par with Susa'[690] (until Xerxes I demoted it, provoking a revolt in 482 bce).[691]
♠ Courts in regional capitals ♣ present ♥ In a discussion of the legal system in Achaemenid Egypt, Dandamaev and Lukonin state that 'All the administrative districts of the country had their own judges. In some nomes the court was headed by a civil servant with the Iranian title of frataraka, who was also simultaneously the chief of the garrison.'[692] 'Judicial authorities related to the Persian administration are ... attested in Egypt.'[693]
♠ Courts in provincial towns ♣ present ♥ Dandamayev mentions a document from 535 bce in which the governor of Babylonia, Nabu-ahhe-bullit, sends three cousins who appealed to him for a legal decision 'to the governor of Uruk, and judges there for a verdict'.[694] Tribunals in this period appear not to be confined to the urban centres of Mesopotamia or the Persian capitals: an anecdote related by Aelian suggests that judges travelled around the provinces and heard cases where necessary. Rhakokes, whom Briant describes as a 'simple peasant',[695] accused his son of harming the Magi, and 'the Judges coming to the place where [Rhakokes] lived, he taking his Son, and binding his hands behind him, brought him before the Judges'.[696] Aelian also says that the man was Mardian;[697] Munson describes the Mardi as 'the most marginal Persian tribe'.[698] If there is some truth in this account, it implies that a marginal location in the Achaemenid Empire did not necessarily entail a lack of access to trials. We also have a fragmented collection of Aramaic papyri, including court records, from Saqqara in Egypt.[699][700]

♠ Typical speed of judicial proceedings in criminal cases ♣ unknown ♥ I have found no estimates of the average duration of a criminal case in the Achaemenid Empire. Wunsch notes that for Neo-Babylonian and Achaemenid litigation records on clay tablets - one of the best sources of evidence for court procedure in this period, at least in Meospotamia - the 'end of the tablet with the date formula' is sometimes broken off.[701]

♠ Typical speed of judicial proceedings in civil cases ♣ unknown ♥ I have found no estimates of the average duration of a civil case in the Achaemenid Empire. Wunsch notes that for Neo-Babylonian and Achaemenid litigation records on clay tablets - one of the best sources of evidence for court procedure in this period, at least in Meospotamia - the 'end of the tablet with the date formula' is sometimes broken off.[702]

Informal Justice[edit]

♠ Non-state judiciary ♣ present ♥ Tuplin sees as 'typical' of the Achaemenid approach to administration the 'continued functioning of the Elders and the "popular" assemblies of Babylonian city/temple communities, which had jurisdiction in various sorts of property dispute ... or the Jewish community assembly at Elephantine (... also a judicial body) or similar foreign community institutions in Babylonia ... and maybe even Persis'.[703]

♠ Religious judiciary ♣ absent ♥ Magdalene claims that there were two major divisions of courts 'with different jurisdictional powers' in the Achaemenid Empire: secular courts and temple courts.[704] However, her article makes it clear that temple courts had a more restricted jurisdiction than secular courts, and were ultimately under the supervision of secular officials rather than able to operate 'without involvement of the state', as specified by the code book. 'Temple courts heard cases where they had subject matter jurisdiction and either in personam or in rem jurisdiction. In such cases, at least one temple official and one royal official had to be on the court. Secular courts had broad power to hear cases, adjudicating all cases that the temple court could not hear. They had, additionally, appellate jurisdiction over temple court decisions'.[705] In Neo-Babylonian court procedure, which we know had many similarities with Achaemenid judicial practice, there was overlap between the jurisdictions of the Eanna temple and the 'royal judges': 'members of the Eanna administration are known to have also been royal judges'.[706] Fried believes that a 'completely Persian judicial system', with 'Iranian judges and magistrates' was installed in Egypt, Beyond the River and Yehud.[707] I have coded 'absent' because there does not appear to have been a religious judiciary that was truly independent of the state apparatus.

Property Rights[edit]

♠ Private property (land) ♣ present ♥ Both Frye and Finer characterize the Achaemenid system of land tenure as having feudal elements: Finer says that 'in principle [the king] owned all property', and Frye that 'in theory the king owned all of the land but the people occupied it in fief'.[708][709] 'Fiefs were granted usually to families, perhaps influenced by the old tradition of collective ownership in Mesopotamia'.[710] Van Driel also refers to the 'feudal system' of Achaemenid-period Babylonia.[711] Nevertheless, Dandamayev mentions several 'land-sale contracts' from Neo-Babylonian and Achaemenid Mesopotamia.[712] Stolper comments that in the Babylonian records related to the activities of a man called Belsunu, 'No distinctive label marks real estate as private property, but farmland was alienable and purchased land was among the assets of Belsunu and his subordinates'.[713] Furthermore, 'During Achaemenid times a woman in Babylonia (as well as in Elam and Egypt) enjoyed great independence and could have her own property, of which she was freely in charge (she could bestow it as a gift, sell it, exchange it, let it out for rent, etc.)'.[714] It seems to me that 'feudal' elements coexisted with a situation in which for all intents and purposes, individuals or groups could own property.

♠ Usufruct ♣ present ♥ “The lands and their dues were considered the property of that person, to do with as he wished, until the king decided to reclaim them and give them to another. Thus the king retained his absolute suzerainty over the land.” [715]
♠ Exclusivity ♣ inferred present ♥ “The lands and their dues were considered the property of that person, to do with as he wished, until the king decided to reclaim them and give them to another. Thus the king retained his absolute suzerainty over the land.” [716]
♠ Partibility ♣ present ♥ Neo-Babylonian and Achaemenid court records show that inherited property could be divided between multiple heirs. One such document analysed by Wunsch may refer to 'the virtual or physical division of an inherited field, an action that usually was triggered by co-owners intending to sell their individual shares.'[717] However, the usual situation for wealthy families in 6th- and 5th-century bce Babylonia was different: 'At the death of their father, his sons do not divide immediately their inheritance. Usually, the eldest takes over his father's assets and liabilities on behalf of them all and continues the business'.[718]
♠ Lease ♣ present ♥ Stolper describes a 'familiar' situation in Achaemenid-period documents from Dilbat and Kasr (in Mesopotamia): 'a royal land grant to an Iranian ... managed for him by a Babylonian ... was rented to a third party, and the rent was collected by the manager's son acting as his representative'.[719] The use of water from canals could also be leased: in a document from Sippar in Babylonia, a village head asks officials of the Ebabbara Temple to lease the tenants of temple fields 'water from a canal for five minas of silver per year'.[720] Temples were significant landowners and engaged in what Stolper has called 'rent-capitalism' in this period.[721]
♠ Lease Rights ♣ ♥
♠ Transfer of property ♣ present ♥ Neo-Babylonian and Achaemenid court records show that inherited property could be divided between multiple heirs. One such document analysed by Wunsch may refer to 'the virtual or physical division of an inherited field, an action that usually was triggered by co-owners intending to sell their individual shares.'[722] However, the usual situation for wealthy families in 6th- and 5th-century bce Babylonia was different: 'At the death of their father, his sons do not divide immediately their inheritance. Usually, the eldest takes over his father's assets and liabilities on behalf of them all and continues the business'.[723]
