♠ Use of property ♣ present ♥ “The lands and their dues were considered the property of that person, to do with as he wished, until the king decided to reclaim them and give them to another. Thus the king retained his absolute suzerainty over the land.” [724]
♠ Property market ♣ inferred absent ♥ “The lands and their dues were considered the property of that person, to do with as he wished, until the king decided to reclaim them and give them to another. Thus the king retained his absolute suzerainty over the land.” [725]

♠ Other property ownership ♣ present ♥ Slaves. [726]

Inheritance System

♠ partible inheritance ♣ present ♥ Neo-Babylonian and Achaemenid court records show that inherited property could be divided between multiple heirs. One such document analysed by Wunsch may refer to 'the virtual or physical division of an inherited field, an action that usually was triggered by co-owners intending to sell their individual shares.'[727] However, the usual situation for wealthy families in 6th- and 5th-century bce Babylonia was different: 'At the death of their father, his sons do not divide immediately their inheritance. Usually, the eldest takes over his father's assets and liabilities on behalf of them all and continues the business'.[728]
♠ patrilineal inheritance ♣ present ♥ The usual situation for wealthy families in 6th- and 5th-century bce Babylonia was different: 'At the death of their father, his sons do not divide immediately their inheritance. Usually, the eldest takes over his father's assets and liabilities on behalf of them all and continues the business'.[729]
♠ matrilineal inheritance ♣ ♥
♠ primogeniture inheritance by only males ♣ present ♥ The usual situation for wealthy families in 6th- and 5th-century bce Babylonia was different: 'At the death of their father, his sons do not divide immediately their inheritance. Usually, the eldest takes over his father's assets and liabilities on behalf of them all and continues the business'.[730]
♠ primogeniture inheritance by males and females ♣ absent ♥ The usual situation for wealthy families in 6th- and 5th-century bce Babylonia was different: 'At the death of their father, his sons do not divide immediately their inheritance. Usually, the eldest takes over his father's assets and liabilities on behalf of them all and continues the business'.[731]
♠ other inheritance practices ♣ present ♥ The usual situation for wealthy families in 6th- and 5th-century bce Babylonia was different: 'At the death of their father, his sons do not divide immediately their inheritance. Usually, the eldest takes over his father's assets and liabilities on behalf of them all and continues the business'.[732]
♠ Trusts ♣ present ♥The usual situation for wealthy families in 6th- and 5th-century bce Babylonia was different: 'At the death of their father, his sons do not divide immediately their inheritance. Usually, the eldest takes over his father's assets and liabilities on behalf of them all and continues the business'.[733]
♠ State expropriation ♣ frequent ♥ Stolper believes that the Murasu business firm 'was in effect expropriated by a member of the royal family', prince Arsam, in 414 bce.[734] However, van Driel disagrees, viewing the abrupt end of the Murasu documents as representative of archival practice ('cleaning up' the archive by removing 'dead' documents) rather than an act of state expropriation.[735] “The lands and their dues were considered the property of that person, to do with as he wished, until the king decided to reclaim them and give them to another. Thus the king retained his absolute suzerainty over the land.” [736]
♠ Non-state expropriation ♣ inferred absent ♥ “The lands and their dues were considered the property of that person, to do with as he wished, until the king decided to reclaim them and give them to another. Thus the king retained his absolute suzerainty over the land.” [737] Note: could the temple reclaim land?

Equity[edit]

Sex Class and Race[edit]

Marriage

♠ Monogamy ♣ present ♥ 'The family in Babylonia, as well as in the majority of other countries of the Achaemenid Empire, was in the main monogamous.'[738] Discussing Achaemenid-period Egypt, Dandamaev and Lukonin also say that 'The Egyptian family was monogamous'.[739] However, polygamy was practised in Iran, 'and it was permitted to have many legal wives and also concubines'.[740]
♠ Formal Elite Polygamy ♣ present ♥ In addition to concubines, the Persian king could have multiple 'lawful wives'.[741] 'Wherever the evidence is available, it is clear' that the Achaemenid 'kings had several wives. Ideally, the king selected his successor from among the sons born of these unions. But the crucial factor in eligibility for the throne was paternal descent, so that sons born by concubines could accede in the absence of "legitimate" offspring.'[742] The king was not the only one legally allowed to be polygamous, however: in Iran, 'it was permitted to have many legal wives and also concubines'.[743]
♠ Informal Elite Polygamy ♣ no ♥ Mir-Hosseini, Shaki and Wakin tell us that 'polygamy was an ancient privilege of the aristocracy and spiritual dignitaries' of Iran,[744] and Iranians in the Achaemenid Empire could have 'many legal wives' in addition to concubines.[745] Coded 'absent' because it appears that elite polygamy was legally permissible in many contexts.
♠ Elite Concubinage ♣ present ♥ Amelie Kuhrt discusses concubines in the royal court: 'Concubines, clearly distinguished from the king's wives, were selected from women who arrived at court from the subject territories, either as tribute or as prisoners-of-war. Although we have repeated statements that the king had 300 (or 360) concubines, it seems likely (on the analogy of other courts) that only some of these were picked for the king's bed, with the rest functioning as maids and entertainers'.[746]
♠ Typical number of extramarital partners ♣ many ♥ At the very highest levels of Persian society, men kept large numbers of concubines. 'We know from many examples that the kings, like other Persians, practiced polygamy and that they also had many concubines.'[747] Herodotus says that 'Every Persian marries many lawful wives, and keeps still more concubines'.[748] Although some of the women referred to as concubines may in fact have functioned as 'maids and entertainers',[749] the Persian kings tended to produce large numbers of offspring with women other than their wives. For instance, 'When Xerxes II was assassinated soon after attaining the throne, competition emerged among the nothoi, the sons of several Babylonian women ... In this case, the institution of concubine perfectly met the objectives of polygamy and possessing multiple concubines ... as Strabo puts it, "For the sake of having many children"'.[750] For instance, Artaxerxes I is said to have had 17 illegitimate children,[751] and Justin claims that Artaxerxes II had 115 sons by his concubines.[752]

Legal rights are different on the basis of:

♠ Gender ♣ no ♥ The Achaemenid period saw improvements in the legal rights of women when compared to older Near Eastern judicial systems. 'Women had standing [to sue in court] in cases involving significant inner-family disputes, specifically their dowries, where their legal status or rights were at issue ... and, when independent, to protect their business interests just like men ... In criminal matters, all persons of whatever status could notify the court of alleged criminal activity, lodge an accusation, and testify in the matter.'[753] Magdalene also notes that for the first time in this area of the world, women could act as witnesses for legal texts, pointing to 'an increased status for women'.[754] Writing specifically on the Egyptian Demotic law which operated in Achaemenid-period Egypt, Dandamaev and Lukonin tell us that 'An Egyptian woman enjoyed substantial rights, and the husband did not have the right to pawn her as security for debts. The Demotic matrimonial contracts provided for the right of a wife to retain her own property in marriage and in the case of divorce; she also had the right to transfer it to her children as an inheritance. A woman could also own property, after having separated it from her husband's property. If the husband obtained a divorce, he had to apportion a certain share of the property to the wife. Divorce could also be carried out on the initiative of the wife ... A daughter inherited the property of the parents equally with her brothers and could dispose of it at her own discretion'.[755] "Upon looking at our sources, which derive essentially from private archives, we are left with the impression that women used to play a considerable part when agreements about property rights were to be negotiated by individuals."[756] In Late Period Egypt: "As is commonly known, (money) lending was a particularly brisk business; this activity attracted also female investors, as is illustrated by a serious of separate agreements in our material."[757]
♠ Social class ♣ no ♥ 'Slaves and former-slaves had standing [to sue in court] in regard to their status as slave or freeman. In criminal matters, all persons of whatever status could notify the court of alleged criminal activity, lodge an accusation, and testify in the matter.'[758]
♠ Ethnicity or race ♣ no ♥ 'In criminal matters, all persons of whatever status could notify the court of alleged criminal activity, lodge an accusation, and testify in the matter.'[759]

Discrimination[edit]

Discrimination on the basis of gender (include de facto discrimination even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to both males and females ♣ yes ♥ Xenophon says that the Pharnabazus, the Persian satrap of Aeolis (Hellespontine Phrygia), made the widow of a local dynast governor of part of his territory.[760] We also have evidence from accounting tablets from Persepolis of female supervisors of workers,[761] and Llewellyn-Jones refers to 'female administrative personnel'.[762] Irdabama, a woman identified from the Persepolis texts, had 'the authority to issue commands to the administrative hierarchy at Persepolis' and appears in the texts 'receiving and distributing food supplies'[763]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to both males and females ♣ yes ♥ Women could attain positions of considerable power in the Achaemenid administrative system. Though no explicit references to promotion from lower to higher positions could be found, Farazmand points to the 'extensive and widespread organizational practice of educated - free for all - women as personnel managers in large-scale capital projects such as building dams, irrigation systems, and public enterprise management. Recent discoveries attest to the fact that women performed exemplarily as "compensations managers" in the construction of the gigantic Persepolis'.[764]
♠ Education is available to both males and females ♣ yes ♥ Kuhrt discusses the privileged education bestowed on 'Persian boys belonging to the elite', including military training and schooling in the 'political and ethical ideals of fidelity and honourable conduct'.[765] However, she also says in a note on a noble Persian female archer: 'Although this is the only (surviving) mention of a Persian woman equipped with military and hunting skills, it is quite likely that the education of royal and noble women included this more generally.'[766] In Late Period Egypt "Egyptian women (unlike Greeks) could act in transactions on their own behalf and without any guardian whatsoever; equally, women could come forward in law-courts totally unaided as plaintiffs or defendants. And it is quite evident that women were capable of independent economic activities regardless of marital status."[767]
♠ Property can be owned by both males and females ♣ yes ♥ Irdabama, a woman identified from the Persepolis texts, was a 'wealthy landowner'.[768] Women could own and manage property in Late Period Egypt.[769]


Discrimination on the basis of social class (include de facto limitations even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all social classes ♣ no ♥ Discussing administrative structures in the empire, Briant says that 'the transmission of titles and profes­sional qualifications was a matter of privilege, passed down along family lines.'[770]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to people from all social classes ♣ no ♥ In his discussion of the position of bureaucrats of local origin in the conquered regions of the Achaemenid Empire, Briant is clear that even members of the local nobility were limited to more subservient roles in the administration: 'Local dignitaries were associated with the government of the Empire as auxiliaries to a new ethnically and socially homogenous ruling group.'[771] 'Furthermore, the transmission of titles and profes­sional qualifications was a matter of privilege, passed down along family lines.'[772] Kuhrt concurs: 'Family descent clearly played an important role, and provided access to high office'.[773]
♠ Education is available to people from all social classes ♣ no ♥ Briant tells us that education 'was available only for the chosen few'.[774] Kuhrt discusses the privileged education bestowed on 'Persian boys belonging to the elite', including military training and schooling in the 'political and ethical ideals of fidelity and honourable conduct'.[775] 'Thus the Persian elite of the diaspora was trained to share the ideals of service to the king and loyalty to the Achaemenid empire'[776]
♠ Property can be owned by people from all social classes ♣ no ♥ Property ownership was not limited to a small elite: the people called 'autourgoi' by the Greeks were poor peasants who worked their own small parcels of land.[777] However, some groups of people were excluded from this privilege, like the 'bonded serfs' describes by Dandamaev and Lukonin: they were 'farmers deprived of their own property' were obliged to work land belonging to 'the state, temples and private individuals'.[778]

Discrimination on the basis of ethnicity/race

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ yes ♥ In a discussion of the organization of the Achaemenid bureaucracy, Farazmand says (in slightly odd phrasing) that 'Gender-based public administration was also complemented by ethnic-based public management practices across the vast realm of the empire that included hundreds, if not thousands, of nationalities and ethnic cultures, a tradition consistent with the theory of "tolerant governance"'.[779] Ethnic Persians dominated the top bureaucratic positions,[780] but locals were certainly employed too. Briant says that in the satrap of Egypt, 'the Persians had Egyptians in their service ... For instance, one of the high officials of the tribute administration, the senti, was Egyptian; we also know of an Osorwer, one of Parnu's subordinates, at Syene.'[781]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ yes ♥ In her notes on one of the Persepolis Fortification Tablets, reporting the orders of Parnaka, an important official, to his secretaries, Kuhrt writes that 'The name of the transmitter is "the Ionian", which may indicate that a Greek occupied a high position in Parnaka's service'. One of the Persepolis Treasury Tablets could 'indicate the presence of yet another Greek making a successful career in the Persepolis bureaucracy'.[782] Kuhrt's use of 'successful' here suggests to me that administrators, including foreigners such as Greeks, may have been able to rise up in the ranks of the bureaucracy through personal merit. Nevertheless, this had its limits, and the highest positions seem to have been occupied by Persians or Medes: 'without excep­tion - after the first years of occupation, local officials were awarded subservient jobs, not positions that involved setting policy at the highest level.'[783]
♠ Education is available to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ no ♥ Briant tells us that education 'was available only for the chosen few'.[784] Kuhrt discusses the privileged education bestowed on 'Persian boys belonging to the elite', including military training and schooling in the 'political and ethical ideals of fidelity and honourable conduct'.[785] 'Thus the Persian elite of the diaspora was trained to share the ideals of service to the king and loyalty to the Achaemenid empire'[786]
♠ Property can be owned by people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ yes ♥ Although Persians certainly formed an elite and privileged group in the Achaemenid Empire, they did not have a monopoly on property ownership. For instance, we hear of two Rhodians, Mentor and Memnon, holding land in the satrapy of Phrygia.[787] The king could choose to bestow gifts and high status on whomever he liked: 'The possibilities offered to all - Persian and provincial - by the system of royal honours was one of the most effective means for holding the empire together.[788]

Social Mobility[edit]

Status[edit]

Elite status

♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ present ♥ Llewellyn-Jones tells us that 'A comparatively small group of nobles occupied a high place in this pyramidal structure [of the court], for these were the hereditary Persian nobility, whom the Greeks called the ‘People of the Gate’ (Plutarch, Themistocles 26.6), and who were obliged - because of blood and status - to serve at court and wait on the king'.[789] At a lower level of prestige, 'State and temple civil servants, priests, scribes, merchants, free craftsmen and farmers were counted among the ranks of citizens with full rights. In a legal sense, they were all regarded as enjoying equal rights, and their status was hereditary.'[790]
♠ elite status is selected ♣ present ♥ Although there was certainly a system of inherited elite status, the king could also choose to bestow favoured status on individuals who had rendered important services, giving them precious gifts.[791] According to Briant, there was a 'recognized court category' of 'benefactors' of the king - Achaemenid subjects could receive rewards (for instance, titles or large estates) in recognition of bravery in battle, 'horticultural achievements', or other good deeds.[792]
♠ formal system of hereditary social status ♣ present ♥ 'State and temple civil servants, priests, scribes, merchants, free craftsmen and farmers were counted among the ranks of citizens with full rights. In a legal sense, they were all regarded as enjoying equal rights, and their status was hereditary.'[793] The status of bonded serf ('farmers deprived of their own property' and working on land that belonged to 'the state, temples and private individuals') was also inherited down the generations.[794]

♠ social mobility ♣ present ♥ Briant notes the lack of Persian written sources which would show us how the Achaemenids conceived of their own society, but thinks 'There are strong grounds for supposing that, for some purposes at least, they still defined it in terms of the ancient Iranian social divisions outlined in parts of the Avesta, where individuals are classified by basic function as priests, warriors, and farmers'.[795] These 'classes' should not be understood in the modern sense of the term and were not strictly 'ranked' (although the Avestan texts depict farmers as subordinate to warriors and priests).[796] Alongside these ancient Iranian social divisions, there was 'social stratification', which 'became more marked as a result of the imperial conquests'.[797] I have coded present because it was evidently possible in the Achaemenid Empire for individuals and families to rise up the ranks of society, or to fall from positions of power and influence. 'During the Achaemenid period nobility and authority were in fact conferred not solely by birth but also by royal favor.'[798]

♠ recruitment of chief executive ♣ equal ♥ Despite the contested nature of Achaemenid royal succession, the man who eventually became king in each case was always recruited from the top Persian nobility, and arguably always from the lineage of Achaemenes. If we believe Darius I, 'Bardiya' was actually a usurper magus named Gaumata, but this is now seen by many scholars as a propagandistic narrative,[799] and the Magians in any case enjoyed high status in the Achaemenid court.[800] Darius III is described by some classical authors as an outsider who either rose to the throne 'through outstanding bravery' or was placed there by the powerful and murderous eunuch Bagoas.[801][802] However, Briant views this as a function of Macedonian propaganda designed to convey Darius III's illegitimacy and legitimize Alexander the Great's conquest. He concludes that Darius was a prominent royal courtier and cousin of Artaxerxes III.[803]
♠ extreme status elevation ♣ present ♥ Dandamaev and Lukonin describe the life of Gimillu, a temple slave at Eanna in Uruk, who 'Over the course of two decades (539-520 B.C.) ... enjoyed great influence and occupied a high and prominent place in the administration of the temple of Eanna and its surroundings. At first he was overseer of the temple livestock, while later he collected rents and leased out hundreds of hectares of temple land'.[804] Despite multiple court convictions and his 'reputation as an incorrigible swindler', he 'obviously enjoyed the patronage of the satrap Gobryas, to whom he had access during his stay in Babylon on matters of business relating to the temple of Eanna.'[805] Briant also mentions Aelian's account of the 'simple peasant Rhakokes', whom Artaxerxes II appointed as a royal judge.[806]
♠ extreme status diminution ♣ present ♥ If we believe Ctesias' account, the changing fortunes of Megabyzus II are a good example of extreme status diminution. He was a 'scion of a prominent family', army commander and 'one of the most influential men around Xerxes [I]'.[807] However, when on a hunt with Xerxes' successor Artaxerxes I he killed a lion before the king had chance to touch it, Artaxerxes ordered him beheaded.[808] After pleading from Amytis (Artaxerxes' sister and Megabyzus' wife) and others, Artaxerxes relented and exiled him instead. 'Megabyzus spends five years in exile, then flees disguised as a pisagas. Pisagas is said by the Persians to be a leper and is untouchable to all'.[809] Eventually Megabyzus returns, is forgiven and becomes a valued 'table-companion' of the king. Kuhrt reproduces this passage as an illustration of the 'precariousness of royal favour'.[810] We also know of an Egyptian noble 'who, after the Persian victory, "fell from great wealth to poverty ... had nothing but what a beggar has"'.[811]

♠ occupational mobility ♣ absent ♥ In the Achaemenid royal court, there were few fixed roles: 'Every court job was temporary and could be transferred from one day to the next to another trustworthy man'.[812] However, this seems to have been a special case, and I am not sure that we can posit the existence of a 'functioning job market' for ordinary people across the empire. Briant says that 'the transmission of titles and professional qualifications was a matter of privilege, passed down along family lines'.[813] David Weisberg's book Guild Structure and Political Allegiance in Early Achaemenid Mesopotamia attempted to demonstrate the presence of guilds and hereditary occupations in this period: Wright notes in a review that he uncovered a 'high but not exclusive association between craft and kin group'.[814]

Slavery[edit]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ unknown ♥ It is difficult to find estimates of the proportion of the population enslaved across the Achaemenid Empire - partly because of the fragmentary nature of the archives that have come down to us - and this would have varied dramatically between satrapies. 'Persian nobles became major slave owners in Babylonia and other conquered countries ... Although there were more slaves at this time than in any previous period, the institution of slavery itself had already begun to decline'.[815] Dandamayev has examined the role of slave labour in various spheres of (private) economic activity in Babylonia from 626 to 331 bce, and concludes that 'the hire of slaves was only reluctantly and rarely resorted to. The wages for slave labor were the equal of the wages of the free hired laborer, but differed from the latter in that the slave tried to avoid work in every possible way'. In Achaemenid-period Babylonia (at least outside the royal and temple economy), hired wage labour made a larger contribution to economic production than slave labour.[816] More generally, Llewellyn-Jones comments: 'Persia did not have an extensive slave economy and, as Dandamayev (1988) emphasises: on the whole, there was only a small number of slaves in relation to the number of free persons even in the most developed countries of the Achaemenid empire, and slave labour was in no position to supplant the labour of free workers.'[817] When Cyrus entered Babylonia he 'released all the exiles and enslaved peoples' returned statues of gods Babylonians had stolen, and undertook many restoration works.[818]

♠ Proportion of population enserfed ♣ unknown ♥ It is difficult to find estimates of the proportion of the population enserfed across the Achaemenid Empire - probably because of the fragmentary nature of the archives that have come down to us - and this would have varied dramatically between satrapies. However, a passage in Dandamaev and Lukonin's book suggests a large population of enserfed people outside the complex urban centres: 'The dependent strata of the population consisted of farmers deprived of their own property and working from generation to generation on land that belonged to the state, temples and private individuals ... Such farmers lived in the rural countryside, where urban self-government was not typical and where, in general, there were no free people'.[819] Late Period Egypt: "I am deliberately not using the term 'slave', since it automatically evokes the notion of absolute property based upon the relationship between a master and an unfree person; note that not every unfree person in Egypt was identical with a slave in the Roman sense. It would appear that in Egypt unfree persons, despite the fact that some might be brought and sold, were recognized by the society to the effect that many an unfree person could hold indeed a good deal of rights. Note furthermore that in Egypt a serf was not necessarily attached to agricultural labour..."[820]

Types

♠ Chattel slavery ♣ present ♥ Certain sources state that 'slavery was banned in the Achaemenid Empire'[821] or that Cyrus the Great 'abolished slavery'[822] However, these claims tend to be made by authors who are not specialists in Persian history. Dandamaev and Lukonin tell us in no certain terms that 'In the most developed countries of the Achaemenid Empire, slaves, who were equivalent to livestock, were the chief form of movable property; they were sold, transferred by inheritance, given away etc.'[823]
♠ Elite slavery ♣ present ♥ Eunuchs served in both royal and satrapal courts, and many boys were 'trafficked' between the Aegean coast and inland provinces for this purpose, or sent as 'human tribute' to the royal court.[824] Kuhrt stresses the need to distinguish eunuchs in 'lowly positions', part of the domestic staff at courts, from 'those described as occupying highly influential positions in relation to kings'.[825] Likewise, some concubines were women 'of high rank' who 'due to their non-Persian descent ... were excluded from marrying the king', while others entered the court as captives from the provinces.[826]
♠ Bonded serfdom ♣ present ♥ 'The dependent strata of the population consisted of farmers deprived of their own property and working from generation to generation on land that belonged to the state, temples and private individuals. Legally, they were not considered to be slaves; for example, it was forbidden to sell them. Such farmers lived in the rural countryside, where urban self-government was not typical and where, in general, there were no free people'.[827] Late Period Egypt: "I am deliberately not using the term 'slave', since it automatically evokes the notion of absolute property based upon the relationship between a master and an unfree person; note that not every unfree person in Egypt was identical with a slave in the Roman sense. It would appear that in Egypt unfree persons, despite the fact that some might be brought and sold, were recognized by the society to the effect that many an unfree person could hold indeed a good deal of rights. Note furthermore that in Egypt a serf was not necessarily attached to agricultural labour..."[828]
♠ Corvée labor to a landowner ♣ inferred absent ♥ The Achaemenid state certainly extracted corvée labour, but I have not found evidence of this for private landowners. The temples of Mesopotamia and elsewhere were a special case: they owed labour to the state and sent their slaves and dependants to fulfil this obligation (see variable below), but derived their own wealth in other ways, namely by hiring wage labourers, using slaves, and extracting tithes. In Mesopotamia 'Anyone who obtained an income from the temple, whether through leased temple land or prebend, was obliged to pay a tithe on it, the esru'.[829]
♠ Corvée labor to the state ♣ present ♥ Frye writes that 'various kinds of slavery and forced labor existed in different parts of the empire, and the Elamite word kurtas in the cuneiform documents meant "workers" in general but under different conditions, many serving a term of forced labor on royal estates'.[830] We find in Dandamaev and Lukonin that 'the temples performed state corvee work, sending their slaves (farmers, shepherds, gardeners, carpenters, etc.) for work in the palace household in Babylon and other cities'.[831] More generally, Briant tells us that 'Subjects and peasants had more than taxes to worry about. They were frequently placed in corvee [forced or statutory labor] to work at tasks undertaken by the royal administration. The case of Babylonia is the best known. We have already seen that the sanctuaries could at any time be required to furnish manual labor upon demand of the satrap, especially for the construction and maintenance of canals ... This is the system referred to as urasu service, which is known from the Neo-Babylonian period. It involved all owners of real estate, whether sanctuaries or private individuals, particularly those whose fields lay along a canal.'[832]

Restraints

♠ Owners can execute slaves ♣ inferred absent ♥ No source could be found to confirm or deny this. Baker has examined the evidence for private slave ownership in mid-1st-millennium bce Babylonia (including the Achaemenid period), and knows of 'no evidence from the Egibi archive to suggest that slaves were subject to physical restraint, nor is there any mention in the texts of recaptured slaves being punished (though our knowledge of record-keeping practices suggests that there was no reason to document such actions in the private contracts). According to one document, Marduk-nasir-apli of the Egibi family finds an escaped slave woman of his in the house of the man who helped her to flee; however, he shows mercy towards the man and does not take her back'.[833]
♠ Owners can impose corporal punishment on slaves ♣ [absent; present] ♥ 'Fugitive slaves were caught, branded like livestock, incarcerated in fetters and returned back to work'.[834] In her examination of slave contracts from mid-1st-millennium bce Babylonia (including the Achaemenid period), Baker found no 'mention in the texts of recaptured slaves being punished (though our knowledge of record-keeping practices suggests that there was no reason to document such actions in the private contracts). According to one document, Marduk-nasir-apli of the Egibi family finds an escaped slave woman of his in the house of the man who helped her to flee; however, he shows mercy towards the man and does not take her back'.[835]
♠ Corporal punishment by the state ♣ present ♥ Wiesehoefer explains how those who refused to submit to the king's laws (and by extension to the 'cosmic order of Auramazda') were punished, experiencing 'in extreme cases mutilation, torture, and execution as "servants of lies"'.[836]

Human sacrifice

♠ Prisoners ♣ absent ♥ I have coded 'absent' here for all forms of human sacrifice because the only reference to human sacrifice I have found comes from one passage in Herodotus (7.114). Describing the progress of Xerxes' expedition against Greece, he says that at the Strymon River, 'the Magi sought good omens by sacrificing white horses'. Then, 'After using these enchantments and many others besides on the river, they passed over it at the Nine Ways in Edonian country, by the bridges which they found thrown across the Strymon. When they learned that Nine Ways was the name of the place, they buried alive that number of boys and maidens, children of the local people. To bury people alive is a Persian custom; I have learned by inquiry that when Xerxes' wife Amestris reached old age, she buried twice seven sons of notable Persians as an offering on her own behalf to the fabled god beneath the earth.'[837] Boyce comments: 'Problems concerning Xerxes' beliefs were created by Herodotus' account of incidents during his Greek war. Human sacrifice (Herodotus 7.114) was certainly profoundly at odds with Zoroastrian morality, and presumably represents an ancient pagan custom revived under stress of war (or in the case of Queen Amestris, of old age).'[838] Similarly, Martin stresses the 'reverence for human life' which characterised the Zoroastrian morality adopted by Persian kings[839] (although this never stopped the Persian elite from waging war or sentencing criminals to death). Keeping in mind that if the Greek historians had heard of other instances of human sacrifice, they almost certainly would have recorded them, I think we must agree with Boyce here.
♠ Low social class ♣ absent ♥
♠ Elites ♣ absent ♥
♠ Retainers ♣ absent ♥
♠ Outsiders ♣ absent ♥

Religion and Normative Ideology[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Jenny Reddish ♥ These codes were developed at Seshat General Workshop in Oxford 2017 and Axial Age Workshop, Oxford, 2015


♠ Expert ♣ ♥

♠ Name of Official Cult ♣ ♥

♠ alternate Names of Official Cult ♣ ♥

♠ Name of other significant religious or ideological systems ♣ ♥

♠ Description of the Normative Ideology ♣ ♥

Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ present ♥The Cyrus Cylinder (composed by priests of Marduk as a record of Cyrus the Great's conquest of Babylon and his proclamations) records that 'Bel-Marduk cast his eye over all countries, seeking for a righteous ruler ... Then he called by name Cyrus, King of Anshan, and pronounced him ruler of the lands'. Bel-Marduk was the 'national god' of the Babylonians [840][841] Because he 'released all the exiles and enslaved peoples' from Babylon, including the Jews, Cyrus the Great also occupies an extraordinary position in the Hebrew canon.[842] The book of Isaiah calls Cyrus the Lord's 'anointed' and his 'shepherd'.[843] Schmitt says that although the Achaemenid kings were not gods or descendants of gods, 'the other fundamental basis of their kingship beside the genealogical principle is the theory of divine right of kings, Gottesgnadentum. They are kings vasna Auramazdaha, "by the favor of Ahura Mazda;" it is unthinkable that Darius would have erected an inscription saying that "he obtained the Persian kingship by the excellence of his horse and groom," as claimed in Herodotus 3.88.3. This supreme god "bestowed the empire" (xsacam frabara) on the kings. As these and similar expressions show, the Achaemenids' reign is legitimized by the gods, and the king is invested by them; i.e., he is their elect and their representative on earth ... These two principles, the dynastic one and that of divine right, belong to contrasting areas and periods—respectively, to prehistoric nomad tribes of Indo-European origin and to the highly civilized Mesopotamian peoples.'[844]

♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ absent ♥ The sources mention 'farnah' (or 'xwarnah', 'farrah' and so on), meaning something like 'glory' or divinely bestowed fortune. Gnoli infers from 'a number of passages in the Avesta' that farnah was 'a magic force of luminous and fiery nature'.[845] 'Zoroaster’s xwarrah is said to have descended from heaven and become manifest "in the form of fire" ... at the moment of his birth'.[846] Shahbazi claims that rightful Achaemenid kings were 'accompanied' by farnah, but the source of this force was the great god Ahura Mazda: the king was not in control of it.[847]

♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ absent ♥ 'The Achaemenid kings were no gods ... and they were not of divine origin.'[848]

♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ absent ♥ Despite the implication in several Greek and Roman sources that the Persian kings were worshipped as gods, 'there is in fact no evidence that they were even deified after death ... and Darius' inscriptions show that deification did not enter his thoughts. In Egypt a Pharaoh was divine. But in the Near East, as in Greece, the notion was - and for 1,500 years had been - universally rejected as long as divinity was regarded as supernal'.[849]

♠ Automatic deification ♣ absent ♥ There is 'no evidence' that Persian kings were deified after death.[850]

♠ Rulers are gods ♣ absent ♥ 'The Achaemenid kings were no gods, in spite of Aeschylus (Persae 157), where Queen Atossa is called "a god's [Darius'] wife" and "a god's [Xerxes'] mother" (here and elsewhere Greek ideas are brought forward); and they were not of divine origin.'[851] v"The Persian King was not a god, but he was a person set apart" [852]

"However, unlike previous Near Eastern dynasties, they did not claim divine descent or nature."[853]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ present ♥ The Avestas contain evidence of the 'egalitarian ideals' of Zoroastrianism.[854] However, the paragraphs below cover contexts in the Achaemenid Empire in which religion was utilized to reinforce inequality of various forms: there is something of a gap between religious doctrine (especially concerning eschatology) and the use of religious imagery and rhetoric by those in power. Alternatively, we could say that maintaining that (for example) men and women can both attain salvation after death does not necessarily mean advocating gender equality in this world.

♠ Ideological thought equates rulers and commoners ♣ absent ♥ Although Zoroastrian eschatology held that the potential for resurrection and salvation was universal,[855] the religion as practised in the Achaemenid Empire, and particularly as interpreted by Achaemenid rulers, did not tend to stress the essential equality of kings and commoners (perhaps unsurprisingly). The kings used public inscriptions to proclaim their special status and right to rule, divinely bestowed by the great Zoroastrian God Ahura Mazda. Most famously, Darius I's Behistun inscription says: 'By the will of Ahuramazda I am king. Ahuramazda gave me kingship', repeating similar formulations throughout the text.[856]
♠ Ideological thought equates elites and commoners ♣ absent ♥ A passage from Shaki's article suggests that in Zoroastrian thought, elites were understood as cosmologically distinct from other social groups: 'The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[857]
♠ Ideological thought equates all ethnicities ♣ absent ♥ The form of Zoroastrianism practised by Achaemenid kings and nobility does seem to have privileged Persian identity above other groups of the empire. In an analysis of Darius I's Bisitun (Behistun) relief, Lincoln notes that 'ethnic self is privileged above ethnic other' and that 'loyal Persians are connected by their king to a divine, transcendent above while disloyal others are similarly consigned to a devalued below'.[858] Through invoking the name of Ahura Mazda and Zoroastrian abhorrence of 'the lie', religion is recruited to serve Darius' purposes: the reinforcement of his own (divinely bestowed) right to rule and the superiority of his own ethnic group.[859] Smith also notes that the Achaemenid Persian 'ethnie' shared a 'common belief in the blessing of Ahura Mazda on the Persian kings and nobles, in a timeless and harmonious cosmic order'.[860]
♠ Ideological thought equates men and women ♣ [absent; present] ♥ There is disagreement about the degree of sexual equality in Zoroastrianism. According to more ancient Indo-Iranian religious traditions, women could not hope for 'ascent to paradise' but were consigned, along with slaves, to 'the kingdom of shadows beneath the earth'.[861] Ketayun Gould thinks that Zoroastrian teachings then 'challenged the status quo by introducing a more gender-inclusive philosophy'. 'Paradise was no longer the exclusive domain of men who had the means to achieve it'.[862] Similarly, 'Mary Boyce and Albert de Jong argued that women were generally held in high esteem and occupied a dignified position in Zoroastrianism, their negative portrayal within the same tradition being only isolated and occasional occurrences and in any case inconsistent with Zoroastrian teaching'.[863] However, Jamsheed Choksy's book 'Evil, Good, and Gender' challenged this widespread opinion, pulling together malign images of women and femininity (e.g. female demons) from a variety of Zoroastrian texts and arguing that these symbolic structures would have had direct implications for women's daily lives.[864] Rose describes how Zoroastrian concerns with ritual purity made 'all women of childbearing age' (along with male priests and male corpse-bearers) into 'liminal figures'; their 'lives have also been constantly circumscribed due to their biological production of "dead matter" through menstruation or childbirth'.[865] The position of women in this religious tradition remains 'paradoxical' because 'In the past, the notion of gender equality in terms of the spiritual potential of women has been tempered by the regular exclusion of women from the ritual domain, due to their particular physiology'.[866]
♠ Ideological thought equates people of all locations ♣ absent ♥ The form of Zoroastrianism practised by Achaemenid kings and nobility does seem to have privileged Persian identity above other groups, 'ethnicities' who were identified with the various provinces or regions of the empire (e.g. in the Apadana staircase reliefs at Persepolis).[867] In an analysis of Darius I's Bisitun (Behistun) relief, Lincoln notes that 'ethnic self is privileged above ethnic other' and that 'loyal Persians are connected by their king to a divine, transcendent above while disloyal others are similarly consigned to a devalued below'.[868] Through invoking the name of Ahura Mazda and Zoroastrian abhorrence of 'the lie', religion is recruited to serve Darius' purposes: the reinforcement of his own (divinely bestowed) right to rule and the superiority of his own group.[869] Lincoln's book has been criticized for its reliance on later Zoroastrian texts to make inferences about Achaemenid religion,[870] but I think we can infer from the combination in Achaemenid art of religious rhetoric and iconography showing Persians to be superior to other groups that their form of Zoroastrianism (or more broadly 'Mazdeanism') could be used to reinforce ideas of Persian superiority.
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes rulers and commoners ♣ present ♥ Although Zoroastrian eschatology held that the potential for resurrection and salvation was universal,[871] the religion as practised in the Achaemenid Empire, and particularly as interpreted by Achaemenid rulers, did not tend to stress the essential equality of kings and commoners (perhaps unsurprisingly). The kings used public inscriptions to proclaim their special status and right to rule, divinely bestowed by the great Zoroastrian God Ahura Mazda. Most famously, Darius I's Behistun inscription says: 'By the will of Ahuramazda I am king. Ahuramazda gave me kingship', repeating similar formulations throughout the text.[872]
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes elites and commoners ♣ present ♥ A passage from Shaki's article suggests that in Zoroastrian thought, elites were understood as cosmologically distinct from other social groups: 'The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[873]
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes all ethnicities ♣ present ♥ The form of Zoroastrianism practised by Achaemenid kings and nobility does seem to have privileged Persian identity above other groups of the empire. In an analysis of Darius I's Bisitun (Behistun) relief, Lincoln notes that 'ethnic self is privileged above ethnic other' and that 'loyal Persians are connected by their king to a divine, transcendent above while disloyal others are similarly consigned to a devalued below'.[874] Through invoking the name of Ahura Mazda and Zoroastrian abhorrence of 'the lie', religion is recruited to serve Darius' purposes: the reinforcement of his own (divinely bestowed) right to rule and the superiority of his own ethnic group.[875] Smith also notes that the Achaemenid Persian 'ethnie' shared a 'common belief in the blessing of Ahura Mazda on the Persian kings and nobles, in a timeless and harmonious cosmic order'.[876]
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes men and women ♣ present ♥ There is disagreement about the degree of sexual equality in Zoroastrianism, but there is clear evidence that ideological thought distinguished between men and women. According to more ancient Indo-Iranian religious traditions, women could not hope for 'ascent to paradise' but were consigned, along with slaves, to 'the kingdom of shadows beneath the earth'.[877] Ketayun Gould thinks that Zoroastrian teachings then 'challenged the status quo by introducing a more gender-inclusive philosophy'. 'Paradise was no longer the exclusive domain of men who had the means to achieve it'.[878] Similarly, 'Mary Boyce and Albert de Jong argued that women were generally held in high esteem and occupied a dignified position in Zoroastrianism, their negative portrayal within the same tradition being only isolated and occasional occurrences and in any case inconsistent with Zoroastrian teaching'.[879] However, Jamsheed Choksy's book 'Evil, Good, and Gender' challenged this widespread opinion, pulling together malign images of women and femininity (e.g. female demons) from a variety of Zoroastrian texts and arguing that these symbolic structures would have had direct implications for women's daily lives.[880] Rose describes how Zoroastrian concerns with ritual purity made 'all women of childbearing age' (along with male priests and male corpse-bearers) into 'liminal figures'; their 'lives have also been constantly circumscribed due to their biological production of "dead matter" through menstruation or childbirth'.[881] The position of women in this religious tradition remains 'paradoxical' because 'In the past, the notion of gender equality in terms of the spiritual potential of women has been tempered by the regular exclusion of women from the ritual domain, due to their particular physiology'.[882]

(CC: Note this code should be in agreement with 'Ideological thought equates men and women'. We need the expert to confirm for both codes, as there seems to be such disagreement in the literature)

♠ Ideological thought distinguishes people from different locations ♣ present ♥ The form of Zoroastrianism practised by Achaemenid kings and nobility does seem to have privileged Persian identity above other groups, 'ethnicities' who were identified with the various provinces or regions of the empire (e.g. in the Apadana staircase reliefs at Persepolis).[883] In an analysis of Darius I's Bisitun (Behistun) relief, Lincoln notes that 'ethnic self is privileged above ethnic other' and that 'loyal Persians are connected by their king to a divine, transcendent above while disloyal others are similarly consigned to a devalued below'.[884] Through invoking the name of Ahura Mazda and Zoroastrian abhorrence of 'the lie', religion is recruited to serve Darius' purposes: the reinforcement of his own (divinely bestowed) right to rule and the superiority of his own group.[885] Lincoln's book has been criticized for its reliance on later Zoroastrian texts to make inferences about Achaemenid religion,[886] but I think we can infer from the combination in Achaemenid art of religious rhetoric and iconography showing Persians to be superior to other groups that their form of Zoroastrianism (or more broadly 'Mazdeanism') could be used to reinforce ideas of Persian superiority.

♠ Ideological reinforcement of inequality ♣ present ♥ A passage from Shaki's article suggests that in Zoroastrian thought, elites were understood as cosmologically distinct from other social groups: 'The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[887]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of monogamy ♣ absent ♥ Given that the Mazda-worshipping Persian elite of the Achaemenid Empire were frequently polygynous,[888] it appears that there was no perceived contradiction between Zoroastrian faith and having many wives. In later Zoroastrian texts, polygamy appears an 'ancient privilege of the aristocracy and spiritual dignitaries'.[889]

(CC: I have changed some of the following variables to 'inferred present' as they are based on sources relating to later Zoroastrianism, assuming continuity)

♠ Ideology reinforces prosociality ♣ inferred present ♥ In an article that stresses continuities between ancient and modern Zoroastrianism, Boyce describes offerings to the dead and the rituals connected with these among modern worshippers: 'Many of these rituals are accompanied by offerings of food and drink, which are afterwards partaken of by the living in communion with the dead, the soul being invited back to join its kinsmen and friends, not in grief but in companionable happiness. In Irani idiom such observances are for "the rejoicing of the soul" ... Some of the offerings are also given to the poor, so that piety towards the dead, cheerfulness, and charity are combined in a way that is wholly Zoroastrian.'[890] Cantera says that 'from its very beginnings Zoroastrianism has developed an ethical imperative of assistance to the needy members of the community'.[891] (CC: If this is based on modern Zoroastrianism then maybe it should be 'inferred present'? The same with the next four variables)

♠ charity ♣ inferred present ♥ In an article that stresses continuities between ancient and modern Zoroastrianism, Boyce describes offerings to the dead and the rituals connected with these among modern worshippers: 'Many of these rituals are accompanied by offerings of food and drink, which are afterwards partaken of by the living in communion with the dead, the soul being invited back to join its kinsmen and friends, not in grief but in companionable happiness. In Irani idiom such observances are for "the rejoicing of the soul" ... Some of the offerings are also given to the poor, so that piety towards the dead, cheerfulness, and charity are combined in a way that is wholly Zoroastrian.'[892] Cantera says that 'from its very beginnings Zoroastrianism has developed an ethical imperative of assistance to the needy members of the community'.[893]
♠ production of public goods ♣ present ♥ Balali, Keulartz and Korthals link Zoroastrian ethics to the construction of qanats, an ancient irrigation system, in pre-Islamic Iran. Through the Zoroastrian exhortation to carry out 'good deeds', they argue that the worshipper 'is directed to relieve the poor, to irrigate and cultivate the soil, to provide food and fresh water in places where needed, and to devote the surplus of his wealth in charity to the well-being and prosperity of his fellow man'.[894] However, the focus of their article is on the lessons we could learn from 'traditional' systems of water management, rather than on rigorous historical analysis. It is unclear to what extent Achaemenid-period worshippers of Ahura Mazda actually laboured to produce public goods, directed solely by their religious values.
♠ communal dining ♣ inferred present ♥ In an article that stresses continuities between ancient and modern Zoroastrianism, Boyce describes offerings to the dead and the rituals - including communal dining - connected with these among modern worshippers: 'Many of these rituals are accompanied by offerings of food and drink, which are afterwards partaken of by the living in communion with the dead, the soul being invited back to join its kinsmen and friends, not in grief but in companionable happiness. In Irani idiom such observances are for "the rejoicing of the soul"'.[895]
♠ morality ♣ inferred present ♥ Zoroastrian thought also encompasses other beliefs and values that may be relevant for the reinforcement of prosociality. For insta

nce, there is an emphasis on what has been called 'free will'. Boyce says that for a Zoroastrian worshipper, 'The moral requirements of his faith make him self-reliant, bearing the responsibility for his own conduct and ultimate fate'.[896]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Slavery[edit]

♠ Ideology forbids slavery for co-ethnics ♣ inferred absent ♥ The evidence for this is scanty. However, in a Babylonian slave contract from Persepolis dating from the reign of Darius I, we find that 'The sellers and the slave women, judging from their names, were of Iranian descent'.[897] The presence of a written contract shows that this form of slavery was legally and culturally acceptable at the time, and the 'Iranian descent' of both slave seller and slaves suggests a lack of any prohibition of slavery for co-ethnics.
♠ Ideology forbids slavery for co-religionists ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no evidence of a ban on slavery for Mazda-worshippers under the Achaemenids, but in the Sasanian period 'A slave belonging to a non-Zoroastrian could ... acquire freedom by converting to Zoroastrianism. In this case the Mazdean community was obliged to help the slave buy himself free (by a loan or other means).'[898]
♠ Ideology forbids slavery universally ♣ inferred absent ♥ It is difficult to find direct evidence of Achaemenid-period Zoroastrian attitudes to slavery, but 'there can be no doubt that the various departments of the Achaemenid court included a large number of slaves'.[899], and 'Under the Achaemenids in Babylonia and other conquered countries Persian nobles became large slave owners'.[900] If there was religious doctrine forbidding ownership of slaves, it was evidently not observed by the Achaemenid Persian elite. In later Sasanian law, founded in Zoroastrian principles, we find a distinction between freeborn men and slaves, which is inconsistent with a doctrinal rejection of the institution of slavery.[901]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Human Sacrifice[edit]

♠ Human sacrifice ♣ 0 ♥ I have coded 'absent' here because the only reference to human sacrifice I have found comes from one passage in Herodotus (7.114). Describing the progress of Xerxes' expedition against Greece, he says that at the Strymon River, 'the Magi sought good omens by sacrificing white horses'. Then, 'After using these enchantments and many others besides on the river, they passed over it at the Nine Ways in Edonian country, by the bridges which they found thrown across the Strymon. When they learned that Nine Ways was the name of the place, they buried alive that number of boys and maidens, children of the local people. To bury people alive is a Persian custom; I have learned by inquiry that when Xerxes' wife Amestris reached old age, she buried twice seven sons of notable Persians as an offering on her own behalf to the fabled god beneath the earth.'[902] Boyce comments: 'Problems concerning Xerxes' beliefs were created by Herodotus' account of incidents during his Greek war. Human sacrifice (Herodotus 7.114) was certainly profoundly at odds with Zoroastrian morality, and presumably represents an ancient pagan custom revived under stress of war (or in the case of Queen Amestris, of old age).'[903] Similarly, Martin stresses the 'reverence for human life' which characterised the Zoroastrian morality adopted by Persian kings[904] (although this never stopped the Persian elite from waging war or sentencing criminals to death). Keeping in mind that if the Greek historians had heard of other instances of human sacrifice, they almost certainly would have recorded them, I think we must agree with Boyce here.

♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have coded 'inferred absent' here because the only reference to human sacrifice I have found comes from one passage in Herodotus (7.114). Describing the progress of Xerxes' expedition against Greece, he says that at the Strymon River, 'the Magi sought good omens by sacrificing white horses'. Then, 'After using these enchantments and many others besides on the river, they passed over it at the Nine Ways in Edonian country, by the bridges which they found thrown across the Strymon. When they learned that Nine Ways was the name of the place, they buried alive that number of boys and maidens, children of the local people. To bury people alive is a Persian custom; I have learned by inquiry that when Xerxes' wife Amestris reached old age, she buried twice seven sons of notable Persians as an offering on her own behalf to the fabled god beneath the earth.'[905] Boyce comments: 'Problems concerning Xerxes' beliefs were created by Herodotus' account of incidents during his Greek war. Human sacrifice (Herodotus 7.114) was certainly profoundly at odds with Zoroastrian morality, and presumably represents an ancient pagan custom revived under stress of war (or in the case of Queen Amestris, of old age).'[906] Similarly, Martin stresses the 'reverence for human life' which characterised the Zoroastrian morality adopted by Persian kings[907] (although this never stopped the Persian elite from waging war or sentencing criminals to death). Keeping in mind that if the Greek historians had heard of other instances of human sacrifice, they almost certainly would have recorded them, I think we must agree with Boyce here.
♠ human sacrifice of an in-group member ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have coded 'inferred absent' here because the only reference to human sacrifice I have found comes from one passage in Herodotus (7.114). Describing the progress of Xerxes' expedition against Greece, he says that at the Strymon River, 'the Magi sought good omens by sacrificing white horses'. Keeping in mind that if the Greek historians had heard of other instances of human sacrifice, they almost certainly would have recorded them, I think we must agree with Boyce here.
♠ human sacrifice of a low-status individual ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have coded 'inferred absent' here because the only reference to human sacrifice I have found comes from one passage in Herodotus (7.114). Describing the progress of Xerxes' expedition against Greece, he says that at the Strymon River, 'the Magi sought good omens by sacrificing white horses'. ♠ human sacrifice of a high status individual ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have coded 'inferred absent' here because the only reference to human sacrifice I have found comes from one passage in Herodotus (7.114). Describing the progress of Xerxes' expedition against Greece, he says that at the Strymon River, 'the Magi sought good omens by sacrificing white horses'. Then, 'After using these enchantments and many others besides on the river, they passed over it at the Nine Ways in Edonian country, by the bridges which they found thrown across the Strymon. When they learned that Nine Ways was the name of the place, they buried alive that number of boys and maidens, children of the local people. To bury people alive is a Persian custom; I have learned by inquiry that when Xerxes' wife Amestris reached old age, she buried twice seven sons of notable Persians as an offering on her own behalf to the fabled god beneath the earth.'[914] Boyce comments: 'Problems concerning Xerxes' beliefs were created by Herodotus' account of incidents during his Greek war. Human sacrifice (Herodotus 7.114) was certainly profoundly at odds with Zoroastrian morality, and presumably represents an ancient pagan custom revived under stress of war (or in the case of Queen Amestris, of old age).'[915] Similarly, Martin stresses the 'reverence for human life' which characterised the Zoroastrian morality adopted by Persian kings[916] (although this never stopped the Persian elite from waging war or sentencing criminals to death). Keeping in mind that if the Greek historians had heard of other instances of human sacrifice, they almost certainly would have recorded them, I think we must agree with Boyce here.
♠ human sacrifice of a relative ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have coded 'inferred absent' here because the only reference to human sacrifice I have found comes from one passage in Herodotus (7.114). Describing the progress of Xerxes' expedition against Greece, he says that at the Strymon River, 'the Magi sought good omens by sacrificing white horses'. Normative Ideological precepts concerning morality/supernatural beings[edit]

Supernatural beings (e.g., gods, spirits, ancestors)

♠ Number of officially sanctioned supernatural beings ♣ polytheistic ♥ "The “Achaemenid religion” is known from cuneiform inscriptions in the local Iranian language, Old Persian, and from tablets in Elamite found at Persepolis, as well as from other sources. It was a dualist religion, postulating the existence of good and evil from the beginning, as well as a polytheistic religion, but with one god, Ahura-Mazdā, outranking the others."[928]
♠ Omnipresent supernatural beings ♣ present ♥ 'Ahura-Mazda' originally meant 'the all-knowing Lord', but by Achaemenid times had become simply a name.[929] However, the quality of divine omniscience seems to have been retained: Whitley uses Yasna 29 to assert that in Zoroastrianism, 'the universe, man and mind are alike created and sustained by Ahura Mazda, omniscient of past, present and future'.[930] Shaki also refers to the 'omniscient Mazdean religion'.[931]
♠ Omniscient supernatural beings ♣ present ♥ 'Ahura-Mazda' originally meant 'the all-knowing Lord', but by Achaemenid times had become simply a name.[932] However, the quality of divine omniscience seems to have been retained: Whitley uses Yasna 29 to assert that in Zoroastrianism, 'the universe, man and mind are alike created and sustained by Ahura Mazda, omniscient of past, present and future'.[933] Shaki also refers to the 'omniscient Mazdean religion'.[934]
♠ Omnipotent supernatural beings ♣ present ♥ "By the favour of Auramazda’, as the Behistun inscription emphasizes, Darius has attained power despite all opposition; in the words of an inscription by this king of Susa: ‘Thus was Auramazda's desire: he chose me as (his) man in all the earth; me he made king in all the earth’ (DSf 12-18).6 With the god's help, moreover, he has also been able to stay in power—by means of a victory over the 'liar-kings.'"[935]
♠ High Gods (creator gods) ♣ moralizing ♥ The following quote makes it clear the Zoroastrian god, Ahura Mazda, was both responsible for the creation of the world and for punishing transgressors. "[T]he prophet feels also the awe due to the God who is “Creator of all” (Y. 44.7), and who, as Lord of aša, presides over a strict justice for mankind by which the wicked, who have chosen to act “at Lie’s commands” (Y. 31.1) will be in due course condemned. Their destination is the Worst Existence, i.e., Hell, which has been brought into being by Angra Mainyu (Y. 30.4). Mazdā has “appointed recompenses” (Y. 43.5), decreeing that “the end shall be different for each” (Y. 48.4); but he “has left to men’s wills (the choice between) holy and unholy” (Y. 45.9), and it is their own inner selves (daēnā-) which lead sinners to destruction (Y. 51.13)."[936]

Supernatural Enforcement of Morality

For this section, include any type of supernatural punishment or reward either in this lifetime (e.g., curses, good fortune) or in the afterlife (e.g., hell, reincarnation). This includes generalized impersonal punishment/reward (e.g., karma, mana) as well as punishment by specific supernatural beings. Include specific details of the nature and extent of punishment/reward in the description. Code “compound” moral actions (e.g., filial piety = kin + respect) in multiple categories as necessary. Morality categories are based on Moral Foundations Theory (Haidt & Graham, 2007) and Morality-As-Cooperation theory (Curry, 2016).
♠ supernatural enforcement against harm ♣ present ♥ Among the Achaemenids, there was no significant distinction between royal and divine law: "Through the proclamations of Xerxes to his subjects: ‘Obey that law (data), which Auramazda has established! Worship Auramazda at the proper time and in the proper ceremonial style!’ (XPh 49-51) we encounter another central term of the Achaemenid 'legal terminology': the data. This word (literally: ‘the recorded/stipulated’), indeed the entire command of the king, is reminiscent of an Avestan text that means that he who despises Ahura Mazda, the Amesa Spentas (the ‘beneficent immortals’), Mithra, the law (data-), Rasnu and justice, cannot obtain the favour of Ahura Mazda, the Beneficent Immortals, and Mithra (Yt. 10, 139)."[937] As for enforcement against harm specifically, in the Behistun inscription of Darius I it says: "It is not my desire that a man should do harm; nor indeed is it my desire, if he should do harm, he should not be punished".[938]
♠ supernatural enforcement of fairness ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ supernatural enforcement of human reciprocity ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ supernatural enforcement of property rights ♣ present ♥ Among the Achaemenids, there was no significant distinction between royal and divine law: "Through the proclamations of Xerxes to his subjects: ‘Obey that law (data), which Auramazda has established! Worship Auramazda at the proper time and in the proper ceremonial style!’ (XPh 49-51) we encounter another central term of the Achaemenid 'legal terminology': the data. This word (literally: ‘the recorded/stipulated’), indeed the entire command of the king, is reminiscent of an Avestan text that means that he who despises Ahura Mazda, the Amesa Spentas (the ‘beneficent immortals’), Mithra, the law (data-), Rasnu and justice, cannot obtain the favour of Ahura Mazda, the Beneficent Immortals, and Mithra (Yt. 10, 139)."[939] "Judicial relief included: compensation in silver or goods, specific performance, orders of eviction, injunctions (both temporary and permanent), capital punishment, criminal forfeiture of defendants’ property, certain forms of corporal punishment, as well as payments in lieu of corporal punishment. One NB trial record apparently refers to a payment in lieu of blood revenge (Wunsch, 2002, p. 356). In the case of compensation to the victim, we find damage multiples of one, two, three, ten, and thirty. The thirty-fold penalty is clearly used only in the case of theft of, or damage to, temple property (Wells, 2004, p. 114)."[940]

♠ supernatural enforcement of in-group loyalty ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ supernatural enforcement of respect ♣ present ♥ "In one sense, all forms of punishment meted out by the Achaemenid state could be considered as carrying out the will of Ahura Mazda: the law of the king 'concurs with that of the god',[941] and Stausberg has described the Persian kings' 'politicizing of the Avestan concepts' so that the Zoroastrian concept of 'the lie' came to encompass resistance to Achaemenid power.[942] In Darius I's inscription at Behistun, he proclaims that he has punished lawbreakers (in particular, the 'so-called liar-kings' and the rebel magus Gaumata) in accordance with the will of Ahura Mazda. The wrongdoing of Darius' enemies is presented as a transgression not only against the social order, but against the divine order created by Ahura Mazda.[943] Also, "Through the proclamations of Xerxes to his subjects: ‘Obey that law (data), which Auramazda has established! Worship Auramazda at the proper time and in the proper ceremonial style!’ (XPh 49-51) we encounter another central term of the Achaemenid 'legal terminology': the data. This word (literally: ‘the recorded/stipulated’), indeed the entire command of the king, is reminiscent of an Avestan text that means that he who despises Ahura Mazda, the Amesa Spentas (the ‘beneficent immortals’), Mithra, the law (data-), Rasnu and justice, cannot obtain the favour of Ahura Mazda, the Beneficent Immortals, and Mithra (Yt. 10, 139)."[944]
