♠ supernatural enforcement of purity ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ general supernatural enforcement of morality ♣ present ♥ "In one sense, all forms of punishment meted out by the Achaemenid state could be considered as carrying out the will of Ahura Mazda: the law of the king 'concurs with that of the god',[945] and Stausberg has described the Persian kings' 'politicizing of the Avestan concepts' so that the Zoroastrian concept of 'the lie' came to encompass resistance to Achaemenid power.[946] In Darius I's inscription at Behistun, he proclaims that he has punished lawbreakers (in particular, the 'so-called liar-kings' and the rebel magus Gaumata) in accordance with the will of Ahura Mazda. The wrongdoing of Darius' enemies is presented as a transgression not only against the social order, but against the divine order created by Ahura Mazda.[947] Also, "Through the proclamations of Xerxes to his subjects: 'Obey that law (data), which Auramazda has established! Worship Auramazda at the proper time and in the proper ceremonial style!' (XPh 49-51) we encounter another central term of the Achaemenid 'legal terminology': the data. This word (literally: ‘the recorded/stipulated’), indeed the entire command of the king, is reminiscent of an Avestan text that means that he who despises Ahura Mazda, the Amesa Spəntas (the ‘beneficent immortals’), Mithra, the law (data-), Rasnu and justice, cannot obtain the favour of Ahura Mazda, the Beneficent Immortals, and Mirtha (Yt. 10, 139)."[948]
♠ other supernatural enforcement of piety♣ ♥
♠ other supernatural enforcement not related to piety ♣ ♥

Human Enforcement of Morality (CC: Many of the 'form of morality' variables seem to be based on an assumption of continuity with modern Zoroastrians. I have changed them to 'inferred' for this reason)

♠ care/harm ♣ inferred present ♥ The Denkard, a compendium of Zoroastrian beliefs dating to the 10th century AD but considered to incorporate much older material, lists a set of virtues which adherents to the faith should cultivate: 'Powerful and resolute endeavor for the sick, the impoverished, and the needy; granting of legal assistance; care and protection and constant eagerness for bringing support to the poor'.[949] Zoroastrian ethics as interpreted by Darius I himself also exhorted his subjects to do no harm. After invoking the support of Ahura Mazda, his Behistun inscription says 'It is not my desire that a man should do harm; nor indeed is it my desire, if he should do harm, he should not be punished'.[950]
♠ fairness/cheating ♣ inferred present ♥ Zoroastrianism, the religion adhered to by Darius I, his successors and probably also his predecessors, 'emphasized the love of truth'.[951][952] Lincoln comments that 'For a Persian, to lie under any circumstances was regarded as a cardinal sin: corrupt, corrupting, and utterly corrosive of order.' Zoroastrian texts especially denounce 'those who lie when making a compact'.[953] We also read in Skjaervo that in Zoroastrianism, 'One should speak only what is true or real, behaving with rectitude, doing and speaking what is straight, not falseness, and not mislead others, behaving crookedly or deviously.'[954]
♠ in-group loyalty ♣ inferred present ♥ Mary Boyce discusses the strong sense of community among Zoroastrians (she is speaking in a general sense, tracing continuities between ancient and modern Zoroastrianism): 'there is also a striking sense of community, of a bond between all those of "the good religion", united by their common purpose and way of life. This sense of community extends to a remarkable degree to the righteous dead, to those souls "who have overcome for righteousness"'.[955]
♠ respect for authority ♣ present ♥ The 'path of righteousness' was presented by the Zoroastrian Achaemenid kings as entailing 'obedience to the Persian king and Ahuramazda'.[956] Kuhrt also makes an interesting point here about the relationship between the consistent Zoroastrian emphasis on 'truth' and a respect for the status quo: 'It is likely that the Old Persian word encapsulating the concept of correct behavior, and hence acceptance of the imperial order, is "truth" (arta)'.[957] Shaki tells us that 'In the domain of government the Mazdean religion, the supreme spiritual power, and royalty, the temporal power, are twins, for sovereignty is essentially religion and religion sovereignty: Royalty ... is founded on the religion and the religion on royalty, and the exaltation of Iranian royalty ... cannot be separated from submission to the Mazdean religion.'[958]
♠ purity ♣ present ♥ Choksy's 1986 monograph 'Purity and Pollution in Zoroastrianism' states that 'Zoroastrian laws and rituals regulating ritual purity were integrated into the religion and practices of ancient Iranian society and presented as a unified part of the planned and purposeful handiwork of the supreme deity, Ahura Mazda'.[959] 'The Greek historians provide the first evidence for the Persians' concern about pollution of water and fire by "dead matter" and the earth by dead bodies, which features prominently in the Avesta in the Videvdad, which deals with pollution ... The disposal of the dead presented a special challenge, and the kings had themselves buried in rock-cut tombs at Naqsh-e Rostam, except Cyrus the Great, who was buried in a stone monument at Pasargadae ... presumably to minimize the possibility of contaminating the earth'.[960]
♠ kinship ♣ inferred present ♥ Zoroastrian texts from later periods (e.g. the Middle Persian Arda Wiraz-namag), communicate clearly defined roles and moral duties for worshippers in relation to their family members. '"Righteous women" were those who were acquiescent and conforming, showing "reverence and obedience to their husbands", and abstaining from sin (AWN 13.1-11). Those women who abused or defied their husbands or who ignored their maternal duty to their children would condemn their own souls to hell (AWN 26, 59).'[961] Chapter 15 of the Videvdad deals with (among other things) 'a man's obligations to a natural child and its mother'.[962] These texts are considered to incorporate older, oral religious traditions, and it seems likely that Achaemenid-period Zoroastrianism would have also laid down ethical prescriptions for the treatment of family members.
♠ bravery ♣ present ♥ We could consider this to be 'absent' because several sources discuss the development of early, pre-Achaemenid Zoroastrianism as, in part, a reaction against the 'patriarchal and militaristic values' of ancient Iranian 'pastoral-nomadic society'. Zoroaster's 'teaching seems largely aimed at criticizing the central values of his society - bull sacrifice and the consumption of a hallucinogenic beverage known as haoma - as being inappropriately orgiastic, violent, and excessive. Another major theme in Zoroaster's teaching is his opposition to the taking by force of livestock and other wealth by the strong from the weak and the ravaging of pasturelands, which he saw as disruptions of the natural order'.[963] The roots of the religion, then, seem to stress peaceful coexistence rather than valour in battle. On the other hand, Darius the Great's Behistun inscription aims to convey a sense of his military strength, always aided and approved of by Ahura Mazda. For instance, 'By the will of Ahuramazda I defeated the army of Nidintu-Bel exceedingly'.[964] (CC: I have changed this code to 'present' based on description.)
♠ respect for property ♣ present ♥ Zoroaster's teaching was opposed to the 'taking by force of livestock and other wealth by the strong from the weak and the ravaging of pasturelands, which he saw as disruptions of the natural order'.[965]

♠ Moralistic punishment by human religious agents ♣ inferred absent ♥ In one sense, all forms of punishment meted out by the Achaemenid state could be considered as carrying out the will of Ahura Mazda: the law of the king 'concurs with that of the god',[966] and Stausberg has described the Persian kings' 'politicizing of the Avestan concepts' so that the Zoroastrian concept of 'the lie' came to encompass resistance to Achaemenid power.[967] In Darius I's inscription at Behistun, he proclaims that he has punished lawbreakers (in particular, the 'so-called liar-kings' and the rebel magus Gaumata) in accordance with the will of Ahura Mazda. The wrongdoing of Darius' enemies is presented as a transgression not only against the social order, but against the divine order created by Ahura Mazda.[968] However, I have coded 'inferred absent' because as I understand it, this variable is intended to capture punishment by religious agents acting independently of the state and on behalf of supernatural beings. I have not found convincing evidence that the Magi, the priests who officiated in Zoroastrian rituals in the Achaemenid Empire, punished offenders against religious morality. The Vendidad (also written 'Videvdad' or 'Videvdat'), an Avestan text 'dealing with law', contains prescriptions for punishments of those who contravened strict rules of ritual purity.[969] For instance, '(If) a Mazdean should violate the period of a woman who has her menstrual period', he should receive 200 lashes from a horsewhip and 200 lashes from another type of whip called a sraosho.carana.[970] However, the date of its composition is widely disputed, and as Malandra comments, 'if the Vendidad were composed by the Magi either during or after the Achaemenid period, why do they avoid all allusion to the historical centers of political power in western Iran?'[971]

Type of punishment this typically entailed (can be more than one):

♠ physical punishment ♣ inferred absent ♥ In one sense, all forms of punishment meted out by the Achaemenid state could be considered as carrying out the will of Ahura Mazda: the law of the king 'concurs with that of the god',[972] and Stausberg has described the Persian kings' 'politicizing of the Avestan concepts' so that the Zoroastrian concept of 'the lie' came to encompass resistance to Achaemenid power.[973] In Darius I's inscription at Behistun, he proclaims that he has punished lawbreakers (in particular, the 'so-called liar-kings' and the rebel magus Gaumata) in accordance with the will of Ahura Mazda. ♠ loss/seizure of property or wealth ♣ inferred absent ♥ Discussing the Achaemenid judicial system, Magdalene mentions 'criminal forfeiture of defendants' property', but this seem to have been more of a pragmatic measure to compensate victims of crime than a divinely sanctioned punishment.[987] Nevertheless, higher payments were demanded in the case of damage to temple property, perhaps indicating a religious aspect to the penalty. 'In the case of compensation to the victim, we find damage multiples of one, two, three, ten, and thirty. The thirty-fold penalty is clearly used only in the case of theft of, or damage to, temple property'.[988]
♠ loss of status ♣ inferred absent ♥ In one sense, all forms of punishment meted out by the Achaemenid state could be considered as carrying out the will of Ahura Mazda: the law of the king 'concurs with that of the god',[989] and Stausberg has described the Persian kings' 'politicizing of the Avestan concepts' so that the Zoroastrian concept of 'the lie' came to encompass resistance to Achaemenid power.[990] In Darius I's inscription at Behistun, he proclaims that he has punished lawbreakers (in particular, the 'so-called liar-kings' and the rebel magus Gaumata) in accordance with the will of Ahura Mazda. Extent of punishment (can be more than one):

♠ punishment of rulers ♣ inferred absent ♥ In one sense, all forms of punishment meted out by the Achaemenid state could be considered as carrying out the will of Ahura Mazda: the law of the king 'concurs with that of the god',[995] and Stausberg has described the Persian kings' 'politicizing of the Avestan concepts' so that the Zoroastrian concept of 'the lie' came to encompass resistance to Achaemenid power.[996] In Darius I's inscription at Behistun, he proclaims that he has punished lawbreakers (in particular, the 'so-called liar-kings' and the rebel magus Gaumata) in accordance with the will of Ahura Mazda. The wrongdoing of Darius' enemies is presented as a transgression not only against the social order, but against the divine order created by Ahura Mazda.[997] However, I have coded 'inferred absent' because as I understand it, this variable is intended to capture punishment by religious agents acting independently of the state and on behalf of supernatural beings. I have not found convincing evidence that the Magi, the priests who officiated in Zoroastrian rituals in the Achaemenid Empire, punished offenders against religious morality. The Vendidad (also written 'Videvdad' or 'Videvdat'), an Avestan text 'dealing with law', contains prescriptions for punishments of those who contravened strict rules of ritual purity.[998] For instance, '(If) a Mazdean should violate the period of a woman who has her menstrual period', he should receive 200 lashes from a horsewhip and 200 lashes from another type of whip called a sraosho.carana.[999] However, the date of its composition is widely disputed, and as Malandra comments, 'if the Vendidad were composed by the Magi either during or after the Achaemenid period, why do they avoid all allusion to the historical centers of political power in western Iran?'[1,000] Although the rationales given for punishment were certainly religiously inflected (the law of the king 'concurs with that of the god')[1,001] the judicial system and the meting out of punishment was intimately linked to the executive - the king - in this period. As far as I can tell, the priesthood did not punish people independently of state inference, and their power was subject to that of the king.
♠ punishment of elites ♣ inferred absent ♥ In one sense, all forms of punishment meted out by the Achaemenid state could be considered as carrying out the will of Ahura Mazda: the law of the king 'concurs with that of the god',[1,002] and Stausberg has described the Persian kings' 'politicizing of the Avestan concepts' so that the Zoroastrian concept of 'the lie' came to encompass resistance to Achaemenid power.[1,003] In Darius I's inscription at Behistun, he proclaims that he has punished lawbreakers (in particular, the 'so-called liar-kings' and the rebel magus Gaumata) in accordance with the will of Ahura Mazda. The wrongdoing of Darius' enemies is presented as a transgression not only against the social order, but against the divine order created by Ahura Mazda.[1,004] However, I have coded 'inferred absent' because as I understand it, this variable is intended to capture punishment by religious agents acting independently of the state and on behalf of supernatural beings. I have not found convincing evidence that the Magi, the priests who officiated in Zoroastrian rituals in the Achaemenid Empire, punished offenders against religious morality. The Vendidad (also written 'Videvdad' or 'Videvdat'), an Avestan text 'dealing with law', contains prescriptions for punishments of those who contravened strict rules of ritual purity.[1,005] For instance, '(If) a Mazdean should violate the period of a woman who has her menstrual period', he should receive 200 lashes from a horsewhip and 200 lashes from another type of whip called a sraosho.carana.[1,006] However, the date of its composition is widely disputed, and as Malandra comments, 'if the Vendidad were composed by the Magi either during or after the Achaemenid period, why do they avoid all allusion to the historical centers of political power in western Iran?'[1,007]
♠ punishment of commoners ♣ inferred absent ♥ In one sense, all forms of punishment meted out by the Achaemenid state could be considered as carrying out the will of Ahura Mazda: the law of the king 'concurs with that of the god',[1,008] and Stausberg has described the Persian kings' 'politicizing of the Avestan concepts' so that the Zoroastrian concept of 'the lie' came to encompass resistance to Achaemenid power.[1,009] In Darius I's inscription at Behistun, he proclaims that he has punished lawbreakers (in particular, the 'so-called liar-kings' and the rebel magus Gaumata) in accordance with the will of Ahura Mazda. The wrongdoing of Darius' enemies is presented as a transgression not only against the social order, but against the divine order created by Ahura Mazda.[1,010] However, I have coded 'inferred absent' because as I understand it, this variable is intended to capture punishment by religious agents acting independently of the state and on behalf of supernatural beings. I have not found convincing evidence that the Magi, the priests who officiated in Zoroastrian rituals in the Achaemenid Empire, punished offenders against religious morality. The Vendidad (also written 'Videvdad' or 'Videvdat'), an Avestan text 'dealing with law', contains prescriptions for punishments of those who contravened strict rules of ritual purity.[1,011] For instance, '(If) a Mazdean should violate the period of a woman who has her menstrual period', he should receive 200 lashes from a horsewhip and 200 lashes from another type of whip called a sraosho.carana.[1,012] However, the date of its composition is widely disputed, and as Malandra comments, 'if the Vendidad were composed by the Magi either during or after the Achaemenid period, why do they avoid all allusion to the historical centers of political power in western Iran?'[1,013]

Well-Being[edit]

Economic Well-Being[edit]

State Intervention in Economy

♠ The state regulates banking ♣ ♥ "Banking in Iran dates back to 600 BCE ... Private banks, such as those of the Egibi family and the Murashu family, were founded at that time and engaged in mortgages, deposits, and loans. Customers possessed checkbooks and invested in property, farms, slaves, cattle, irrigation projects, fishing, and commercial ships. The Egibi family had the oldest banking institution in Persia, with headquarters in Ecbatana, Pasargadae, Babylonia, Jerusalem, and Sardis at the time of Cyrus the Great. The bank was responsible for collecting taxes and fees for the central government."[1,014]
♠ Price controls ♣ present ♥ According to Richard T. Hallock's interpretation, discussed in Dandamaev and Lukonin (1989), ten of the Persepolis Fortification texts contain evidence that 'civil servants sought in a number of cases to exchange a certain amount of grain, fruit and wine for livestock ... Hallock assumes that the civil servants were attempting to exchange foodstuff surpluses for commodities that were in scarcer supply, but their efforts were, in the majority of cases, doomed to failure, in view of the fact that these officials had to hold strictly to the prices established by the state ... Apparently, the prices set by the state for the sector of the royal economy in Persia and Elam remained unchanged, although, judging by indirect data, they differed markedly from the prices on the free market, where some goods were significantly more expensive'.[1,015]
♠ Regulations against hoarding/stockpiling goods ♣ inferred absent ♥ This seems unlikely given that 'The Persian kings were avid and avaricious hoarders of gold and silver, which they kept (as we know from the hoards Alexander discovered) in palace treasuries at Susa and Persepolis'.[1,016]
♠ Regulations regarding usury ♣ inferred absent ♥ In Late Period Egypt loans could be made to individuals and interest could be charged. "Our documentation contains also a large number of agreements settled by or for women about loans of money or of grain for profit ... In one case a woman took from a man seven artabae of wheat to be paid back increased by 50% within half a year..."[1,017] In Late Period Egypt: "As is commonly known, (money) lending was a particularly brisk business; this activity attracted also female investors, as is illustrated by a serious of separate agreements in our material."[1,018]
♠ Debt-relief measures ♣ ♥
♠ Pensions ♣ inferred absent ♥ I can find no evidence of any system of paying out state pensions either to elites or non-elites in the Achaemenid Empire. The one reference I have found is in Llewellyn-Jones' book, where he says: 'We know that the Egyptian physician Udjahorresnet returned to his native country with the blessing of the Great King (and perhaps a handsome pension) after serving many years at the court of Persia'.[1,019] This is quite tentative and suggests that pensions (if they existed) would have been granted on an ad hoc basis by the king.
♠ Standardization of weights/measures ♣ absent ♥ There was standardization but no single standardization; there were different standardizations in different regions. Dandamaev and Lukonin say that even after Darius' reforms, 'Each province remained an independent socio-economic region with its own social institutions and internal structure; with its old ... systems of weights and measures'.[1,020] Frye also notes that 'The relation of gold to silver was about 13 1/3:1, but it varied as did indeed the weights and measures in various parts of the empire.[1,021]
♠ Guarantee stability of coin value ♣ ♥ Darius minted gold and silver coins. Only the King of Kings could mint gold coins. These weighed about 8.4 grams.[1,022]
♠ Public markets ♣ present ♥ Dandamaev's and Lukonin's discussion of prices for grain and other staples makes a distinction between 'prices set by the state for the sector of the royal economy in Persia and Elam', and 'prices on the free market'.[1,023] Peter Temin used regression analysis to examine price data for six agricultural commodities in Babylon from 464 to 472 bce and concluded that these are 'market prices', moving 'freely in response to changing conditions'.[1,024] 'These agricultural prices moved like the random walk of modern prices, and they varied together in response to weather that affected all crops. These changes are understood clearly within a market framework; they are impossible to understand within an administrative one. I conclude, therefore, that the scribes recorded prices set in functioning markets.'[1,025]

Public Goods[edit]

♠ Famine relief ♣ inferred absent ♥ Kristin Kleber has applied a 'microhistorical approach' to the harvest failure and ensuing famine in Babylonia in 528-26 BCE, during the reign of Cambyses II.[1,026] Her analysis of administrative documents from the archive of the Eanna Temple of Uruk shows the temple struggling to feed its dependent workers and ordering people to guard the barley, with no reference to any public provision of famine relief.[1,027] The presence of Cambyses' travelling entourage in Southern Babylonia exacerbated the problem by using up food.[1,028] Similarly, an article by Berquist suggests a lack of provision of famine relief in the Persian 'colony' (he uses this term rather than 'province') of Yehud. Drawing on the Book of Nehemiah (5:1-12), he comments: 'The passage starts with unsurprising information: some residents of the colony of Yehud were poor. In these poor ones' second statement, they mention that they must acquire debt in order to survive famines. Though this indicates a more specialized and pernicious form of poverty, it would not have been without parallel in Israel's former history. Such debt drove an increasing class differentiation within the society. The next statement, though, pushes the point to a new level. Here the financial difficulty is the king's tax, and the results are most severe'.[1,029] So here we have a population who are not only without famine relief, but who must shoulder the additional burden of taxes to the Achaemenid king, going into debt for both reasons.

♠ provision of famine relief ♣ ♥

♠ Food storage ♣ present ♥ The Persepolis Fortification Tablets (PF) 'deal with the collection, warehousing, and distribution of foodstuffs' by the 'central authorities' and by officials outside Persepolis (who had to report to higher-ranking bureaucrats in the capital).[1,030]

♠ provision of food storage ♣ mixed ♥ The Achaemenid state maintained food storage facilities, at least along the 'Persepolis-Susa axis' - it is unclear how far outside this central zone the imperial administration of food distribution extended.[1,031] The Persepolis Fortification Tablets (PF) 'deal with the collection, warehousing, and distribution of foodstuffs' by the 'central authorities' and by officials outside Persepolis (who were under the supervision of higher-ranking bureaucrats in the capital).[1,032] Discussing the evidence from PF 144-58 of food storage in warehouses attached to 'paradises', Briant infers that 'paradise administration functioned as just another branch of government, at least under certain conditions' ('paradise' here refers to parcels of agriculturally productive land, e.g. orchards).[1,033] Rations were disbursed to a variety of workers and travellers from these storage facilities. I have coded 'mixed' because there is also evidence of food storage by religious agents: in Achaemenid-period Babylonia, the Eanna Temple had 'storehouses' for food and and distributed rations to support its various dependants.[1,034] The separation between state and religious food storage was not clear cut: Kozuh assumes 'that over time the Achaemenids attempted to integrate the temples into the administrative fabric of the royal and satrapal administrations'.[1,035]

♠ Alimentary supplementation ♣ present ♥ Food rations were used as a method of payment of workers, distributed from warehouses controlled by the state administration. For instance, we have records of the 'transport to Persepolis of grain, flour, and wine intended for craftsmen's rations'.[1,036] 'Women about to give birth were among the recipients of special rations (PF 1200-37) ... The ration is doubled in the event of the birth of a boy ... Several tablets show that each mother received both wine/beer and grain products'.[1,037] Briant comments that 'this documentation testifies to a policy of voluntary supplements on the part of the administration'.[1,038]

♠ provision of alimentary supplementation ♣ mixed ♥ I have explained the Achaemenid state's role in providing alimentary supplementation above, but there are also indications that temples may sometimes have played a similar role. Although the Eanna Temple at Uruk clearly had no stored food specifically for the purposes of famine relief (see 'Famine relief' variable), we have a letter from the 'bel piqitti' (probably some kind of clerk cooperating with the temple administration)[1,039] to the chief administrator of Eanna at the time of a famine in Babylonia: 'Regarding what I wrote you about the dates: I have not written that I wanted to distribute the dates as rations, but: "I want to give them [the workers] one litre of dates each as summutu and they shall do the work"'.[1,040] Kleber comments that 'Summutu must designate a ration not identical with the normal monthly payment. The quantity is low, which suggests that the dates were given as a meal to satisfy hunger in order to prevent the workers from escaping, or even from dying.'[1,041]

♠ Drinking water ♣ present ♥ The 'Jewish priest and scientist' Esdras wrote that Susa (one of the Achaemenid capitals) had sewers and that 'water is taken to upper floors of buildings by conduits'.[1,042] Qanats, as well as serving to irrigate crops, provided drinking water, and Darius I's capital city of Persepolis was 'watered by qanats'.[1,043] Mahmoudian and Mahmoudian claim that 'Boiling water and keeping it in silver vessels were two treatment methods practised during the Achaemenid period'.[1,044]

♠ provision of drinking water ♣ mixed ♥ The Achaemenid state utilized slave labour to construct qanats: 'slaves and captives were trained to construct qanats under the Achaemenid and Sassanian kings. Maintenance and repairs were accomplished by corvee (forced labor)'.[1,045] However, it also encouraged private construction: 'The Achaemenid rulers provided a major incentive for qanat construction by allowing qanat builders and their heirs to retain profits from newly-built qanats for five generations.'[1,046]

♠ Irrigation works ♣ present ♥ "From the earliest times, the flow of water was controlled for agricultural purposes by an elaborate system of canals, sluices, dams, embankments, and dikes."[1,047] Qanat technology - 'subterranean irrigation canals'.[1,048] 'Under the Achaemenids (550-331 BC), when Persian rule extended from the Indus to the Nile, qanat technology spread well beyond the confines of the Iranian Plateau.'[1,049]

♠ provision of irrigation works ♣ mixed ♥ One of the Achaemenid Empire's major achievements under Darius I was 'the improvement of irrigation, which was of vital importance for the increase of productive capacity in Egypt, Babylonia, Iran, and Central Asia, wherever water was short. Success in the unceasing struggle against the depletion of cultivable land was possible only by digging subterranean irrigation canals (NPers. qanat), which still today supply much of Iran and Afghanistan's water in agriculture. For the most part they were in possession of the king, who leased them at a high water rate, but who rewarded the building of a new canal also by conceding usufruct of the thus cultivated land to the kin in question for five generations (Polybius 10.28.3).'[1,050] Also, as noted above in Phase I variables, Darius I was called a Pharaoh by the Egyptians 'since by digging qanats and other initiatives he had supplied the south of Egypt with irrigation water.'[1,051] However, I have coded 'mixed' instead of 'state' because Dandamayev tells us that 'Some irrigation canals also belonged to the temples and private individuals'.[1,052]

♠ Health infrastructure ♣ absent ♥ Udjahorresnet, an Egyptian in the entourage of Darius I, 'restored an institution for teaching medicine (House of Life)' in the Nile Valley, 'providing the students with all they needed to pursue their studies, "as it had been before"'.[1,053] Udjahorresnet acknowledged the king's support in an inscription: 'His Majesty did this because he knew the usefulness of this art for reviving every sick person'.[1,054] We also have evidence of Greek and Egyptian physicians serving in the Achaemenid court.[1,055] However, with the exception of the Egyptian medical schools (a continuation of pre-existing Egyptian institutions), I have found no evidence of anything we could call a 'health infrastructure' proper (i.e. hospitals). Although there was a 'highly organized native system of medicine' in Achaemenid times according to Elgood, doctors apparently took the form of itinerant priests with medical expertise:[1,056] 'the Avesta always speaks of "wandering priest-physicians", never of physicians with a fixed abode'.[1,057]

♠ Control of infectious disease ♣ present ♥ Interestingly, Elgood highlights the practical health benefits of the strict Zoroastrian ritual purity laws. 'It is in the realm of public health that Zoroastrian medicine reached its highest level. The laws which governed ceremonial purity are so blended with those that aim at preventing the spread of disease that it is impossible to separate them'.[1,058] On the subject of rabies, which 'must have been one of the earliest diseases which forced itself upon the attention of mankind', he infers from the Avestan texts that 'Zoroaster fully realized the danger of infection. "If there be a mad dog in the house of a worshipper of Ahura Mazda or one that bites without barking, what shall the worshippers of Mazda do? Ahura Mazda answered: They shall put a wooden collar around his neck and they shall tie him to a post ... If they shall not do so and the mad dog smite a sheep or wound a man, the dog shall pay for it as for wilful murder."'[1,059] More generally, Zoroastrian rules surrounding the handling of corpses, avoidance of contamination of water and earth, and the cleanliness of the worshipper's home must have contributed to the control of infectious disease.[1,060]
♠ Care of war invalids/veterans ♣ inferred absent ♥ Military service was apparently compulsory. 'After service in what we may call the "Persian National Army", Strabo (15.3.19) tells us that the young men were demobilized, but remained liable for military service until their fiftieth year'.[1,061] Kuhrt mentions the evidence in Babylonian texts for 'the organization of modest land parcels granted to individuals in return for military service; some, but not all, were attached to large estates':[1,062] presumably at least some of the soldiers injured in battle could count on these productive agricultural lands to sustain them. Frye also says that 'Soldiers and officers were rewarded for bravery or service with special titles or objects of rank, orders such as golden laurel leaves, robes of honour, special daggers of honour, bracelets, or similar things'.[1,063] However, I have coded 'inferred absent' here as I have found no indication in any of the sources of funds set aside specifically for the care of injured veterans.
♠ Care of orphans ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no evidence of this in the empire as a whole. However, there are Achaemenid-period Babylonian adoption contracts for 'foundlings (i.e., "ownerless" children)'.[1,064] There may also have been some minor religiously motivated provision for the care of orphaned children in areas where Jewish law was applied: it is written in Deuteronomy, which incorporates laws from the time of exile in Babylon (i.e. predating Achaemenid rule),[1,065] that 'When you reap your harvest in your field and forget a sheaf in the field, you shall not go back to get it; it shall be left for the alien, the orphan, and the widow, so that the Lord your God may bless you in your undertakings. When you beat your olive trees, do not strip what is left; it shall be for the alien, the orphan, and the widow'.[1,066]
♠ Care of elderly ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no evidence of the care of the elderly on a larger scale than within individual families. It seems likely that this duty was taken on by one's children, or in the case of wealthier families, by slaves. Baker describes a Neo-Babylonian document stipulating the conditions of manumission of a particular slave: 'the elderly Iqisa son of Kudurru, of the Nur-Sin family ... freed a slave of his on condition that the slave provide him with food rations and clothing; however, the slave absconded, the manumission was revoked and the slave given to Iqisa's daughter-in-law, who undertook to look after the old man'.[1,067]

♠ State pensions for elites ♣ inferred absent ♥ I can find no evidence of any system of paying out state pensions either to elites or non-elites in the Achaemenid Empire. The one reference I have found is in Llewellyn-Jones' book, where he says: 'We know that the Egyptian physician Udjahorresnet returned to his native country with the blessing of the Great King (and perhaps a handsome pension) after serving many years at the court of Persia'.[1,068] This is quite tentative and suggests that pensions (if they existed) would have been granted on an ad hoc basis by the king. I suspect that on a more general level, elites were sustained in their later years by revenue from the estates allotted to them by the king (e.g. the hatru system in Babylonia, or the concessions of royal land in Egypt).[1,069]

♠ State pensions for non-elites ♣ inferred absent ♥ I can find no evidence of any system of paying out state pensions either to elites or non-elites in the Achaemenid Empire. The one reference I have found is in Llewellyn-Jones' book, where he says: 'We know that the Egyptian physician Udjahorresnet returned to his native country with the blessing of the Great King (and perhaps a handsome pension) after serving many years at the court of Persia'.[1,070] This is quite tentative and suggests that pensions (if they existed) would have been granted on an ad hoc basis by the king.

Prosocial Behavior[edit]

♠ Volunteering ♣ inferred present ♥ Briant says 'we know that ancient armies - including the Achaemenid armies - did not have, properly speaking, a quartermaster corps. Most often, the soldiers lived off the land, either by pillage or by purchase from the locals. This is how Cyrus the Younger's mercenaries survived; the only preparation he undertook was to plan for flour and wine wagons in case the Greeks were unable to find supplies on the spot'.[1,071] It seems, then, that many soldiers would have volunteered for fight in the Persian army (or rival forces like that of Cyrus the Younger) without a guarantee of pay or even food rations. However, for its building projects, the Achaemenid state instead used slave and corvee labour (e.g. for qanats).[1,072]

♠ Charity ♣ inferred absent ♥ Cantera says that 'from its very beginnings Zoroastrianism has developed an ethical imperative of assistance to the needy members of the community'.[1,073] However, I have been unable to find direct evidence of 'frequent' or 'large-scale' donations made for charitable purposes.

Voluntary Associations

♠ voluntary trade associations ♣ inferred present ♥ Dandamaev and Lukonin briefly describe the merchants' associations present in 5th-century bce Nippur: 'It can be seen from the documents of the archives of Murashu that in the fifth century in the Nippur district there existed special "associations of merchants," which were allotted fields from the state land fund and which were in the royal service ... These associations were headed by "the chiefs of the merchants," who were responsible for the payment of royal taxes'.[1,074] Stolper also mentions 'smallholders' associations' in Nippur.[1,075] However, it is unclear how 'voluntary' these associations were, and they clearly had a close relationship with the state administration. Likewise, Weisberg (in Guild Structure and Political Allegiance in Early Achaemenid Mesopotamia) has attempted to demonstrate the presence of guilds in this period, but it is not clear how 'voluntary' these were: Wright notes in a review that he uncovered a 'high but not exclusive association between craft and kin group'.[1,076]
♠ prestige of voluntary trade associations ♣ ♥
♠ voluntary community organizations ♣ ♥

Taxation[edit]

♠ Degree of taxation ♣ 14,560: 519 bce ♥ Euboic talents of silver. According to Herodotus (3.89-97), the annual tax payments collected by Darius I from all his provinces amounted to 14,560 (Euboic) talents.[1,080] One Euboic talent weighed 25.86kg,[1,081] so if we believe Herodotus, this was about 377,000 kg of silver in total. In addition, the king received 'traditional "gifts" that included 1,000 talents of frankincense from Arabia, 360 white horses from Cappadocia, and 500 Babylonian boys destined to become eunuchs, plus unspecified amounts of ebony and ivory from Ethiopia'. Persia itself was 'exempt from tribute' in money, but not from 'taxes in kind'.[1,082][1,083] The use of the term 'tribute' here is slightly ambiguous, but I am using the code book's distinction of tax (paid by subject peoples) from tribute (paid by groups not under formal Persian control). Darius' new fixed taxation system was established in 519 bce.[1,084] Before this time, under Cyrus the Great and Cambyses II, there was 'no firmly regulated system of taxes'.[1,085]

Taxes are imposed on:

♠ Land ♣ present ♥ 'Taxes were many and varied ... Land, of course, was measured, registered and taxed extensively'.[1,086]

Wages and Costs[edit]

♠ Wages ♣ 12 ♥ silver shekels a year.[1,087] Dandamaev and Lukonin say that this was the 'average annual pay of an adult hired worker regardless of whether he was free or a slave', and that 'for one shekel it was possible to purchase 180 liters of barley or dates'.[1,088] From documents from the Eanna Temple archive, we learn that corvee labourers (temple dependants) working on royal building projects in southern Babylonia at the end of Cyrus the Great's and beginning of Cambyses' reign (around 530-527 bce) received 90 litres of barley per person per month (1080 litres per year).[1,089] Kleber says that this was the 'standard ration' at this time.[1,090]

♠ staple cost of principal source of carbohydrates ♣ 0.3: 492-458 bce ♥ shekels for one bar (100 litres) of barley.[1,091] 'It is known from the Treasury documents that during the period from 492 to 458 B.C., prices for sheep and wine remained unchanged (a sheep cost 3 shekels of silver, while a jug of wine with a volume of 9.7 litres cost 1 shekel). If these rates already prevailed in the earlier period to which the Fortification tablets are dated, then, with the ratio of sheep and grain at 1:10, one bar of barley cost 0.3 shekel ... Hallock notes that in the period from December to August of the year 466 B.C., the cost of one bar of barley fluctuated from 0.66 to 2.5 shekels, instead of the normal price of 0.3 shekel, from which it is possible to conclude that there was a great shortage of grain at that time'.[1,092] 'Grain in Iran was much more expensive than in Babylonia'.[1,093] 'After the capture of Babylonia by Cyrus, prices on food products and various commodities at first remained primarily at the former level, but gradually rose by the end of the Achaemenid period to approximately one and a half times what they had been'.[1,094]

♠ Top fortune ♣ ♥

♠ Other wealth distribution ♣ ♥ Describing the sack of Persepolis by Alexander the Great's forces, Diodorus Siculus claimed that it was 'the richest city under the sun and the private houses had been furnished with every sort of wealth over the years. The Macedonians raced into it slaughtering all the men whom they met and plundering the residences; many of the houses belonged to the common people and were abundantly supplied with furniture and wearing apparel of every kind. Here much silver was carried off and no little gold, and many rich dresses gay with sea purple or with gold embroidery became the prize of the victors.'[1,095] It is noteworthy than the houses of the 'common people' were also said to be full of riches, but as Persepolis was an immensely wealthy centre of the Achaemenid Empire, it would seem unwise to extend this to other cities and less central satrapies. Briant, discussing the distribution of war spoils among the 'king's men', says that there is 'no doubt that the royal favorites got their hands on riches - such as Bagoas, an intimate of Artaxerxes III, who grabbed a quantity of gold and silver taken from the Egyptian temples and then sold back to the priests at an exor­bitant price the "ancient sacred annals" that he had just stolen from them'.[1,096]

HPI Variables[edit]

♠ Dedicated police force ♣ inferred present ♥ Army and local military garrisons seem to have been used for law and order but Iranian words appear to suggest the presence of a special word for police and the hierarchy of intelligence officers/agents might also count as policemen. Tipati - word with proto-Iranian root that likely means ‘supervisor, chief of the guards’. Context of the word is thought to be "a title of a police official", other translations include 'guarding the thieves' and 'police chief’.[1,097] 'The use of Persian loanwords in the Egyptian texts implies a completely Persian judicial system in the Egyptian satrapy, with judges, police, and intelligence officers all Iranian. The same would have been true throughout the empire, including the satrapy of Beyond the River and the province of Yehud.'[1,098] Other scholars say conquered people ruled more according to local traditions.[1,099] Persians and their local rulers used the army to apprehend "criminal gangs that raided villages and towns and then fled", and "military garrisons stationed in strategic locations of a sophisticated and well-maintained highway network" for some of its policing. The commander of a military garrison could imprison accused thieves before their trial. "There were also local militia groups who helped the army and local garrisons in performing their order-imposing duties throughout the realm.” The king also used intelligence agents to spy on local authorities, such as the law and order imposing garrison commander.[1,100]
♠ Police levels ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Proscriptions of elites and or property ♣ ♥ Use of the state to repress or purge rival elite groups.
♠ Corporal punishment by the state ♣ ♥


♠ Military control over independent or semi-independent vassal states ♣ present ♥ Loose federation with irregular taxation under Cyrus and Cambyses with automomous satrapies. Relied on non-Persian officials and existing institutions. After Darius put down rebellions he created the 'world empire’ but under this system regions were still autonomous, the improvement was fixed system of tax collection based on surveys and an inspection regime.[1,101] "Prior to the Parthians, political systems in Southwest Asia were for the most part relatively loose confederations in which central government ruled their 'empires' through unstable alliances with vassals and satraps. Even Hammurabi, Darius, and Alexander were only temporarily successful in linking their centralized governments to local administrative institutions, particularly outside of the core areas of Greater Mesopotamia.”[1,102]
